Liability for Usurped Land: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for usurped land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability for usurped land and distinguish between the opinions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Land usurpation does not trigger liability upon destruction — ghasb requires physical transfer, impossible for immovable property.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does a usurper guarantee land if it perishes in his hand? The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states no liability, following Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's later opinion. However, Muhammad, Zufar, and even al-Shafi'i argue for liability. The commentary unpacks the legal reasoning, including a hadith and the distinction between literal and metaphorical ghasb. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how Hanafi imams balance textual evidence with qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its proponents
Identify that the matn states no liability for usurped land that perishes. Note that this is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's later opinion. Recognize that this is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).
2
Identify the opposing Hanafi opinions
List the opponents: Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, and Abu Yusuf's first opinion. These hold that the usurper does guarantee the land. Note that al-Shafi'i agrees with this view.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the opponent's proof (dalil)
The opponents argue that ghasb is realized by establishing an aggressive hand and removing the legitimate hand, which can occur in land. They also cite the hadith: 'Whoever usurps a span of land...' and analogize to cases of deposit (wadi'a) where liability arises.
The hadith is a threat for sin, not necessarily for liability.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response asserts that ghasb requires physical transport (naql) of the property, which is impossible for land. The usurper's act of expelling the owner is a disposition on the owner, not on the land itself. The term 'ghasb' applied to land is metaphorical, not literal, similar to 'sale of a free person'.
Thus, no liability for mere possession.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical consequence: if land perishes without the usurper's action, no compensation is due per the relied-upon view. However, if the usurper actively causes damage (e.g., by building and destroying), he is liable by consensus. An exception is waqf land where fatwa is given with Muhammad's opinion.
Al-Bazzaziyya confirms the correct opinion is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (فإن غصب عقاراً وهلك في يده لم يضمنه) وهذا عند الإمام وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد وزفر والشافعي : يضمنه وهو قول أبي يوسف أولاً. وفي العيني : ويفتى بقول محمد في عقار الوقف. ولأن الغصب يتحقق بوصفين بإثبات اليد العادية وإزالة اليد المحقة وذلك يمكنه في العقار لأن إثبات اليدين المتدافعتين على شيء واحد لا يمكن لتعذر اجتماعهما فيه فإذا ثبت اليد العادية للغاصب انتفت اليد المحقة للمالك ضرورة ولهذا يضمن العقار المودع بالجحود والإقرار به لغير المالك وبالرجوع عن الشهادة بعد القضاء ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام من غصب شبراً من أرض طوقه الله أرضين»(1). ولنا القيامة من سبع يوم أن الغاصب تصرف في المغصوب بإثبات يده وإزالة يدا المالك ولا يكون ذلك إلا بالنقل والعقار لا يمكن نقله وأقصى ما يكون فيه إخراج المالك منه وذلك تصرف في المالك لا في العقار فلا الضمان، ومسائل الوديعة على الخلاف على الأصح فلا يلزمه ولئن سلم يوجب
(۱) رواه مسلم في كتاب المساقاة حدیث ۱۳۷.
بسكناه وزراعته ضمن النقصان كما في النقلي وإن استغله تصدق بالغلة كما لو تصرف موجب عجز أحد فالضمان فيما ذكر بترك الحفظ الملتزم. وإطلاق لفظ الغصب عليه لا يدل على تحقق غصب للضمان كإطلاق لفظ البيع على بيع الحر لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «من باع حراً» الحديث. لا يدل على تحقق بيع الحر وهذا لما عرف أن في لسان الشرع حقيقة ومجازاً. وفي هذا سؤال تقديره كيف جمع بين لفظ غصب وعدم الضمان مع أن الغصب للضمان؟ موجب وعلى هذا الخلاف لو باع العقار بعد الغصب وأقر بذلك وكذبه المشتري لا يقبل إقراره في حق المشتري لأن ملكه ظاهر ولا يضمن البائع عندهما لأنه لم يتلفه وإنما إتلافه مضاف إلى المالك عن إقامة البينة. وفي الكافي : ولو غصب عقاراً وهلك في يده بأن غلب السيل عليه فهلك تحت الماء أو غصب داراً فهدمت بآفة سماوية أو سيل فذهب بالبناء لم يضمن عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد والشافعي وزفر وهو قول أبي يوسف أولاً: يضمن. وفي البزازية : والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وفي الينابيع : فإن حدثت هذه الأشياء بفعل من الناس فضمانه على المتلف عندهما وعند محمد هو مخير بين ضمان الغاصب والمتلف، فإن ضمن الغاصب يرجع على المتلف وإن حدثت هذه الأشياء بفعل الغاصب وسكناه فالضمان عليه بالإجماع. وفي الكافي : وعلى هذا أي على غصب العقار لا ينعقد موجباً للضمان إذا باع دار الرجل وأدخلها المشتري في بنائه لم يضمن البائع عند أبي حنيفة وهو قول أبي يوسف آخراً، وعند محمد يضمن قيمتها، ومعنى المسألة إذا باعها واعترف بالغصب وكذبه المشتري ؛ كذا ذكره فخر الإسلام.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'For if he usurped land and it perished in his hand, he does not guarantee it.' And this is according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i said: he does guarantee it, and it was Abu Yusuf's first opinion. In al-'Ayni: and fatwa is given according to Muhammad's opinion regarding waqf land. And because usurpation is realized by two attributes: establishing the aggressive hand and removing the legitimate hand, and that is possible in land because establishing two opposing hands on one thing is impossible due to the impossibility of their coexistence. So when the aggressive hand of the usurper is established, the legitimate hand of the owner is necessarily negated. For this reason, the depositary of land is liable by denial and by admitting for non-owner and by retracting testimony after judgment, and due to his statement (peace and blessings be upon him): 'Whoever usurps a span of land, Allah will yoke him with seven lands on the Day of Resurrection.' And we have the resurrection from seven days [sic] that the usurper acted in the usurped property by establishing his hand and removing the owner's hand, and that is only by transport, and land cannot be transported, and the utmost in it is expelling the owner from it, and that is an act on the owner, not on the land, so no liability. And the cases of deposit are based on disagreement according to the most correct view, so it does not necessitate, and if conceded, it necessitates. [Line: occupied by dwelling and cultivation, he guarantees the decrease as in movables, and if he uses it, he gives the produce in charity, as if he acted... the liability for what is mentioned is due to abandoning the required preservation. And applying the term 'usurpation' to it does not indicate the occurrence of usurpation for liability, like applying the term 'sale' to the sale of a free person, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever sells a free person...' the hadith. It does not indicate the occurrence of a sale of a free person. This is because it is known that in the language of the Sharia there is reality and metaphor. And in this there is a question: how can one combine the word 'usurpation' and the absence of liability when usurpation necessitates liability? And upon this disagreement, if he sold the land after usurpation and admitted to that and the buyer denied, his admission is not accepted with regard to the buyer because his ownership is apparent, and the seller does not guarantee according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) because he did not destroy it, but rather its destruction is attributed to the owner due to the inability to establish evidence. In al-Kafi: And if he usurped land and it perished in his hand because a flood overwhelmed it and it perished under water, or he usurped a house and it collapsed due to a divine calamity or a flood and the building was carried away, he does not guarantee according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad, al-Shafi'i, and Zufar said: he guarantees, and it was Abu Yusuf's first opinion. In al-Bazzaziyya: and the correct is the opinion of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. In al-Yanabi': if these things occur due to human action, then liability is on the destroyer according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and according to Muhammad, he is given the choice between holding the usurper or the destroyer liable. If he holds the usurper liable, he can recourse to the destroyer. And if these things occur due to the usurper's action and his occupation, liability is upon him by consensus. In al-Kafi: And upon this, meaning, concerning usurpation of land, it does not arise as a cause for liability. If he sold a man's house and the buyer incorporated it into his building, the seller does not guarantee according to Abu Hanifa and it is the latter opinion of Abu Yusuf; according to Muhammad, he guarantees its value. The meaning of the issue is when he sold it and confessed to usurpation and the buyer denied him; thus mentioned by Fakhr al-Islam.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the usurper of land ('aqar) is liable if the land perishes in his hand. The matn states no liability, attributed to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (later opinion). Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i hold the opposite. The commentator presents arguments: the opposing view claims that usurpation (ghasb) requires both establishing the usurper's hand and removing the owner's hand, which can occur in land because the two hands cannot coexist. They also cite the hadith: 'Whoever usurps a span of land...' The Hanafi response argues that ghasb necessitates physical transport (naql), impossible in land. The utmost is expelling the owner, which is a disposition on the owner, not the property, thus no liability. The text then clarifies that the term 'ghasb' applied to land is metaphorical, not literal, similar to 'sale of a free person.' Various scenarios are discussed: natural calamities (flood, collapse) – no liability according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad and others hold liability. If the usurper actively destroys, he is liable by consensus. The fatwa according to al-Bazzaziyya is with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, except for waqf land where fatwa is with Muhammad. The passage also covers the case of selling the usurped land and admission of usurpation.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; the obligation to compensate for loss or destruction.
عقار ('aqar): Immovable property, land or buildings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling if someone usurps land and it perishes in his hand due to a natural calamity?
A) He must guarantee the value
B) He does not guarantee
C) He guarantees only if he occupied it
D) He is given a choice

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, usurping land does not entail liability for destruction unless the usurper actively causes damage, because land cannot be physically transferred.

Compensation for Decrease from Usurper's Occupation
Ruling with proof that usurper compensates for decrease from occupation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the usurper guarantees the decrease from dwelling and cultivation, and explain why it is based on direct destruction (itlaf) rather than possession.

Core Idea
The usurper compensates for decrease not because he holds the land, but because he actively caused damage.

In Hanafi fiqh, usurping land does not normally trigger liability for the land itself due to it being immovable. However, this passage establishes a clear exception: the usurper must compensate for any decrease (nuqsan) caused by his active use—dwelling or cultivation—because this constitutes direct destruction (itlaf), which incurs liability regardless of possession. The rule is supported by consensus (ijma) and analogy to movables.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states clearly: 'And whatever decreases by his dwelling and cultivation, he guarantees the decrease, as in movables.' This is the central ruling.
2
Note the Authority
The commentator asserts this ruling is by consensus (ijma), making it undisputed within the madhhab.
Consensus strengthens the obligation to wajib level.
3
Understand the Distinction
The key distinction is between liability for destruction (damān al-itlāf) and liability for usurpation (damān al-ghasb). For itlaf, possession is not required; direct causation suffices.
Land is normally not guaranteed in ghasb, but it is guaranteed by itlaf.
4
Apply to the Scenario
Dwelling or cultivation is considered an act of destruction (itlaf) because it causes decrease, even if the land is not physically moved.
Examples include building collapse due to blacksmith work, or moving soil.
5
Extend to Analogous Cases
The same principle applies to riding another's animal without permission if it dies from the ride, or sitting on another's rug.
If no destruction, no liability (e.g., riding without causing death).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وما نقص بسكناه وزراعته ضمن النقصان كما في النقلي) وهذا بالإجماع قال القدوري كما إذا انهدمت أو ضعف البناء كما لو عمل فيها حداد فانهدمت أو ضعف البناء والفرق لهما أنه أتلفه بفعله كما لو نقل ترابه والعقار يضمن بالإتلاف ولا يشترط لضمان الإتلاف أن يكون في يده ألا الحر يضمن به بخلاف ضمان الغصب حيث لا يضمن إلا بالحصول في اليد فعلى هذا لو ركب دابة الغير بغير إذنه ولم يسيرها حتى نزل ثم هلكت لم يضمن لعدم النقل، وإن تلفت بركوبه يضمن لوجود الإتلاف بفعله وهو نظير ما لو قعد على بساط الغير بغير إذنه. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : غصب أرضاً وزرعها وبنت فلصاحبها أن يأخذ الأرض ويأمر الغاصب بقلع الزرع تفريغاً لملكه، فإن أبى أن يفعل فللمغصوب منه أن يفعل. وفي الذخيرة: وإن لم يحضر المالك حتى أدرك الزرع فالزرع للغاصب وللمالك أن يرجع على الغاصب بنقصان الأرض بسبب الزراعة، وإن حضر المالك والزرع لم ينبت فإن شاء صاحب الأرض يتركها حتى ينبت الزرع ثم يأمر بقلع الزرع ، وإن شاء أعطاه قيمة بذره لكن مبذوراً في أرض غيره وهو أن تقوم الأرض مبذورة وغير مبذورة فيضمن فضل ما بينهما والبذر له. وفي العيون : غصب من آخر أرضاً وزرعها حنطة ثم اختصما وهي بذر لم تنبت بعد فصاحب الأرض تري أن
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And whatever decreases by his dwelling and cultivation, he guarantees the decrease, as in movables.' And this is by consensus. Al-Quduri said: as when it collapses or the building weakens, as if he worked as a blacksmith in it and it collapses or the building weakens. And the distinction for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that he destroyed it by his act, as if he moved its soil. And land is liable through destruction, and it is not required for liability of destruction that it be in his hand; otherwise, a free person is liable through it, unlike the liability of usurpation where he is not liable except by being in his hand. Based on this: if he rode another's animal without permission and did not make it go until he dismounted, then it died, he does not guarantee due to lack of transport. But if it died due to his riding, he guarantees due to existence of destruction by his act, and this is analogous to if he sat on another's rug without permission. In Fatawa Abi al-Layth: He usurped land, cultivated it, and built; the owner may take the land and order the usurper to uproot the crop to vacate his property. If he refuses, the owner may do so. In al-Dhakhira: If the owner does not come until the crop ripens, the crop belongs to the usurper, and the owner may recourse against the usurper for the decrease in the land due to cultivation. If the owner comes and the crop has not sprouted, then if the owner wishes, he can leave it until the crop sprouts and then order uprooting, and if he wishes, he may give him the value of his seed but sown in another's land, which is that the land is valued when sown and unsown, and he guarantees the difference, and the seed belongs to him. In al-'Uyun: He usurped land from another and planted it with wheat, then they disputed while it was seed that had not sprouted, so the owner... (cut off).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof that the usurper of land must compensate for any decrease (nuqsan) caused by his dwelling (sukna) or cultivation (zira'ah). The commentator states this is by consensus (ijma'). The proof is that the usurper caused destruction by his own act (itlaf bi fi'lihi), and land is liable for damage if one actively destroys it, regardless of whether it is in his hand (unlike the liability of usurpation itself, which requires physical possession). Examples: if the building collapses due to his activity as a blacksmith, or he moves soil. The distinction is drawn between liability for destruction (daman al-itlaf) and liability for usurpation (daman al-ghasb). For itlaf, it suffices that he directly caused the damage, even without being in possession. The commentator then applies this to analogous cases: riding another's animal without permission—if he merely rides without making it move, no liability; if it dies due to his riding, he guarantees. Sitting on another's rug is similar. The passage then provides practical details from Fatawa Abi al-Layth and al-Dhakhira about crops planted by the usurper. The owner may order removal, and if the crop ripens, it belongs to the usurper but the owner claims damages. If the seed has not sprouted, the owner can either wait or pay the value of the seed as sown in his land, compensating for the difference in land value.

Key Terms
نقصان (nuqsan): The decrease in value or physical deterioration of the usurped land due to the usurper's actions like dwelling or cultivation.
إتلاف (itlaf): Destruction; causing harm to property through direct action, which triggers liability regardless of possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the usurper guarantee the decrease from dwelling and cultivation despite the general rule that land is not guaranteed in usurpation?
A) Because of a special hadith
B) Because he destroyed it by his act (itlaf)
C) Because the land is now in his possession
D) Because of istihsan

Key Takeaway
The usurper compensates for decrease caused by his occupation because he directly destroyed the land's value, and this liability is established by consensus.

Usurper's Liability for Decrease in Real Estate
Explaining when a usurper of real estate is liable for decrease and the legal nature of the liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on usurper's liability for decrease in real estate and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
A usurper of real estate is not liable for natural destruction, only for his own harmful acts.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, usurpation (ghasb) of movable property creates strict liability, but for real estate (ʿaqār) the rule differs. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq explores when a usurper is liable for decrease in value. The author clarifies that liability arises from actual use causing harm, and the term 'ghasb' is applied figuratively to real estate. A key intra-madhhab dispute arises if the property is destroyed by a natural disaster: the relied-upon view holds no liability, while Muhammad al-Shaybani and others hold the usurper liable. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Identify the specific act (usurpation of real estate) and the ruling on liability for decrease. Note that liability is not automatic but conditional on actual harmful use.
The passage distinguishes between dwelling/cultivation causing decrease (liable) and natural destruction (not liable in the relied-upon view).
2
Locate the Dalil
The author uses an analogy: the figurative use of 'ghasb' for real estate is like calling the sale of a free person a 'sale'—it is metaphorical, not real. This is supported by a hadith: 'Whoever sells a free person...'
The key point is that the legal reality of ghasb (which entails liability) does not fully apply to real estate.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves that linguistic usage does not determine legal reality. The phrase 'sale of a free person' is not a real sale because a free person cannot be sold. Similarly, 'ghasb' of real estate is not real ghasb causing liability; it is a loose usage.
This demonstrates a usul principle: the language of the Law includes both literal and figurative meanings.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Identify the disputed scenario: natural destruction (e.g., flood, earthquake). Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf say no liability; Muhammad, al-Shafiʿi, Zufar, and earlier Abu Yusuf say liable. The passage cites multiple sources: al-Kafi, al-Bazzaziyya, al-Yanabiʿ.
The ikhtilaf is both intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (later): no liability for natural destruction. This is declared 'sahih' in al-Bazzaziyya. Also, if a third party causes destruction, the usurper is not liable; the third party is liable.
Muhammad's view is recognized but not mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسكناه وزراعته ضمن النقصان كما في النقلي وإن استغله تصدق بالغلة كما لو تصرف موجب عجز أحد فالضمان فيما ذكر بترك الحفظ الملتزم. وإطلاق لفظ الغصب عليه لا يدل على تحقق غصب للضمان كإطلاق لفظ البيع على بيع الحر لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «من باع حراً» الحديث. لا يدل على تحقق بيع الحر وهذا لما عرف أن في لسان الشرع حقيقة ومجازاً. وفي هذا سؤال تقديره كيف جمع بين لفظ غصب وعدم الضمان مع أن الغصب للضمان؟ موجب وعلى هذا الخلاف لو باع العقار بعد الغصب وأقر بذلك وكذبه المشتري لا يقبل إقراره في حق المشتري لأن ملكه ظاهر ولا يضمن البائع عندهما لأنه لم يتلفه وإنما إتلافه مضاف إلى المالك عن إقامة البينة. وفي الكافي : ولو غصب عقاراً وهلك في يده بأن غلب السيل عليه فهلك تحت الماء أو غصب داراً فهدمت بآفة سماوية أو سيل فذهب بالبناء لم يضمن عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد والشافعي وزفر وهو قول أبي يوسف أولاً: يضمن. وفي البزازية : والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وفي الينابيع : فإن حدثت هذه الأشياء بفعل من الناس فضمانه على المتلف عندهما وعند محمد هو مخير بين ضمان الغاصب والمتلف، فإن ضمن الغاصب يرجع على المتلف وإن حدثت هذه الأشياء بفعل الغاصب وسكناه فالضمان عليه بالإجماع.
By his dwelling in it and his cultivation, he is liable for the defect, just as in movable property. And if he uses it, he gives the yield in charity, just as if he disposed [of it]. ... The liability in what is mentioned is due to abandoning the required preservation. The application of the word 'usurpation' to it does not indicate the reality of usurpation for liability, like the application of the word 'sale' to the sale of a free person due to his statement, peace be upon him, "Whoever sells a free person..." the hadith. It does not indicate the reality of the sale of a free person, and this is because it is known that in the language of the Law there is literal and figurative. And in this there is a question: How can the word 'usurpation' and absence of liability be combined while usurpation entails liability? ... And upon this difference, if he sold the real estate after usurpation and acknowledged it, and the buyer denied it, his acknowledgment is not accepted regarding the buyer because his ownership is apparent, and the seller is not liable according to the two [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf] because he did not destroy it; rather, its destruction is attributed to the owner upon establishing the evidence. In al-Kafi: If he usurped real estate and it perished in his hand, such as a flood overwhelming it and it perished under water, or he usurped a house and it collapsed due to a natural disaster or flood and the building was carried away, he is not liable according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad, al-Shafi'i, and Zufar, and it was the first opinion of Abu Yusuf, said: He is liable. In al-Bazzaziyya: The correct view is the opinion of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. In al-Yanabi': If these things occurred by the act of people, then the liability is on the destroyer according to the two [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf], and according to Muhammad he has the choice between holding the usurper liable or the destroyer; if he holds the usurper liable, he may recourse against the destroyer. And if these things occurred by the act of the usurper or his dwelling, then liability is on him by consensus.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the liability (damān) for decrease in usurped real estate (ʿaqār). The author clarifies that mere usurpation of immovable property does not automatically make the usurper liable for any subsequent damage; rather, liability arises from actual use (dwelling, cultivation) that causes harm. The term 'ghasb' is applied figuratively here, as the legal reality of usurpation entails liability, but in this context it is a metaphorical extension. The author provides an analogy: calling a sale of a free person a 'sale' is figurative, not real. The passage then addresses a specific case: if the usurper sells the property and the buyer denies the usurpation, the seller's confession is not effective against the buyer because the buyer's apparent ownership prevails. The seller is not liable according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf because he did not cause the destruction; the destruction is attributed to the owner (by his own actions such as failing to prove his case). The text then reports an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the property is destroyed by a natural disaster, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold no liability, while Muhammad al-Shaybani, al-Shafiʿi, Zufar, and the earlier opinion of Abu Yusuf hold liability. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Finally, it distinguishes: if the destruction is caused by a third party's action, the liability is on that third party; if caused by the usurper's own act or dwelling, he is liable by consensus.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation: wrongful taking of another's property without right, but in this context applied metaphorically to real estate where liability differs.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability or guarantee; here, the obligation to compensate for loss or damage to the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, if a usurped house collapses due to a natural flood, what is the ruling on the usurper's liability?
A) The usurper is liable for the full value.
B) The usurper is not liable.
C) The usurper is liable only if he was dwelling there.
D) The usurper is partially liable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a usurper of real estate is liable only for decreases caused by his own actions, not for losses due to natural disasters.

Options of the Landowner When Usurped Crop Is Seed
Options for landowner when crop not sprouted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the landowner when usurped land is cultivated and the crop is still seed, and calculate the compensation for the seed's enhancement.

Core Idea
The landowner may either wait for sprouting then order uprooting, or pay the enhancement value of the seed.

In Hanafi fiqh, when land is usurped and cultivated, the owner is not forced to accept the crop as is. This lesson explores the rulings from multiple authoritative works (Fatawa Abi al-Layth, al-Dhakhira, al-Uyun, al-Hawi) that detail the landowner's options when the crop is still seed. You will learn the legal basis for the two choices and the method of calculating the compensation due to the usurper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal condition
First, determine whether the crop has sprouted or is still seed. This lesson applies only to the latter.
2
State the landowner's two options
The landowner may either wait for the crop to sprout and then order the usurper to uproot it, or pay the usurper the value of the seed's enhancement to the land.
The option to pay is not a purchase of the crop but compensation for the increase in land value.
3
Understand the calculation method
The enhancement value is the difference between the land's value with the seed (considered removable) and its value without the seed.
Some narrations (Abu Yusuf) require valuing the seed as subject to uprooting, but the core method is similar.
4
Note the Hanafi textual variations
The sources differ slightly in expression but agree on the fundamental rule. Recognize that these are expansions of the same principle.
This shows the development of fiqh through later works (fatawa mutakhkhirin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : غصب أرضاً وزرعها وبنت فلصاحبها أن يأخذ الأرض ويأمر الغاصب بقلع الزرع تفريغاً لملكه، فإن أبى أن يفعل فللمغصوب منه أن يفعل. وفي الذخيرة: وإن لم يحضر المالك حتى أدرك الزرع فالزرع للغاصب وللمالك أن يرجع على الغاصب بنقصان الأرض بسبب الزراعة، وإن حضر المالك والزرع لم ينبت فإن شاء صاحب الأرض يتركها حتى ينبت الزرع ثم يأمر بقلع الزرع ، وإن شاء أعطاه قيمة بذره لكن مبذوراً في أرض غيره وهو أن تقوم الأرض مبذورة وغير مبذورة فيضمن فضل ما بينهما والبذر له. وفي العيون : غصب من آخر أرضاً وزرعها حنطة ثم اختصما وهي بذر لم تنبت بعد فصاحب الأرض بالخيار إن شاء تركها حتى تنبت ثم يقول له اقلع زرعك وإن شاء إعطاه ما زاد البذر فيه، وطريق معرفة ذلك أن تقوم مبذورة وغير مبذورة فيه فيضمن فضل ما بينهما . مسألة وفي الحاوي وروي عن أبي يوسف أنه يقوم الأرض غير مبذور فيها وتقوم وهي مبذور فيها بذر مستحق القلع فيضمن فضل ما بينهما وهو قيمة بذر مبذور في أرض الغير فيضمن الفضل. وفي الفتاوى غصب حنطة فزرعها تصدق بالفضل إلا على قول أبي يوسف. وفي المنتقى للمعلى وفي نوادره عن أبي يوسف أرض بين رجلين زرعها أحدهما بغير إذن شريكه فتراضيا على أن يعطي غير الزارع نصف البذر ويكون الزرع بينهما نصفين قال : إن كان ذلك منهما بعدما نبت الزرع فهو جائز، وإن كان قبل أن ينبت لا يجوز، وإن كان الزرع قد نبت وأراد الذي لم يزرع أن يقلع الزرع فإن الأرض تقسم بينهما نصفين، فما أصلب الذي لم يزرع من الزرع فله ويضمن له الزراع ما دخل أرضه من نقصان الزراعة . وقوله بسكناه أو زراعته ليس بقيد فلو غصب عقاراً وحبس عن صاحبه حتى نزت أرضه أو أرضاً حتى غلب عليها ما يمنع من الزراعة يضمن النقصان لظهور العيب عنده كما لو غصب عبداً وسرق ما في يده وهي حادثة الفتوى وأجاب الفقير عنها بما ذكر أخذاً من العبد. وفي الأسبيجابي : رجل غصب أرضاً فأجادها وأخذ غلتها أو زرع الأرض كراً فخرج منه ثلاثة أكرار قال : يأخذ رأس ماله الكر ويتصدق بالفضل ويضمن الغلة ويضمن النقصان وهذا في قولهم جميعاً. وفي الكافي ويأخذ الغاصب رأس ماله أي البذر وما أنفق وما غرم النقصان ويتصدق بالفضل وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند الثاني لا يتصدق.
In Fatawa Abi al-Layth: He usurped land, cultivated it and [built?]; so its owner may take the land and order the usurper to uproot the crop to vacate his property; if he refuses, the one from whom it was usurped may do it. In al-Dhakhira: If the owner did not come until the crop ripened, the crop belongs to the usurper, and the owner may recourse against the usurper for the defect in the land due to cultivation. If the owner comes and the crop has not sprouted, the landowner may either leave it until the crop sprouts and then order its uprooting, or give him the value of his seed but sown in another's land, which is that the land is valued with and without the seed, and he guarantees the difference, and the seed is his. In al-Uyun: He usurped another's land and cultivated it with wheat, then they disputed while it was seed not yet sprouted; the landowner has the choice: if he wishes, he leaves it until it sprouts then says 'Uproot your crop'; or if he wishes, he gives him the increase that the seed brought in it. The way to know that is to value it with and without the seed, and he guarantees the difference. Issue: In al-Hawi and narrated from Abu Yusuf that he values the land without the seed, and values it with the seed being subject to uprooting, so he guarantees the difference, which is the value of seed sown in another's land; he guarantees the excess. In al-Fatawa: He usurped wheat and sowed it; he gives the excess in charity, except according to Abu Yusuf's opinion. In al-Muntaqa al-Mu'alla and in his Nawadir from Abu Yusuf: Land between two men; one of them cultivated it without the partner's permission, and they agreed that the non-farmer gives half the seed and the crop is between them equally. He said: If that was from them after the crop sprouted, it is permissible; if before sprouting, it is not permissible. If the crop had sprouted and the one who did not cultivate wants to uproot it, the land is divided between them in half; whatever the one who did not cultivate strengthens from the crop is his, and the cultivator guarantees for him the defect of cultivation that entered his land. And his statement 'by his dwelling or his cultivation' is not a restriction; for if he usurped real estate and detained it from its owner until his land became salty, or land until something overcame it preventing cultivation, he guarantees the defect due to the appearance of the defect with him, just as if he usurped a slave and stole what was in his hand. This is the incident of fatwa, and the poor one answered it with what was mentioned, taking from the slave. In al-Isbijabi: A man usurped land and improved it and took its produce, or sowed the land with one kurr and three kurr came out; he said: He takes his capital, the kurr, and gives the surplus in charity, and he guarantees the produce and guarantees the defect. This is according to all of them. In al-Kafi: The usurper takes his capital, i.e., the seed and what he spent and what he incurred as defect, and gives the surplus in charity. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; according to the second [Abu Yusuf], he does not give charity.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage collects multiple rulings from various later Hanafi works (Fatawa, al-Dhakhira, al-Uyun, etc.) regarding the situation where land is usurped and cultivated. The key points: If the owner arrives after the crop has ripened, the crop belongs to the usurper (since he sowed it), but the owner is entitled to compensation for the defect (nuqsān) caused to the land. If the crop is still seed (not yet sprouted), the owner has an option: either allow it to grow then order the usurper to uproot it, or pay the usurper the value of the enhancement (the increase in land value due to the seed being sown). The method to calculate this is to appraise the land without seed and with seed (but the seed is considered removable), and the usurper must pay the difference. The text also discusses a co-ownership case: if one partner cultivates without permission, and they later agree, it is valid only after sprouting. Finally, it mentions that the rule about dwelling and cultivation is not restrictive; any act that causes defect (like making land salty) also incurs liability. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the usurper must give the surplus produce in charity: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes; Abu Yusuf says no (he keeps it).

Key Terms
البذر (al-badhr): Seed that is sown in the ground; its value and status determine the calculation of compensation.
النقصان (al-nuqsān): The defect or reduction in value of the land caused by cultivation or other use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the texts cited, what is the method for calculating the enhancement value of seed when the landowner chooses to pay instead of waiting?
A) Value the seed at its purchase price.
B) Subtract the land value without seed from the land value with seed.
C) Multiply the seed weight by the current market price.
D) Estimate the expected harvest and divide by half.

Key Takeaway
When usurped land is cultivated and the crop is still seed, the landowner has two equitable options: wait for sprouting then order uprooting, or pay the enhancement value of the seed.

Ripened Crop Belongs to Usurper; Charity Obligation on Surplus
Ripened crop ownership and charity dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the ripened crop belongs to the usurper, state the landowner's right to compensation for defect, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on charity for surplus produce.

Core Idea
If crop ripens before owner recovers land, crop is usurper's; owner gets defect compensation; surplus yield must be given in charity (majority view).

This lesson covers the ruling when the landowner arrives after the crop has ripened. The usurper keeps the harvest, but the owner is entitled to compensation for any decrease in land value (nuqsān). Additionally, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding surplus produce: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the usurper to give any surplus yield in charity, while Abu Yusuf holds he may keep it. This lesson uses al-Isbijabi and al-Kafi to illustrate the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal condition: crop ripened
If the owner does not come until the crop is fully grown (adraka al-zar'), the crop belongs to the usurper.
This is stated in al-Dhakhira and other sources.
2
State the owner's right to compensation for defect
Even though the crop is lost to the owner, he can recover for the reduction in land value caused by cultivation.
The defect (nuqsān) is compensable regardless of who owns the crop.
3
Understand the charity obligation on surplus
The usurper recovers his capital (seed and expenses) from the harvest. Any surplus must be given in charity (majority view).
This prevents unjust enrichment from usurpation.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on charity
Abu Yusuf disagrees and holds that the usurper may keep the surplus. Later practice often follows the majority.
The text says 'this is according to all of them' for the capital recovery, then specifies the disagreement on charity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : غصب أرضاً وزرعها وبنت فلصاحبها أن يأخذ الأرض ويأمر الغاصب بقلع الزرع تفريغاً لملكه، فإن أبى أن يفعل فللمغصوب منه أن يفعل. وفي الذخيرة: وإن لم يحضر المالك حتى أدرك الزرع فالزرع للغاصب وللمالك أن يرجع على الغاصب بنقصان الأرض بسبب الزراعة، وإن حضر المالك والزرع لم ينبت فإن شاء صاحب الأرض يتركها حتى ينبت الزرع ثم يأمر بقلع الزرع ، وإن شاء أعطاه قيمة بذره لكن مبذوراً في أرض غيره وهو أن تقوم الأرض مبذورة وغير مبذورة فيضمن فضل ما بينهما والبذر له. وفي العيون : غصب من آخر أرضاً وزرعها حنطة ثم اختصما وهي بذر لم تنبت بعد فصاحب الأرض بالخيار إن شاء تركها حتى تنبت ثم يقول له اقلع زرعك وإن شاء إعطاه ما زاد البذر فيه، وطريق معرفة ذلك أن تقوم مبذورة وغير مبذورة فيه فيضمن فضل ما بينهما . مسألة وفي الحاوي وروي عن أبي يوسف أنه يقوم الأرض غير مبذور فيها وتقوم وهي مبذور فيها بذر مستحق القلع فيضمن فضل ما بينهما وهو قيمة بذر مبذور في أرض الغير فيضمن الفضل. وفي الفتاوى غصب حنطة فزرعها تصدق بالفضل إلا على قول أبي يوسف. وفي المنتقى للمعلى وفي نوادره عن أبي يوسف أرض بين رجلين زرعها أحدهما بغير إذن شريكه فتراضيا على أن يعطي غير الزارع نصف البذر ويكون الزرع بينهما نصفين قال : إن كان ذلك منهما بعدما نبت الزرع فهو جائز، وإن كان قبل أن ينبت لا يجوز، وإن كان الزرع قد نبت وأراد الذي لم يزرع أن يقلع الزرع فإن الأرض تقسم بينهما نصفين، فما أصلب الذي لم يزرع من الزرع فله ويضمن له الزراع ما دخل أرضه من نقصان الزراعة . وقوله بسكناه أو زراعته ليس بقيد فلو غصب عقاراً وحبس عن صاحبه حتى نزت أرضه أو أرضاً حتى غلب عليها ما يمنع من الزراعة يضمن النقصان لظهور العيب عنده كما لو غصب عبداً وسرق ما في يده وهي حادثة الفتوى وأجاب الفقير عنها بما ذكر أخذاً من العبد. وفي الأسبيجابي : رجل غصب أرضاً فأجادها وأخذ غلتها أو زرع الأرض كراً فخرج منه ثلاثة أكرار قال : يأخذ رأس ماله الكر ويتصدق بالفضل ويضمن الغلة ويضمن النقصان وهذا في قولهم جميعاً. وفي الكافي ويأخذ الغاصب رأس ماله أي البذر وما أنفق وما غرم النقصان ويتصدق بالفضل وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند الثاني لا يتصدق.

Commentary
This passage collects multiple rulings from various later Hanafi works (Fatawa, al-Dhakhira, al-Uyun, etc.) regarding the situation where land is usurped and cultivated. البذر (al-badhr): Seed that is sown; part of the capital deduction.
النقصان (al-nuqsān): Defect or reduction in land value, compensable in all views.
التصدق بالفضل (al-taṣadduq bil-faḍl): Giving the surplus yield in charity; obligatory in majority view.

In the al-Kafi quote, what does the usurper take as his capital before charity?
A) Only the seed.

Key Takeaway
When the crop ripens before owner recovers land, the usurper owns the crop but must compensate for land defect, and the surplus must be given in charity according to the majority of Hanafi imams.

Co-ownership Cultivation and Liability for Defect Beyond Cultivation
Co-ownership cultivation and extension of defect liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when one co-owner cultivates without permission, identify when agreements are valid, and explain that liability for defect applies to any act that diminishes property value (e.g., causing salination).

Core Idea
Co-owner cultivation without permission is invalid if agreed before sprouting; liability for defect extends to any damage, not just cultivation.

This lesson covers two distinct but related points from the passage. First, when one co-owner cultivates shared land without the other's permission, any agreement to share the crop is valid only if made after the crop has sprouted. Second, the principle of liability for defect (nuqsān) is not limited to cultivation; any act that causes a reduction in property value, such as making land salty or detaining a slave, triggers compensation. This extension is stated explicitly by the author, drawing an analogy to the slave case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the co-ownership cultivation scenario
Two partners share land; one cultivates without the other's permission.
2
Identify the validity condition for agreements
If they agree to share the crop after it has sprouted, the agreement is valid. If before sprouting, it is invalid.
The rationale: before sprouting, the seed is not yet property, so the agreement is like selling something that doesn't exist.
3
Learn the remedy for the non-cultivating partner
If the crop has sprouted, the non-cultivating partner can demand uprooting of the crop on his half of divided land.
The land is divided equally; whatever he strengthens/crops is his, and the cultivator compensates for defect on his half.
4
Extend liability beyond cultivation
The text explicitly states that the ruling is not restricted to dwelling or cultivation; any act causing defect (like making land salty) incurs liability.
The analogy to usurping a slave and stealing from his hand is used to illustrate that the principle applies broadly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : غصب أرضاً وزرعها وبنت فلصاحبها أن يأخذ الأرض ويأمر الغاصب بقلع الزرع تفريغاً لملكه، فإن أبى أن يفعل فللمغصوب منه أن يفعل. وفي الذخيرة: وإن لم يحضر المالك حتى أدرك الزرع فالزرع للغاصب وللمالك أن يرجع على الغاصب بنقصان الأرض بسبب الزراعة، وإن حضر المالك والزرع لم ينبت فإن شاء صاحب الأرض يتركها حتى ينبت الزرع ثم يأمر بقلع الزرع ، وإن شاء أعطاه قيمة بذره لكن مبذوراً في أرض غيره وهو أن تقوم الأرض مبذورة وغير مبذورة فيضمن فضل ما بينهما والبذر له. وفي العيون : غصب من آخر أرضاً وزرعها حنطة ثم اختصما وهي بذر لم تنبت بعد فصاحب الأرض بالخيار إن شاء تركها حتى تنبت ثم يقول له اقلع زرعك وإن شاء إعطاه ما زاد البذر فيه، وطريق معرفة ذلك أن تقوم مبذورة وغير مبذورة فيه فيضمن فضل ما بينهما . مسألة وفي الحاوي وروي عن أبي يوسف أنه يقوم الأرض غير مبذور فيها وتقوم وهي مبذور فيها بذر مستحق القلع فيضمن فضل ما بينهما وهو قيمة بذر مبذور في أرض الغير فيضمن الفضل. وفي الفتاوى غصب حنطة فزرعها تصدق بالفضل إلا على قول أبي يوسف. وفي المنتقى للمعلى وفي نوادره عن أبي يوسف أرض بين رجلين زرعها أحدهما بغير إذن شريكه فتراضيا على أن يعطي غير الزارع نصف البذر ويكون الزرع بينهما نصفين قال : إن كان ذلك منهما بعدما نبت الزرع فهو جائز، وإن كان قبل أن ينبت لا يجوز، وإن كان الزرع قد نبت وأراد الذي لم يزرع أن يقلع الزرع فإن الأرض تقسم بينهما نصفين، فما أصلب الذي لم يزرع من الزرع فله ويضمن له الزراع ما دخل أرضه من نقصان الزراعة . وقوله بسكناه أو زراعته ليس بقيد فلو غصب عقاراً وحبس عن صاحبه حتى نزت أرضه أو أرضاً حتى غلب عليها ما يمنع من الزراعة يضمن النقصان لظهور العيب عنده كما لو غصب عبداً وسرق ما في يده وهي حادثة الفتوى وأجاب الفقير عنها بما ذكر أخذاً من العبد. وفي الأسبيجابي : رجل غصب أرضاً فأجادها وأخذ غلتها أو زرع الأرض كراً فخرج منه ثلاثة أكرار قال : يأخذ رأس ماله الكر ويتصدق بالفضل ويضمن الغلة ويضمن النقصان وهذا في قولهم جميعاً. وفي الكافي ويأخذ الغاصب رأس ماله أي البذر وما أنفق وما غرم النقصان ويتصدق بالفضل وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند الثاني لا يتصدق.
In Fatawa Abi al-Layth: He usurped land, cultivated it and [built?]; so its owner may take the land and order the usurper to uproot the crop to vacate his property; if he refuses, the one from whom it was usurped may do it. In al-Dhakhira: If the owner did not come until the crop ripened, the crop belongs to the usurper, and the owner may recourse against the usurper for the defect in the land due to cultivation. If the owner comes and the crop has not sprouted, the landowner may either leave it until the crop sprouts and then order its uprooting, or give him the value of his seed but sown in another's land, which is that the land is valued with and without the seed, and he guarantees the difference, and the seed is his. In al-Uyun: He usurped another's land and cultivated it with wheat, then they disputed while it was seed not yet sprouted; the landowner has the choice: if he wishes, he leaves it until it sprouts then says 'Uproot your crop'; or if he wishes, he gives him the increase that the seed brought in it. The way to know that is to value it with and without the seed, and he guarantees the difference. Issue: In al-Hawi and narrated from Abu Yusuf that he values the land without the seed, and values it with the seed being subject to uprooting, so he guarantees the difference, which is the value of seed sown in another's land; he guarantees the excess. In al-Fatawa: He usurped wheat and sowed it; he gives the excess in charity, except according to Abu Yusuf's opinion. In al-Muntaqa al-Mu'alla and in his Nawadir from Abu Yusuf: Land between two men; one of them cultivated it without the partner's permission, and they agreed that the non-farmer gives half the seed and the crop is between them equally. He said: If that was from them after the crop sprouted, it is permissible; if before sprouting, it is not permissible. If the crop had sprouted and the one who did not cultivate wants to uproot it, the land is divided between them in half; whatever the one who did not cultivate strengthens from the crop is his, and the cultivator guarantees for him the defect of cultivation that entered his land. And his statement 'by his dwelling or his cultivation' is not a restriction; for if he usurped real estate and detained it from its owner until his land became salty, or land until something overcame it preventing cultivation, he guarantees the defect due to the appearance of the defect with him, just as if he usurped a slave and stole what was in his hand. This is the incident of fatwa, and the poor one answered it with what was mentioned, taking from the slave. In al-Isbijabi: A man usurped land and improved it and took its produce, or sowed the land with one kurr and three kurr came out; he said: He takes his capital, the kurr, and gives the surplus in charity, and he guarantees the produce and guarantees the defect. This is according to all of them. In al-Kafi: The usurper takes his capital, i.e., the seed and what he spent and what he incurred as defect, and gives the surplus in charity. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; according to the second [Abu Yusuf], he does not give charity.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage collects multiple rulings from various later Hanafi works (Fatawa, al-Dhakhira, al-Uyun, etc.) regarding the situation where land is usurped and cultivated. The key points: If the owner arrives after the crop has ripened, the crop belongs to the usurper (since he sowed it), but the owner is entitled to compensation for the defect (nuqsān) caused to the land. If the crop is still seed (not yet sprouted), the owner has an option: either allow it to grow then order the usurper to uproot it, or pay the usurper the value of the enhancement (the increase in land value due to the seed being sown). The method to calculate this is to appraise the land without seed and with seed (but the seed is considered removable), and the usurper must pay the difference. The text also discusses a co-ownership case: if one partner cultivates without permission, and they later agree, it is valid only after sprouting. Finally, it mentions that the rule about dwelling and cultivation is not restrictive; any act that causes defect (like making land salty) also incurs liability. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the usurper must give the surplus produce in charity: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes; Abu Yusuf says no (he keeps it).

Key Terms
الزرع (al-zar'): The crop; its existence (sprouted or not) determines validity of agreement.
النقصان (al-nuqsān): Defect or reduction in land value, compensable for any cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the co-ownership case, what is the consequence if the agreement to share the crop is made before the crop has sprouted?
A) The agreement is valid but optional.
B) The agreement is invalid.
C) The agreement is valid only if witnessed.
D) The agreement is suspended until harvest.

Key Takeaway
Cultivation by a co-owner without permission yields a conditional agreement validity (sprouting required), and the liability for defect applies broadly to any act harming the property.

Planting Trees on Usurped Land: The Planter’s Obligation to Remove
Rulings on planting trees in another's land and the owner's right to demand removal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on planting trees on usurped land and explain the conditions under which removal is required or compensation is given.

Core Idea
A tree planted on usurped land belongs to the planter, but he must remove it at the landowner’s demand; compensation is only an option if removal harms the land.

When a person plants a tree on another’s land without permission, the tree is his property, but the landowner retains the right to have it removed. This lesson examines the Hanafi position: the planter must uproot the tree at his own expense. Only if uprooting would cause damage to the land can the owner choose to pay the tree’s value instead. We will explore the key principles and exceptions from the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is planting (ghars) in another's land without permission. The planter becomes owner of the tree, but the ruling is that removal (qalʿ) is wajib upon him if the landowner demands it.
Ownership of the tree does not grant the right to keep it on another's property.
2
Understand the landowner's right
The landowner has the right to demand removal; he cannot be forced to accept compensation instead. Only the planter may voluntarily choose to uproot or accept payment.
Contrast with other schools that allow the owner to force payment.
3
Recognize the exception for harmful removal
If uprooting would harm the land (e.g., by damaging its structure), the owner may pay the planter the value of the tree uprooted at the time of removal. This is an alternative to physical removal.
The value is assessed at the time of uprooting, not planting.
4
Note the source of authority
The passage cites al-Tatimma, Ali ibn Ahmad, and al-Khujandi to clarify that the landowner's right is limited to ordering removal; the planter decides on removal or compensation.
Later fatāwā uphold this distinction.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a planter refuses to remove after demand, the landowner may remove the tree himself and claim damages for any defect caused to the land.
The planter remains liable for any damage from the planting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غصب أرض إنسان وزرعها في ناحية أخرى من تلك الأرض فكبرت التالة وصارت شجرة تالة فالشجرة للغارس وعليه قيمة التالة لصاحبها يوم غصبها ويأمر الغارس بقلع الشجرة، وكذلك لو غرس رجل تالة نفسه في أرض غيره فلصاحب الأرض أن يأخذه بقلعها، وإن كان القلع يضر الأرض أعطاه صاحب الأرض قيمة شجرته مقلوعة؛ كذا قيل، وفي يوم يختصمان. وعلى قياس مسألة الزرع التي تقدم ذكرها يمكن أن يقال : أعطاه صاحب الأرض قيمة شجرة مستحقة القلع وفي التتمة سئل عمن غرس في أرض الغير غرساً فكبر هل لصاحب الأرض أن يقول ادفع لك قيمته ولا تقلعه؟ فقال : لا إنما للغارس أن يقلعه ويضمن النقصان إن ظهر في الأرض نقصان، وإنما لصاحب الأرض الأمر بالقلع فحسب، وسئل عنها علي بن أحمد فقال : للغارس قيمة الأغصان حين غرسها إذا كان في قلعها ضرر بالأرض ولم يتعرض هل يضمن القيمة وقت الغرس أو وقت القلع. وسئل الخجندي عمن غرس في أرض غيره فنبت هل للغارس أن يقلعها؟ فقال له أن يقلعها إن لم تنقص الأرض. وفي فتاوى : رجل زرع أرض نفسه فجاء رجل وألقى بذره في تلك الأرض وقلب الأرض قبل أن تنبت بذر صاحب الأرض أو لم يقلب وسقى الأرض حتى نبت البذر فالنابت من من من التتمة
He usurped a person's land and cultivated it in another part of that land, so the seedling grew and became a tree; the tree is for the planter, and he owes the value of the seedling to its owner on the day he usurped it, and he orders the planter to uproot the tree. Similarly, if a man plants his own seedling in another's land, the landowner may take him to uproot it. If the uprooting harms the land, the landowner gives him the value of his tree uprooted; thus it is said. And on the day they dispute. And by analogy to the issue of cultivation mentioned earlier, it can be said: The landowner gives him the value of a tree subject to uprooting. In al-Tatimma: He was asked about one who planted a seedling in another's land and it grew; can the landowner say 'I will give you its value and you do not uproot it'? He said: No, only the planter may uproot it and guarantee the defect if any defect appears in the land; the landowner only has the order to uproot. He was asked about it by Ali ibn Ahmad, who said: The planter is given the value of the branches when he planted them if their uprooting would harm the land, and he did not specify whether the value is at the time of planting or of uprooting. He asked al-Khujandi about one who planted in another's land and it grew; does the planter have the right to uproot it? He said: He may uproot it if it does not harm the land. In Fatawa: A man sowed his own land; another man came and threw his seed in that land and turned the soil before the seed of the landowner sprouted or did not turn, and watered the land until the seed sprouted; then the sprouted [belongs] to... from at-Tatimma.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of planting trees on usurped land. The general rule: the planter owns the tree (since he planted it), but he must remove it at the landowner's request. The landowner cannot be forced to accept compensation instead of removal. However, if uprooting would cause significant harm to the land, the landowner may pay the value of the tree (as it stands) to the planter as an alternative. The value is assessed at the time of uprooting, but there is some difference of opinion. The key distinction is that only the planter has the right to uproot voluntarily; the landowner can only demand removal. If the planter does not comply, the landowner may do it himself and claim damages for any defect caused. The passage also mentions a scenario where someone sows seed in another's land without permission; the resulting crop belongs to the sower but he must compensate for the land use. The last part is cut off.

Key Terms
الغارس (al-ghāris): The planter, one who plants a tree or seedling in the ground.
القلع (al-qalʿ): Uprooting or removal of the tree from the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Tatimma, can the landowner force the planter to accept payment instead of uprooting the tree?
A) Yes, the landowner can force payment.
B) No, only the planter may choose to uproot or accept payment.
C) No, the landowner can only demand uprooting.
D) Yes, if the tree has grown large.

Key Takeaway
A planter on usurped land must remove the plant at the owner’s demand; the owner may only pay compensation if removal would harm the land.

Usurper's Planting: Owner's Options and Valuation
States the owner's options and valuation method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the owner of land usurped by planting and apply the Hanafi valuation method.

Core Idea
When usurped land is seeded, the owner may either await harvest or accept the increased land value.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in property disputes: a usurper plants seeds on another's land. The Hanafi school grants the original owner two equitable options to resolve the matter without undue hardship to either party. Understanding these options and the valuation method is essential for applying justice in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal context
Recognize that the ruling applies to usurpation (ghasb) of land where the usurper has planted seeds. The act is not the usurpation itself but the planting that increases value.
Usurpation is haram, but the ruling here addresses the remedy.
2
Outline the two options
The owner may either: (1) wait until the crop grows and demand the usurper remove it, or (2) accept the increased value of the land due to the seed and pay the usurper the difference.
The owner has full discretion; no option is forced.
3
Understand the valuation method
The compensation for the second option is determined by appraising the land in two states: seeded and unseeded. The difference (faḍl mā baynahumā) is what the usurper must pay.
This method isolates the seed's contribution to land value.
4
Note the consequences of each choice
If the owner chooses removal, the usurper bears the cost. If the owner keeps the increased value, he compensates the usurper. The owner cannot change his mind after choosing.
Certainty in selection avoids prolonged disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالخيار إن شاء تركها حتى تنبت ثم يقول له اقلع ،زرعك وإن شاء إعطاه ما زاد البذر فيه، وطريق معرفة ذلك أن تقوم مبذورة وغير مبذورة فيه فيضمن فضل ما بينهما .
He has the option: if he wishes, he leaves it until it grows, then tells him to remove your crop; and if he wishes, he gives him the increase from the seed in it. The method of knowing that is to appraise it seeded and unseeded, then he guarantees the difference between them.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This is a ruling on usurping land by planting seeds. The owner has two choices: wait for the crop to grow and then order the usurper to remove it, or accept the increased value from the seed. The value increase is measured by comparing the land's value with and without the seed. This is a typical Hanafi position, protecting the owner's property while allowing the usurper to recover his costs.

Key Terms
فضل ما بينهما (faḍl mā baynahumā): The difference in value between land seeded and unseeded

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the method to determine the compensation when the owner chooses to take the increased value?
A) Compare the market price of the crop
B) Appraise the land both seeded and unseeded, then pay the difference
C) Pay the value of the seed alone
D) Pay double the value of the land

Key Takeaway
The owner of usurped planted land has two options: wait for harvest or accept the increased land value measured by the seeded-unseeded difference.

Abu Yusuf's Method for Valuing Sown Seed
Narration of Abu Yusuf's valuation opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu Yusuf's valuation method and distinguish it from the general Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf values land with and without removably-sown seed; the difference is compensation.

In the previous lesson, we learned the general Hanafi ruling for valuation when seed is sown in another's land. Here, we examine a specific narration from Imam Abu Yusuf that introduces a refined method. This intra-Hanafi dispute highlights how the condition of the seed — whether it is liable to be uprooted — affects the calculation of compensation. Understanding this nuance deepens your grasp of Hanafi taqwim (valuation) principles.

Guided Path
1
Recall the General Hanafi Rule
Begin by recalling the general Hanafi position: when seed is sown in another's land, the cultivator compensates the landowner for the seed's value as a separate item. That is the standard view.
If needed, review the previous lesson's material.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's Method
Now, read the passage: Abu Yusuf values the land without seed, then values it with seed that is liable to be uprooted (mustaḥiqq al-qalʿ). The difference between these two values is the compensation. This is not the value of the seed alone, but the excess in land value caused by the sown seed.
The key phrase is 'بذر مستحق القلع' — seed that the owner may remove.
3
Understand the Condition 'Mustahiqq al-Qal'
Abu Yusuf's method only applies when the seed is not a permanent fixture — i.e., it can be uprooted. This condition changes the valuation because the seed is not considered an integral part of the land. If the seed were permanent, the general rule would apply.
Reflect on how the condition affects the legal reasoning.
4
Compare and Contrast with General Position
The general position (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) values the seed itself as an independent item. Abu Yusuf's method values the enhancement to the land. The practical difference is in the amount of compensation. The general position is mufta bihi.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحاوي وروي عن أبي يوسف أنه يقوم الأرض غير مبذور فيها وتقوم وهي مبذور فيها بذر مستحق القلع فيضمن فضل ما بينهما وهو قيمة بذر مبذور في أرض الغير فيضمن الفضل.
In al-Hawi, it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he appraises the land without seed, and appraises it with seed that is liable to be uprooted, then he guarantees the difference between them; that is the value of seed sown in another's land, so he guarantees the excess.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf's method differs from the general ruling. He values the land without seed and with seed that must be removed (i.e., the seed is not necessarily stay). The difference is the compensation. This is a refinement of the valuation method.

Key Terms
مستحق القلع (mustaḥiqq al-qalʿ): Liable to be uprooted; seed that the owner may remove

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what is the compensation when seed is sown in another's land?
A) The value of the seed alone
B) The value of the land with seed minus without seed
C) The value of the land without seed, plus the seed value
D) No compensation if the seed can be removed

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's method values the enhancement to land from removable seed, not the seed itself, representing a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Charity on Profit from Usurped Wheat
Ruling on charity from profit of usurped planted wheat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on charity from profit of usurped wheat and distinguish Abu Yusuf's dissenting view.

Core Idea
Profit from stolen property must be given in charity — except according to Abu Yusuf.

Imagine someone takes another's wheat without permission and plants it. The harvest is a mix of the original value and a surplus. The Hanafi school, as recorded in al-Fatawa, requires the usurper to give the surplus in charity to purify the unlawful gain. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that no charity is required, as the profit is considered permissible. This intra-Hanafi dispute highlights the school's internal reasoning on unlawful gain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The scenario is usurping wheat and planting it. The ruling in the text is 'yatasaddaq bi al-fadl' (give the excess in charity).
The ruling is clear for the majority.
2
Understand the basis for charity
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) sees the profit as tainted because it arose from wrongful possession; charity purifies it.
The principle of 'al-taba'iyyah' (the profit follows the origin) applies.
3
Recognize Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf likely considers the wheat transformed by planting, so the profit is not ill-gotten; the original value is returned, and the profit is permissible.
His view is noted as an exception.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The later Fatwa literature adopts the majority view as the operative ruling.
Thus, in practice, charity is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الفتاوى غصب حنطة فزرعها تصدق بالفضل إلا على قول أبي يوسف.
In al-Fatawa: he usurped wheat and planted it; he gives the excess in charity except according to Abu Yusuf's opinion.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This is another intra-Hanafi dispute. If someone usurps wheat and plants it, the resulting profit (beyond his costs and original value) must be given in charity according to the general position. Abu Yusuf disagrees and likely allows the usurper to keep the profit, as it is not ill-gotten. The majority opinion requires charity to purify the unlawful gain.

Key Terms
فضل (faḍl): Excess profit after returning the original value or costs

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi ruling regarding the excess profit from planted stolen wheat according to al-Fatawa?
A) The usurper may keep it
B) The usurper must give it in charity
C) The usurper must return it to the original owner
D) The usurper must destroy it

Key Takeaway
The profit from stolen property must be given in charity, as per the majority Hanafi view adopted in fatwa.

Guaranteeing Deficiency Due to Detention of Property
Ruling on guarantee of deficiency from mere detention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the guarantee of deficiency when property is detained without use, and apply the analogy to cases of usurped land.

Core Idea
A usurper guarantees deficiency even without using property, if mere detention causes damage.

This passage addresses a critical nuance in the law of usurpation (ghasb): does the usurper owe compensation for a decrease in value caused solely by detention, even if he never used the property? The text clarifies that the condition of 'dwelling or planting' in earlier discussions is not exclusive; the key is the defect arising during detention. The ruling is derived by analogy to a usurped slave, establishing a general principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wrongful act
Recognize that the act is usurpation and detention of real estate, depriving the owner of access.
2
Determine the type of damage
The damage is deterioration (e.g., land drying up, becoming unfit for farming) caused by the detention itself, not by active use.
3
Apply the ruling
The usurper guarantees the deficiency (nuqsan) because a defect appears in his possession, regardless of whether he dwelt or planted.
The guarantee is wajib (obligatory) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Trace the analogy
The ruling is analogized (qiyas) to a usurped slave: if the slave’s property is stolen while in the usurper’s hands, the usurper guarantees it. Similarly, if land value decreases due to detention, the usurper bears the loss.
The text labels this a 'incident of fatwa' answered by this analogy.
5
Understand the fatwa context
This ruling was issued as a fatwa for a novel case, showing how Hanafi jurists extend established rulings to new scenarios.
The authority weight is 'fatawa mutakhkhirin' (later fatwas).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله بسكناه أو زراعته ليس بقيد فلو غصب عقاراً وحبس عن صاحبه حتى نزت أرضه أو أرضاً حتى غلب عليها ما يمنع من الزراعة يضمن النقصان لظهور العيب عنده كما لو غصب عبداً وسرق ما في يده وهي حادثة الفتوى وأجاب الفقير عنها بما ذكر أخذاً من العبد.
And his saying 'by dwelling or planting' is not a condition. So if he usurped real estate and detained it from its owner until his land dried up, or land until something overwhelmed it that prevents farming, he guarantees the deficiency because a defect appears thereby, just as if he usurped a slave and what was in his hand was stolen. And it is an incident of fatwa, and the poor one answered about it with what was mentioned, taking from the slave.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that the guarantee of deficiency is not limited to cases of dwelling or planting. If the usurper detains property causing damage (e.g., land drying up, becoming unfit), he is liable for the decrease in value. This is analogized to usurping a slave and then having the slave's property stolen.

Key Terms
نزت أرضه (nazat arḍuhu): His land dried up or deteriorated due to lack of irrigation
ضمان النقصان (ḍamān al-nuqṣān): Guarantee of the deficiency: compensation for the decrease in value

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the basis for the ruling that the usurper guarantees the deficiency of land that dried up during his detention?
A) The text says 'by dwelling or planting' is a condition
B) It is analogized to a usurped slave whose property is stolen
C) Because the land becomes a liability (daman)
D) Because the owner consented

Key Takeaway
Mere detention of property causing damage triggers a guarantee of deficiency in Hanafi fiqh, even without active use.

Usurper's Rights and Duties on Improved Land
Ruling on usurper's obligations after improving land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a usurper who improves land and harvests the crop, and distinguish between recouping capital, giving charity on excess, and compensating the owner.

Core Idea
Usurper recovers costs, gives profit in charity, and compensates owner for yield and loss.

This passage from al-Asbijabi presents a consolidated Hanafi ruling on a specific scenario: a person wrongfully takes land, improves it, and harvests the crop. The ruling balances the usurper's right to recoup his investment with the owner's right to full compensation. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi approach to unjust enrichment and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
The scenario involves a usurper (ghasib) who wrongfully takes land, improves it (by tilling, fertilizing, etc.), and then takes the produce (ghullah) from the land.
2
Determine the usurper's entitlement
The usurper is allowed to take his capital (raʾs al-māl) from the harvested yield. Capital refers to the seed and other expenses incurred in cultivation.
This is not a profit but a recovery of costs.
3
Identify the charity obligation
The excess of the yield over the capital must be given in charity (sadaqah). The usurper cannot keep this profit because it arose from unlawful possession, but the owner is not entitled to it either, as it resulted from the usurper's labor and expense.
The charity purifies the usurper's gain from the taint of wrongdoing.
4
Identify the two guarantees
The usurper must guarantee (yadman) two things: (1) the yield (al-ghullah) – he must compensate the owner for the crop he took, and (2) the deficiency (al-nuqsan) – any reduction in the land's value due to his actions.
These guarantees ensure the owner is made whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الأسبيجابي : رجل غصب أرضاً فأجادها وأخذ غلتها أو زرع الأرض كراً فخرج منه ثلاثة أكرار قال : يأخذ رأس ماله الكر ويتصدق بالفضل ويضمن الغلة ويضمن النقصان وهذا في قولهم جميعاً.
In al-Asbijabi: A man usurped land and improved it and took its produce, or planted the land with a kur and got three kur from it. He said: He takes his capital, the kur, and gives charity on the excess, and guarantees the produce, and guarantees the deficiency. And this is in all their sayings.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This is a consolidated ruling: the usurper may take his costs (capital) from the crop, give the profit in charity, and also compensate the owner for both the yield taken and any loss to the land value. This applies according to all Hanafi imams except where noted elsewhere.

Key Terms
رأس المال (raʾs al-māl): The capital invested, here the seed and expenses
الغلة (al-ghullah): The yield, produce, or crop harvested from the land
النقصان (al-nuqsan): The decrease in value of the land due to the usurper's actions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the usurper do with the yield from the usurped land?
A) He gives it all to the owner
B) He takes his capital, gives charity on excess, and guarantees the yield and deficiency
C) He keeps everything
D) He gives half to the owner

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a usurper who improves land and harvests crops recovers his costs, donates the excess profit to charity, and fully compensates the owner for both the crop taken and any loss in land value.

Usurper's Charity: Intra-Hanafi Split
Intra-Hanafi dispute on usurper's charity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the usurper's excess profit, and identify the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Core Idea
The usurper must give charity on excess profit according to the majority Hanafi view; Abu Yusuf disagrees.

In cases of usurpation (ghasb) where the usurper invests the property and generates profit, the school debates whether the profit must be purified through charity. This passage from al-Kafi records the split: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require charity, while Abu Yusuf does not. Understanding this disagreement helps us see how intra-madhhab ikhtilaf can affect fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
The passage from al-Kafi states that the usurper takes his capital (seed, expenses, compensation for deficiency) and gives charity on the surplus (al-fadl). This is the position attributed to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
The source is al-Kafi, a Hanafi text.
2
Distinguish the two positions
Abu Yusuf (al-thānī) holds that the usurper does NOT give charity on the surplus. So there is a clear intra-madhhab disagreement.
Note the shorthand 'al-thānī' for Abu Yusuf.
3
Note the authority and weight
The majority of the early Hanafi school (including the two main students of Abu Hanifa on this issue) hold the charity position. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) is generally the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Zahir al-Riwayah supports the charity position.
4
Understand the practical consequence
If the usurper follows Abu Yusuf, he keeps the profit without charity. If he follows the majority, he must discharge the charity. This affects the permissibility of using the profit.
Charity purifies the wealth gained from a potentially unlawful source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الكافي ويأخذ الغاصب رأس ماله أي البذر وما أنفق وما غرم النقصان ويتصدق بالفضل وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند الثاني لا يتصدق.
And in al-Kafi: The usurper takes his capital, meaning the seed and what he spent and what he paid for the deficiency, and gives charity on the excess. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. But according to the second (Abu Yusuf), he does not give charity.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This sources the opinion to al-Kafi, clarifying that requiring charity on the excess profit is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, while Abu Yusuf holds no charity is needed. This is a key intra-Hanafi split.

Key Terms
الثاني (al-thānī): Refers to Abu Yusuf

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what must the usurper do with the excess profit?
A) Return it to the owner
B) Give it in charity
C) Keep it
D) Divide it with the owner

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi view requires the usurper to give the excess profit in charity; Abu Yusuf holds no charity is needed.
