Planting a Date-Palm in Usurped Land: Ownership and Removal
Ruling on planting a palm tree in another's land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the ownership of a tree planted in usurped land and identify the conditions under which the landowner may demand removal or must compensate.

Core Idea
The planter owns the tree, but the landowner can force removal; if removal harms, the owner pays to keep it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common scenario of trespass: someone plants a date-palm sapling (tāla) in another person's land without permission. The text carefully balances the rights of the planter (who performed the planting) and the landowner (whose property was violated). The ruling distinguishes between the usurper's own sapling and one taken from the landowner, and introduces a key exception when removal would damage the land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is planting a date-palm (tāla) in another's land without permission. The ruling is that the tree belongs to the planter, but the landowner has the right to demand its removal.
This is not a ruling on the permissibility of the planting itself, but on the legal consequences.
2
Separate the two scenarios
First scenario: the usurper plants a sapling that he took from the landowner. The tree belongs to the planter, but he must pay the value of the sapling on the day of usurpation. Second scenario: the usurper plants his own sapling. The landowner can still order removal.
In both cases, removal is the default remedy.
3
Analyze the exception of harm
If removal of the tree would harm the land, the landowner may instead keep the tree by paying the planter the value of the tree as if uprooted (i.e., a reduced value). The landowner does not have the right to keep the tree without compensation.
This is analogous to the earlier ruling on crops (zar') where the landowner could pay the value of the standing crop.
4
Identify the legal mechanism
The text uses qiyas (analogy) to the crop case. The underlying principle is that the landowner has the option to either enforce removal (even if harmful) or pay compensation and let the tree remain. The default is removal; compensation is only when removal causes harm.
The ruling protects both parties: the planter's labor is compensated, and the landowner's property rights are respected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غصب أرض إنسان وزرعها في ناحية أخرى من تلك الأرض فكبرت التالة وصارت شجرة تالة فالشجرة للغارس وعليه قيمة التالة لصاحبها يوم غصبها ويأمر الغارس بقلع الشجرة، وكذلك لو غرس رجل تالة نفسه في أرض غيره فلصاحب الأرض أن يأخذه بقلعها، وإن كان القلع يضر الأرض أعطاه صاحب الأرض قيمة شجرته مقلوعة؛ كذا قيل، وفي يوم يختصمان. وعلى قياس مسألة الزرع الذي تقدم ذكرها يمكن أن يقال : أعطاه صاحب الأرض قيمة شجرة مستحقة القلع.
He usurped someone's land and planted a date-palm in another part of that land, and the sapling grew and became a date-palm tree. The tree belongs to the planter, and upon him is the value of the sapling on the day of usurpation to its owner, and the planter is ordered to remove the tree. Likewise, if a man plants his own sapling in another's land, the owner of the land has the right to order him to remove it. And if removal harms the land, the owner of the land gives him the value of his tree as if uprooted. Thus it is said, and on the day they contest. And on the analogy of the case of crops already mentioned, it can be said: the landowner gives him the value of a tree liable to be uprooted.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This ruling covers planting a palm tree (or any tree) in usurped land. The tree belongs to the planter, but the landowner can demand its removal. If removal damages the land, the owner may pay the planter the value of the tree as if uprooted, thus keeping the tree but compensating the planter. This is analogized to the earlier crop ruling.

Key Terms
التالة (al-tāla): A young date-palm or sapling
الغارس (al-ghāris): The planter, the person who plants the tree
قلع (qalʿ): Uprooting or removal of a tree from the ground
مقلوعة (maqlūʿa): As if uprooted – referring to the value of the tree in a removed state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if removing a tree planted by the usurper would harm the land?
A) The tree must be removed anyway
B) The landowner pays the value of the tree as if uprooted and keeps it
C) The usurper pays compensation for any harm
D) The landowner cannot force removal

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a tree planted on usurped land belongs to the planter, not the landowner, but the landowner can demand removal or, if harmful, pay compensation to keep the tree.

Crop Ownership and Seed Compensation in Usurped Land
Explores multiple scenarios of planting in usurped land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of crops planted in usurped land and apply the method of valuing seed compensation across different scenarios.

Core Idea
Crop yield belongs to the landowner; the usurper is compensated for seed value based on whether it is sown in his own or another's land.

When someone plants seeds in land they have no right to, questions arise: who owns the crop? How is the usurper compensated? The Hanafi school provides detailed rulings that vary depending on timing, tilling, and consent. This lesson unpacks the key scenarios from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic scenario
The passage begins with a scenario where a usurper plants seeds in land that already has the owner's seeds. According to Abu Hanifa, the crop belongs to the second planter? Actually the text is ambiguous; read carefully to understand that the second planter is the usurper, and the first is the landowner. The yield goes to the landowner, but the usurper recovers the difference in land value with and without his seed.
The phrase 'يكون للثاني عند أبي حنيفة' may mean the yield is for the second (usurper) in some readings, but the subsequent valuation indicates the landowner ultimately gets the crop.
2
Analyse the double-planting scenario
If the landowner replants his seeds and both sets grow, the entire yield belongs to the landowner. He must compensate the usurper by paying the value of the usurper's seed as if it were sown in another's land (i.e., lower value). The comprehensive answer details a two-way calculation: the usurper pays the landowner for his seed as sown in his own land (higher value), and the landowner pays the usurper for both seeds as sown in another's land.
The key distinction is the valuation method: 'mabdhuran fi ardi nafsihi' (sown in his own land) vs. 'mabdhuran fi ardi ghayrihi' (sown in another's land).
3
Consider pre-existing sprouts
If the owner's crop had already sprouted before the usurper plants, the ruling splits: if the owner's crop can regrow if turned, the previous rules apply; if it cannot regrow, the usurper gets the yield but must pay the owner the value of his sprouting crop.
This hinges on whether the owner's sprouts are salvageable.
4
Apply the fatwa on different crops
A specific case: someone plants wheat in his own land, then another plants barley. The barley planter pays the value of his seed as sown. If the wheat owner did not consent, he can wait and uproot the barley or forgive the guarantee. When harvested, they share proportionally.
This fatwa from al-Zahiriyyah shows that consent matters, and the non-consenting party has options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله يكون للثاني عند أبي حنيفة ويكون على الثاني قيمة بذره ولكن مبذوراً في أرض نفسه فتقوم الأرض ولا بذر فيها وتقوم وبها بذره فيرجع بفضل ما بينهما، فإن جاء الزراع الأول وهو صاحب الأرض وألقى فيها بذر نفسه مرة أخرى وقلب الأرض قيل أن ينبت البذران أو لم يقلب وسقى الأرض فنبتت البذور كلها فجميع ما نبت لصاحب الأرض وعليه للغاصب مثل بذره ولكن مبذوراً في أرض غيره، وهكذا ذكر ولم يسمع الجواب. والجواب المشبع أن الغاصب يضمن لصاحب الأرض قيمة بذره مبذوراً في أرض نفسه ويضمن صاحب الأرض للغاصب قيمة البذرين لكن مبذوراً في أرض الغير. وهذا كله إذا لم يكن الزرع نابتاً بها، فأما إذا نبت زرع المالك فجاء رجل وألقى بذره وسقى فإن لم يقلب حتى نبت الثاني فإن كان الزرع النابت إذا قلب ينبت مرة أخرى فالجواب كما قلنا وإن كان لا ينبت مرة أخرى فما نبت فهو للغاصب ويضمن الغاصب للمالك قيمة زرعه نابتاً. وفي الظهيرية سئل نصير رحمه عمن زرع أرض نفسه براً فجاء رجل وزرعها شعيراً قال : على صاحب الشعير قيمة بذره مبذوراً؛ روى ذلك محمد بن سماعة عن محمد بن الحسين رحمه الله تعالى . قال الفقيه أبو الليث رحمه الله تعالى : هذا إذا رضي صاحب البذر، وأما إذا لم يرض فهو بالخيار إن شاء ترك حتى ينبت فإذا نبت يأخذه بالقلع وإن شاء أبرأه عن الضمان، فإذا استحصد الزرع وحصداه فهو بينهما على مقدار نصيبهما.
Allah it is for the second according to Abu Hanifa, and upon the second is the value of his seed but as sown in his own land: so the land is appraised without seed and appraised with its seed, and he recovers the difference. If the first farmer, who is the landowner, comes and throws his own seed again and turns the soil before the two seeds sprout or does not turn it and waters the land, and all the seeds sprout, then all that sprouts belongs to the landowner, and upon him is the like of the usurper's seed but as sown in another's land. Thus it is mentioned, and the answer was not heard. The comprehensive answer is that the usurper guarantees for the landowner the value of his seed as sown in his own land, and the landowner guarantees for the usurper the value of the two seeds but as sown in another's land. This all is if the crop was not sprouting. As for if the owner's crop had sprouted and a man came and threw his seed and watered and did not turn the soil until the second sprouted, then if the sprouting crop, if turned, would sprout again, the answer is as we said; and if it would not sprout again, then what sprouted belongs to the usurper, and the usurper guarantees for the owner the value of his crop as sprouting. In al-Zahiriyyah: Nasir was asked about one who planted his own land with wheat, and a man came and planted it with barley; he said: upon the owner of the barley is the value of his seed as sown; that was narrated by Muhammad ibn Sama'ah from Muhammad ibn al-Husayn. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This is if the owner of the seed consented; but if he did not consent, he has a choice: if he wishes, he leaves it until it sprouts, then takes it by uprooting; and if he wishes, he absolves him from guarantee. When the crop ripens and they harvest it, it is between them according to their shares.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the scenario where someone plants seeds in another's land without permission (ghasp of the land itself or the planting right). The text begins with the case where the usurper plants seeds in his own land? Actually it says 'يكون للثاني عند أبي حنيفة' which refers to a previous scenario: the first farmer (owner) had planted, then the usurper plants again. Abu Hanifa rules that the second (usurper) gets the yield? But then it details evaluation. The key principle: the produce belongs to the owner of the land, and the usurper is compensated for his seed but valued as if it were sown in the usurper's own land (i.e., with the land's value included), then the difference is given. If the owner replants and both grow, the owner gets all produce but must compensate the usurper for his seed. The 'comprehensive answer' clarifies: the usurper pays the owner the value of the owner's seed as sown in the owner's land (high value), and the owner pays the usurper the value of both seeds but as sown in another's land (lower value). If the owner's crop had already sprouted and the usurper plants later without tilling, if the owner's crop can regrow after being turned, the same rule applies; if not (crop cannot regrow), the usurper gets the produce but must compensate the owner for the value of the owner's crop as sprouting. Then a specific fatwa: if the owner planted wheat and another planted barley, the barley planter pays the value of his seed as sown. Abu al-Layth adds: if the seed owner (the barley planter) consents, it's fine; otherwise, the landowner can either wait and uproot the barley or forgive the guarantee. When harvested, they share according to their respective shares.

Key Terms
بذر (badhr): Seeds used for planting.
مبذوراً (mabdhuuran): Sown or planted in the ground, used to describe the state of the seed when evaluating its value.
قلع (qal'): Uprooting the crop from the ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the landowner replants his seeds after the usurper planted, and both seeds grow, to whom does the entire yield belong according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The usurper exclusively
B) The landowner exclusively
C) They share equally
D) It depends on who watered first

Key Takeaway
The landowner ultimately owns all crops grown on his land, but must compensate the usurper for seed value based on whether the seed is considered sown in his own or another's land.

8 min
Liability for Dependents Eating from Sharecropped Vineyard
Fatwa on liability for consuming sharecropped fruit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the giver's liability when his household consumes sharecropped fruit with or without permission.

Core Idea
Permission alone does not shift liability; the giver guarantees only for dependents.

A giver of a vineyard on sharecropping (mu'amalah) may find his family eating from the produce. The fatwa clarifies when the giver becomes liable for the worker's share—only if he permitted consumption by someone whose maintenance is his duty. This lesson unpacks the conditions for liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
The giver (dafi') and the worker (amil) in a sharecropping contract. The giver's household includes those he maintains.
The worker's share is at stake.
2
Determine permission
Did the household eat with or without the giver's permission? The ruling distinguishes both scenarios.
Without permission: the eaters are liable, not the giver.
3
Check dependency
If permission was given, is the eater a dependent (man tajibu nafaqatuhu)? Only then does the giver become liable.
Dependency triggers respondeat superior logic.
4
Apply the liability rule
If dependent and permission: giver liable for worker's share. If non-dependent or no permission: giver not liable.
This protects the worker's rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل أبو جعفر عمن دفع كرماً معاملة فأثمر الكرم أو كان الدافع وأهل داره يدخلون الكرم ويأكلون منه ويحملون والعامل لا يدخل إلا قليلاً هل على الدافع ضمان؟ قال : إن أكلوا وحملوا بغير إذن الدافع فلا ضمان عليه والضمان على الذين أكلوا وحملوا وإن كانوا أكلوا بإذنه فإن كانوا ممن تجب نفقتهم عليه فهو ضامن نصيب العامل فصار كأنه هو الذي أكله وإن كانوا أخذوا بإذنه وهو ممن لا تلزمه فلا ضمان عليه فصار كأنه دل على استهلاك مال الغير وسئل الشيخ عطاء بن حمزة رحمه الله تعالى عمن زرع أرض إنسان ببذر نفسه بغير إذن صاحب الأرض هل لصاحب الأرض أن يطالب بحصة الأرض؟ قال : نعم إن جرى الغرف في ذلك أنهم يزرعون الأرض بثلث الخارج أو ربعه أو نصفه أو شيء مقدر شائع يجب ذلك القدر الذي جرى به العرف. قيل له : هل فيه رواية؟ قال : نعم رجل غصب أرضاً وبنى فيها حائطاً فجاء صاحب الأرض وأخذ الأرض وأراد الغاصب أن يأخذ الحائط ؛ فإن كان الغاصب يبني الحائط من تراب هذه الأرض ليس له النقض ويكون الحائط لصاحب الأرض، فإن بنى الحائط لا من تراب هذه الأرض فله النقض.
Abu Ja'far was asked about one who gave a vineyard on a sharecropping basis (mu'amalah) and the vineyard bore fruit, or the giver and his household entered the vineyard and ate from it and carried away, while the worker rarely entered. Is there liability on the giver? He said: If they ate and carried without the giver's permission, there is no liability on him, and liability is on those who ate and carried. If they ate with his permission, then if they are those whose maintenance is obligatory upon him, he is liable for the worker's share, so it is as if he himself ate it. If they took with his permission and he is not obligated to maintain them, then there is no liability on him, so it is as if he led to the consumption of another's property. And Shaykh 'Ata' ibn Hamzah was asked about one who planted a person's land with his own seed without the landowner's permission: does the landowner have the right to demand a share of the land? He said: Yes, if the custom ('urf) in that regard is that they plant the land for one-third of the produce, or one-fourth, or half, or a specified common share, then that amount which is customary is obligatory. It was said to him: Is there a narration on this? He said: Yes, a man usurped land and built a wall on it; then the landowner came and took the land, and the usurper wanted to take the wall. If the usurper built the wall from the soil of that land, he has no right to dismantle it, and the wall belongs to the landowner. If he built the wall not from the soil of that land, then he has the right to dismantle it.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct fatwas related to ghasp. First, about a vineyard given on mu'amalah (sharecropping): if the giver's household eats fruit without permission, they are liable; if with permission, the giver is liable only if he is obligated to maintain them (e.g., dependents), otherwise not. Second, if someone plants another's land without permission, the landowner can demand a customary share (e.g., one-third) as rent, based on prevailing custom ('urf). Third, if a usurper builds a wall on usurped land using the land's own soil, the wall becomes the landowner's without compensation; if he uses foreign soil, he may dismantle and take it. These rulings demonstrate Hanafi reliance on custom ('urf) and causation (tasabbub) in liability.

Key Terms
معاملة (mu'amalah): A sharecropping contract where one party provides land and the other labor and/or seeds, sharing the produce.
ضمان (daman): Liability, financial responsibility to compensate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ja'far's fatwa, if the giver's adult child (non-dependent) eats from the sharecropped vineyard with the giver's permission, who is liable for the worker's share?
A) The child only
B) The giver only
C) No one, because permission was given
D) Both share equally

Key Takeaway
In sharecropping, the giver is not liable for others' consumption unless the consumer is his dependent and he permitted it.

Customary Rent for Unauthorized Cultivation
Fatwa on obligatory customary rent for unauthorized cultivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a landowner can demand a customary share when someone cultivates his land without permission.

Core Idea
The cultivator must pay the landowner the customary share, even without a contract, because custom ('urf) determines the rent.

When someone plants another's land without permission, the landowner is not left empty-handed. The fatwa of Shaykh 'Ata' ibn Hamzah establishes that the prevailing custom in that area determines the rent due. This lesson explores the reliance on 'urf as a source of obligation in mu'amalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
The planter (zarri') uses the landowner's (sahib al-ard) land without permission, with his own seed.
No contract existed.
2
Determine the landowner's right
The landowner may demand a share of the produce. The right is based on the land's value.
The share is not arbitrary.
3
Refer to custom ('urf)
If there is a prevailing custom in that region for such lands (e.g., rent is one-third, one-fourth), that amount becomes obligatory.
Custom varies by locality.
4
Confirm the evidence
The shaykh cites a narration about the usurper's wall to support the principle that without agreement, the landowner gets something.
The wall analogy shows that benefit follows the land.

Source Text

Building on Usurped Land: Soil Origin Determines Ownership
Fatwa on the right to dismantle a wall built on usurped land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state whether a usurper can dismantle a wall built on usurped land, based on the soil used.

Core Idea
If built with the land's own soil, the structure belongs to the landowner; if with foreign soil, the builder may dismantle.

A usurper builds a wall on someone else's land. When the landowner reclaims the land, the builder wants his wall back. The fatwa provides a clear rule based on the origin of the construction material. This lesson explores the Hanafi approach to accession and compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the structure
A usurper (ghasib) builds a wall on usurped land. The landowner (sahib al-ard) takes back the land. The usurper wants to remove the wall.
The wall is a structure attached to the land.
2
Examine the construction material
Was the wall built from the soil of the usurped land itself, or from foreign soil brought from elsewhere?
This is the decisive factor.
3
Apply the rule
If built with the land's own soil: the usurper has no right to dismantle; the wall becomes the landowner's property. If built with foreign soil: the usurper may dismantle and take the material.
The rule prevents the landowner from losing the value added by the land's own material.
4
Consider the rationale
When using the land's soil, the material is essentially part of the land, so the structure merges. When using foreign soil, the builder retains ownership of the material.
This is analogous to the rule of accession (tab'iyyah) in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

نقض (naqd): Dismantling, taking apart a structure.

Key Takeaway

Liability for Spontaneous Defects in Usurped Property
Ruling on defects in usurped property without the usurper's action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on defects that occur in usurped property without the usurper's action and apply the method of compensation.

Core Idea
The usurper is liable for any defect occurring during his possession, even if he did not cause it.

When property is wrongfully possessed (ghasb), the usurper bears full responsibility for its safety. This lesson examines a specific scenario: defects that arise spontaneously—such as a slave running away, falling ill, or becoming blind—without any action by the usurper. Al-Quduri's ruling clarifies that the usurper must compensate for the decrease in value, and the compensation method ensures fairness. Understanding this principle is essential for distinguishing liability in Islamic property law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is usurpation (ghasb) of a slave. The ruling: the usurper is liable (daman) for any defect that reduces value, even if he did not cause it. The defect occurs 'in his hand' (yad).
The key term is 'madhmun' (liable/guaranteed).
2
List the examples of defects given
The text mentions: running away (abaq), zina (adultery/fornication), theft, blindness, paralysis, fever, whiteness in the eye. These occur spontaneously or through third parties, not by the usurper's act.
Also includes similar defects in a deposit (wadi'ah) and unauthorized use (e.g. grinding, cooking).
3
Understand the compensation method
The slave is valued in two states: sound (without defect) and defective. The owner takes the defective slave plus the difference in value (arsh). This compensates the owner for the loss caused by the defect.
The valuation is at the time of return.
4
Analyze the scenario of a temporary defect
If the usurper compensates for a defect (like fever or whiteness) that later disappears, the usurper can reclaim the compensation from the owner. Reason: the defect no longer exists, so retaining the arsh would be unjust enrichment.
This shows the principle of fairness in compensation.
5
Confirm the scope of the ruling
The same ruling applies to deposits (wadi'ah) and to unauthorized use (e.g., grinding without permission). The key is that the property is in one's possession (yad) without rightful ownership or consent, creating liability.
This is based on the maxim: 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' (liability accompanies benefit), though here it is strict liability due to wrongful possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القدوري رحمه الله تعالى في كتابه: غصب من آخر عبداً أو جارية فأبق في يد الغاصب ولم يكن أبق قبل ذلك أو زنت أو سرقت ولم تكن فعلت ذلك قبل فعلي الغاصب ما انتصت بسبب السرقة والإباق وعيب الزنا، وكذلك ما حدث في يد الغاصب مما تنقص به القيمة من عور أو شلل في المغصوب والوديعة وربح وملك بلا حل انتفاع قبل أداء الضمان بطحن وطبخ أو ما أشبه ذلك يكون مضموناً فيقوم العبد صحيحاً ويقوم وبه العيب فيأخذه ويرجع بفضل ما بينهما. وإن أصابه حمى في يد الغاصب أو أصابه بياض في عينه ثم رد على المولى ورد معه الأرش ثم ذهبت الحمى وزال البياض فللغاصب أن يرجع على المولى بالأرش.
Al-Quduri said in his book: If someone usurps another's male or female slave, and the slave runs away while in the usurper's hand and had not run away before, or commits zina or theft and had not done that before, then upon the usurper is what diminished due to theft, running away, and the defect of zina; similarly, whatever occurs in the usurper's hand that decreases the value, such as blindness or paralysis in the usurped property or deposit, or profit, or ownership without permission to benefit before paying the guarantee, by grinding or cooking or the like, is guaranteed. So the slave is appraised sound and appraised with the defect, and he (the owner) takes him and recovers the difference. And if a fever struck him in the usurper's hand or whiteness appeared in his eye, then he returned him to the owner together with the compensation (arsh), then the fever went away and the whiteness disappeared, the usurper may recover the arsh from the owner.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage quotes al-Quduri on liability for defects that occur in usurped property without any action by the usurper (i.e., spontaneously). Even though the usurper did not cause the defect, he is liable for the decrease in value because the property is in his possession (yad). The same applies to a deposit (wadi'ah) and to unauthorized use of another's property before compensation. The method: the slave is valued in sound condition and with the defect; the owner takes the slave plus the difference. If the usurper paid compensation for a defect that later disappears, he can reclaim it from the owner, as the defect no longer exists and compensation would be unjust enrichment.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation for a defect in a commodity, typically the difference in value between sound and defective.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage.
يد ضمان (yad daman): Possession that carries full liability for the property, as in ghasb (usurpation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurped slave becomes blind in the usurper's hand without any act by the usurper, what is the ruling according to Al-Quduri?
A) The usurper is not liable because he did not cause the blindness.
B) The usurper must pay the full value of the slave.
C) The slave is appraised sound and with defect; the owner takes the slave plus the difference.
D) The usurper must replace the slave with a sound one.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the usurper is strictly liable for any defect that reduces the value of the usurped property while it is in his possession, regardless of causation.

8 min
Profits (Ghullah) from Usurped Property Must Be Given in Charity
States ruling that profits from usurped property, like rent, must be given in charity by the usurper

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profits from usurped property, explain the ta'lil (legal reason) using the concept of manafi', and apply the analogy to similar cases.

Core Idea
The usurper owns the profits through his contract, but must give them in charity because the source is unlawful.

This lesson addresses a key principle in Hanafi law: when someone wrongfully takes property and derives income from it (like rent), that income does not revert to the owner but belongs to the usurper by virtue of the contract he made. However, because the underlying possession is sinful, the usurper cannot lawfully keep the profit; he must purify it by giving it in charity. This ruling extends to profits from selling the usurped property as well.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The usurper has profited from usurped property (e.g., rent from a slave). The ruling is that he must give that profit in charity (sadaqah).
This is a wajib upon the usurper.
2
Recognize the legal reason (ta'lil)
Benefits (manafi') are not owned by the owner of the property; they come into existence only through a contract. The usurper contracted the hire, so the benefits belong to him.
This distinguishes the profit from the property itself, which must be returned.
3
Understand the analogy
The same ruling applies if the usurper sells the property and makes a profit – that profit must also be given in charity.
Both cases involve the usurper using the property to generate income through his own contract.
4
Note the consequences
The usurper must also compensate the owner for any diminution in the property (e.g., wear from use) and still give the ghullah in charity.
Charity does not absolve him from liability for damage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن استغله تصدق بالغلة كما لو تصرف في المغصوب والوديعة وربح) أي استغل المغصوب بأن كان عبداً مثلاً فأجره فنقصه الاستعمال وضمن النقصان تصدق الغاصب بالغلة كما يتصدق بالربح فيما إذا تصرف في المغصوب أو الوديعة بأن باعه وربح فيه لأن المنافع لا تقوم إلا بالعقد والعاقد وهو الغاصب فتكون الأجرة له بخلاف ما إذا
He (the author) said: 'And if he derives profit (ghullah) from it, he should give the ghullah in charity, just as if he disposed of the usurped property or deposit and made a profit.' That is, he derives ghullah from the usurped property, for example by renting out a slave, which diminishes the slave through use, and he compensates for the diminution. The usurper gives the ghullah in charity, just as he gives the profit in charity when he disposes of the usurped property or deposit by selling it and making a profit. For benefits (manafi') are not established except by a contract, and the contracting party is the usurper, so the hire belongs to him, unlike the case where...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on profits (ghullah) generated from usurped property. Unlike the property itself, the benefits (manafi') are not considered owned by the original owner; rather, they become the property of the usurper by virtue of his contract (e.g., hire). However, since the usurper wrongfully possessed the property, he cannot keep the profits lawfully. Therefore, he must give them in charity (sadaqah) to rid himself of the unlawful gain. This is analogous to the case where someone sells usurped property or a deposit and makes a profit: that profit must also be given in charity. The reason (ta'lil) is that benefits are not realized except through a contract, and the contracting party is the usurper, so the hire income belongs to him (ownership), but since the origin is unlawful, he must dispose of it by charity.

Key Terms
غلة (ghullah): Periodic profit or yield from property, such as rent from a slave or land.
منافع (manafi'): Usufruct or benefits derived from an asset, considered a separate property in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurper rents out a usurped slave and receives rent, what must he do with the rent according to the Hanafi school?
A) Return it to the owner
B) Keep it
C) Give it in charity
D) Destroy it

Key Takeaway
Profits (ghullah) from usurped property belong to the usurper by contract but must be given in charity to purify the unlawful gain.

The Obligation to Give Charity from Profit of Usurped Property
Commentary on the matn requiring charity from profit of usurped property, with differences from Abu Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit from usurped property, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the rule to a new scenario.

Core Idea
The profit from usurped property must be given in charity — Abu Yusuf dissents, but the school follows the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines the ruling on profits (ghallah) generated from property that has been wrongfully taken (ghasb). The key question: does the usurper own the profit, or is it tainted and must be purified? The majority Hanafi view requires charity, while Abu Yusuf holds a different opinion. We will unpack the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the primary ruling
The act is using usurped property to generate profit (ghallah). The ruling is that the usurper must give that profit in charity.
The text states: 'تصدق بالغلة'.
2
Recognize the intra-madhhab difference
Note that Abu Yusuf holds that charity is not required, and the usurper may keep the profit.
The text says: 'وعند أبي يوسف لا يتصدق به'.
3
Understand the reasoning for charity
The profit is considered tainted (khabith) because it arises from unlawful possession; charity purifies it.
The author explains that the profit arises from a contract made by the usurper, but the underlying possession is unlawful.
4
Compare with the owner's rights
The owner is entitled to the property itself and compensation for decrease, but the profit belongs to the usurper in a contractual sense yet is tainted.
This distinction is important for subsequent cases.
5
Apply to a new scenario
If someone usurps a car and rents it out, what must he do with the rental income? He must give it in charity according to the mufta bihi view.
This illustrates the practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن استغله تصدق بالغلة كما لو تصرف في المغصوب والوديعة وربح) أي استغل المغصوب بأن كان عبداً مثلاً فأجره فنقصه الاستعمال وضمن النقصان تصدق الغاصب بالغلة كما يتصدق بالربح فيما إذا تصرف في المغصوب أو الوديعة بأن باعه وربح فيه لأن المنافع لا تقوم إلا بالعقد والعاقد وهو الغاصب فتكون الأجرة له بخلاف ما إذا رده غصب جارية وغصبها ووطئها الزوج فالعقر للمالك دون الغاصب لأن العقر يجب باستيفاء منفعة البضع عند قيام الشبهة لا بالعقد. أما الأول وهو الاستعمال فالمذكور هنا قولهما وهو التصدق، وعند أبي يوسف لا يتصدق به وقد ذكرنا الوجه في الجانبين في المسألة التي قبلها . وكان ينبغي أن يتصدق بما زاد على ما ضمن عندهما لا بالغلة كلها كما في المسألة الأولى، ثم إنما يضمن الغاصب النقصان إذا كان النقصان في العين وكان غير زيوف لأنه دخل جميع أجزائه في ضمانه فيجب عليه قيمة ما تعذر رده ما أجزائه كلاً أو بعضاً بخلاف المبيع حيث النقصان الحادث فيه قبل القبض إلا بالخيار ولا يوجب شيء من الثمن لأن الأوصاف لا تضمن بالعقد وتضمن بالفعل، وإن كان لتراجع السعر لا يضمن بعد أن في مكان الغصب لأن ذلك لقلة الرغبات فيه لا لنقصان في العين بفوات جزء. وإن كان ربوياً لا يمكنه أن يضمنه النقصان استرداد العين لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا إذا لجودة لا قيمة لها في الأموال الربوية ولكنه يخير بين أن يأخذه ولا شيء له وبين أن يتركه على الغاصب من الربويات أو قيمته. ولك أن تقول : عدم إمكان ذلك مسلم فيما إذا كان نقصان الربويات في الأوصاف كما إذا غصب حنطة فعفنت في يده لأنه لا اعتبار للوصف عندنا، وأما إذا كان نقصانها في الأجزاء كما إذا غصب كيلياً أو وزنياً فتلف بعض أجزائه فنقص فرده كيلاً أو وزناً فيكون لصاحب المال تضمين النقصان مع استراد الباقي ولا يؤدي إلى الربا كما لا يخفى. وفي العناية فسر الربويات بما إذا غصب حنطة فعفنت عنده أو إناء فضة فإنهشم في يده أقول : في كون إناء الفضة من الربويات عندنا فيه نظر ظاهر فإنهم صرحوا في شرح الهداية ومنهم صاحب العناية بأن الوزني الذي في تبعيضه ضرر كالمصوغ من القمقم والذث ليس هو بمثلي بل هو من ذوات القيم، ولا شك أن إناء الفضة منه فكيف مثل به؟ ولاستغلال العبد المستعار بالايجار كاستغلال المغصوب حتى يجب عليه ضمان النقصان ويتصدق بالغلة عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف والوجه قد بيناه. ولو هلك في يده بعدما استعمله فضمنه المالك كان له أن يستعين بالغلة في أداء الضمان لأن الخبث لأجل المالك، فإذا أخذه المالك لا يظهر الخبث في حقه ولهذا لو أسلم الغلة إليه مع العبد يباح له التناول فيزول الخبث بالتسليم وتبرأ ذمته عن القيمة بقدره بخلاف ما إذا باعه الغاصب بعدما استغله وهلك في يد المشتري وضمنه المالك قيمته ثم رجع المشتري على الغاصب بالثمن حيث لا يكون للغاصب أن يستعين بالغلة في أداء الثمن إلى المشتري لأن الخبث كان لحق المالك والمشتري ليس بمالك فلا يزول الخبث بالأداء إليه فلا يؤديه إليه إلا إذا كان لا يجد غيره فيرجح هو على غيره من الفقراء باعتبار أنه ملكه وهو محتاج إليه كما أن للملتقط أن يصرف الغلة على نفسه إذا كان محتاجاً، ثم إذا أصاب مالاً يتصدق بمثله إن كان غنياً وتعذر الاستغلال، وإن كان فقيراً فلا شيء عليه لما ذكرنا من ترجيحه على غيره من الفقهاء.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he uses it, he gives its profit in charity, just as if he disposed of the usurped property or deposit and made a profit). Meaning, he used the usurped property, for example a slave, and rented him out, and the use caused a decrease in value, and he guaranteed the decrease, the usurper gives the profit in charity, just as he gives the profit in charity if he disposed of the usurped property or deposit by selling it and making a profit from it, because benefits are not obtained except by contract, and the contractor is the usurper, so the rental becomes his, unlike if he returns it. Usurpation of a slave-girl, and her usurpation, and the husband has intercourse with her: the dower is for the owner, not the usurper, because the dower becomes obligatory by the enjoyment of the benefit of the vulva at the time of a doubt, not by contract. As for the first, which is use, what is mentioned here is their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) view, which is charity; according to Abu Yusuf he does not give charity with it, and we have mentioned the reasoning for both sides in the preceding issue. And it would be fitting that he gives charity with what exceeds what he guaranteed according to them (the two), not the entire profit, as in the first issue. Then the usurper only guarantees the decrease if the decrease is in the substance and it is not counterfeit, because all its parts entered his guarantee, so he must pay the value of what could not be returned, of its parts in whole or in part, unlike a sale, where a defect arising before delivery does not, except in cases of option, obligate any part of the price, because attributes are not guaranteed by contract but are guaranteed by action. If it is due to a decline in market price, he does not guarantee after that in the place of usurpation, because that is due to a lack of demand, not a decrease in the substance due to loss of a part. If it is a ribawi (usurious) item, he cannot guarantee the decrease while retaining the substance, because it leads to riba if for quality, which has no value in ribawi commodities; but he has a choice between taking it and having nothing, or leaving it with the usurper as a ribawi item or its value. You may say: The impossibility of that is accepted when the decrease in ribawi items is in attributes, such as if he usurped wheat and it rotted in his hand, because we do not consider attributes; but if the decrease is in parts, such as if he usurped a measured or weighed item and some of its parts were lost, so the return is by measure or weight, then the owner may claim compensation for the decrease along with taking back the remainder, and it does not lead to riba, as is not hidden. In al-Inayah, he interpreted ribawi items as when he usurped wheat and it rotted with him, or a silver vessel and it broke in his hand. I say: Regarding the silver vessel being among ribawi items according to us, there is apparent consideration, for they stated in Sharh al-Hidayah, including the author of al-Inayah, that a weighed item whose division causes harm, like a vessel made of a pitcher or a pot, is not a mithli but is among items of value; there is no doubt that a silver vessel is from that category, so how could he give an example with it? And the use of a borrowed slave by renting is like the use of usurped property, so he must guarantee the decrease and give charity with the profit according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, and we have explained the reasoning. If it perishes in his hand after he has used it, and the owner holds him liable for its value, he may use the profit to pay the compensation, because the unlawfulness is for the sake of the owner, so when the owner takes it, the unlawfulness does not appear in his regard; therefore, if he gives the profit to him along with the slave, it becomes permissible for him to take it, and the unlawfulness is removed by delivery, and his liability is discharged to the extent of it, unlike if the usurper sold it after using it, and it perished in the hand of the buyer, and the owner held him liable for its value, then the buyer recovers the price from the usurper, in which case the usurper may not use the profit to pay the price to the buyer, because the unlawfulness was for the right of the owner, and the buyer is not an owner; so the unlawfulness is not removed by payment to him; so he does not pay it to him unless he finds no other means, then he gives it priority over other poor people because it is his property and he is in need of it, just as a finder may spend the profit on himself if he is needy. Then if he acquires wealth, he gives charity of its equivalent if he is rich and cannot use it; if he is poor, then nothing is due from him, due to the preference we mentioned over other jurists.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that if a usurper extracts profit from the usurped property (e.g., by renting a slave), he must give that profit in charity. The author explains that the profit is considered the usurper's because the contract of hire is with him, but since the property is unlawfully held, the profit is tainted (khabith) and must be purified by charity. However, Abu Yusuf disagrees and holds that the usurper may keep the profit, though the relied-upon view (qawlahuma) is that he must give charity. The text also distinguishes between the case where the usurper returns the property after use: he must compensate for any decrease caused, but if the owner later takes the property and the usurper used the profit to pay compensation, that is acceptable because the taint is removed when paid to the owner. The passage further discusses scenarios involving ribawi items (commodities subject to usury rules). If a usurped ribawi item decreases in quality (e.g., wheat rots), the usurper cannot simply pay the decrease and keep the item, as that would be usury. Instead, the owner has a choice: take the item as is with no compensation, or leave it with the usurper and claim its value. However, if the decrease is in quantity (e.g., part of a measured item is lost), the owner can take the remainder and claim compensation for the missing part. The author critiques the example given in al-Inayah (silver vessel) because silver vessels are not mithli (fungible) but qimi (valuable), so they are not subject to riba rulings in the same way. Finally, the text addresses cases where the usurper sells the property and it perishes with the buyer: the usurper cannot use the profit to pay the buyer's claim because the buyer is not the owner; only the owner can receive the profit lawfully. This passage is a detailed commentary explaining the underlying principles and subtle distinctions.

Key Terms
غلة (ghallah): Profit or income generated from property, such as rent from a slave or crops from land.
ربوي (ribawi): A commodity subject to the rules of riba, meaning it can only be exchanged in equal measures and immediate delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Abu Yusuf regarding profit from usurped property?
A) He must give it in charity.
B) He may keep it.
C) He must return it to the owner.
D) He must destroy it.

Key Takeaway
The profit from usurped property must be given in charity according to the majority Hanafi view.

Special Scenarios: Ribawi Goods and Compensation
Commentary on the matn requiring charity from profit of usurped property, with differences from Abu Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on profit from usurped property to ribawi items and determine the validity of using profit for compensation.

Core Idea
For ribawi items, quality decrease cannot be compensated without causing riba; a choice is given. Profit can be used to pay compensation to the owner.

This lesson examines two special scenarios: when the usurped property is a ribawi commodity, and when the usurper uses the profit to pay compensation for decrease in value. The text provides detailed distinctions and a critique of a previous commentator's example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Usurped property is ribawi (e.g., wheat, silver vessel). A decrease occurs in quality or quantity.
The text discusses both cases.
2
Distinguish quality vs quantity decrease
If quality decreases (e.g., rotting), the usurper cannot simply keep the item and pay the difference because that would be riba. Instead, the owner has a choice.
The owner can either take the item as is with no compensation, or leave it with the usurper and claim full value.
3
Analyze the critique
The author criticizes al-Inayah for using a silver vessel as an example of ribawi, because silver vessels are not mithli but qimi.
This shows the importance of proper classification.
4
Use of profit for compensation
If the property perishes after use, the usurper may use the profit to pay compensation to the owner, because the taint is removed when paid to the owner.
Contrast with paying a buyer: taint remains because buyer is not owner.

وتبرأ ذمته عن القيمة بقدره بخلاف ما إذا باعه الغاصب بعدما استغله وهلك في يد المشتري وضمنه المالك قيمته ثم رجع المشتري على الغاصب بالثمن حيث لا يكون للغاصب أن يستعين بالغلة في أداء الثمن إلى المشتري لأن الخبث كان لحق المالك والمشتري ليس بمالك فلا يزول الخبث بالأداء إليه فلا يؤديه إليه إلا إذا كان لا يجد غيره فيرجح هو على غيره من الفقراء باعتبار أنه ملكه وهو محتاج إليه كما أن للملتقط أن يصرف الغلة على نفسه إذا كان محتاجاً، ثم إذا أصاب مالاً يتصدق بمثله إن كان غنياً وتعذر الاستغلال، وإن كان فقيراً فلا شيء عليه لما ذكرنا من ترجيحه على غيره من الفقهاء.

Meaning, he used the usurped property, for example a slave, and rented him out, and the use caused a decrease in value, and he guaranteed the decrease, the usurper gives the profit in charity, just as he gives the profit in charity if he disposed of the usurped property or deposit by selling it and making a profit from it, because benefits are not obtained except by contract, and the contractor is the usurper, so the rental becomes his, unlike if he returns it. I say: Regarding the silver vessel being among ribawi items according to us, there is apparent consideration, for they stated in Sharh al-Hidayah, including the author of al-Inayah, that a weighed item whose division causes harm, like a vessel made of a pitcher or a pot, is not a mithli but is among items of value; there is no doubt that a silver vessel is from that category, so how could he give an example with it? And the use of a borrowed slave by renting is like the use of usurped property, so he must guarantee the decrease and give charity with the profit according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, and we have explained the reasoning. If it perishes in his hand after he has used it, and the owner holds him liable for its value, he may use the profit to pay the compensation, because the unlawfulness is for the sake of the owner, so when the owner takes it, the unlawfulness does not appear in his regard; therefore, if he gives the profit to him along with the slave, it becomes permissible for him to take it, and the unlawfulness is removed by delivery, and his liability is discharged to the extent of it, unlike if the usurper sold it after using it, and it perished in the hand of the buyer, and the owner held him liable for its value, then the buyer recovers the price from the usurper, in which case the usurper may not use the profit to pay the price to the buyer, because the unlawfulness was for the right of the owner, and the buyer is not an owner; so the unlawfulness is not removed by payment to him; so he does not pay it to him unless he finds no other means, then he gives it priority over other poor people because it is his property and he is in need of it, just as a finder may spend the profit on himself if he is needy. Then if he acquires wealth, he gives charity of its equivalent if he is rich and cannot use it; if he is poor, then nothing is due from him, due to the preference we mentioned over other jurists.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that if a usurper extracts profit from the usurped property (e.g., by renting a slave), he must give that profit in charity. The author explains that the profit is considered the usurper's because the contract of hire is with him, but since the property is unlawfully held, the profit is tainted (khabith) and must be purified by charity. However, Abu Yusuf disagrees and holds that the usurper may keep the profit, though the relied-upon view (qawlahuma) is that he must give charity. The text also distinguishes between the case where the usurper returns the property after use: he must compensate for any decrease caused, but if the owner later takes the property and the usurper used the profit to pay compensation, that is acceptable because the taint is removed when paid to the owner. The passage further discusses scenarios involving ribawi items (commodities subject to usury rules). If a usurped ribawi item decreases in quality (e.g., wheat rots), the usurper cannot simply pay the decrease and keep the item, as that would be usury. Instead, the owner has a choice: take the item as is with no compensation, or leave it with the usurper and claim its value. However, if the decrease is in quantity (e.g., part of a measured item is lost), the owner can take the remainder and claim compensation for the missing part. The author critiques the example given in al-Inayah (silver vessel) because silver vessels are not mithli (fungible) but qimi (valuable), so they are not subject to riba rulings in the same way. Finally, the text addresses cases where the usurper sells the property and it perishes with the buyer: the usurper cannot use the profit to pay the buyer's claim because the buyer is not the owner; only the owner can receive the profit lawfully. This passage is a detailed commentary explaining the underlying principles and subtle distinctions.

Key Terms
غلة (ghallah): Profit or income generated from property, such as rent from a slave or crops from land.
ربوي (ribawi): A commodity subject to the rules of riba, meaning it can only be exchanged in equal measures and immediate delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what option does an owner have if a ribawi item decreases in quality (e.g., wheat rots)?
A) He can take the wheat and claim compensation.
B) He can take the wheat without compensation or leave it and claim value.
C) He must return the wheat and demand full replacement.
D) He must accept the loss.

Key Takeaway
For ribawi items, quality decrease cannot be separately compensated; the owner chooses between taking the item as is or claiming full value.

Who Gets the Bride-Price (ʿUqr) for a Usurped Slave-Girl?
States the ruling on who gets the bride-price when a usurped slave-girl is married

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on who receives the ʿuqr when a usurped slave-girl is married, and explain the legal cause (ʿilla) for that ruling.

Core Idea
The ʿuqr belongs to the owner because it is compensation for using his property, not the usurper's.

This lesson examines a specific case of property rights and compensation in marriage. When a usurped slave-girl is married by the usurper and the husband has intercourse, a compensation called ʿuqr is due. The text clarifies that this compensation goes to the rightful owner, not the usurper, based on the principle that ʿuqr is tied to the enjoyment of the usufruct, not the marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the elements of the case
Note the key actors: the usurper (who wrongfully took the slave-girl), the husband (who married her via the usurper's arrangement), and the owner (who holds the property right). Also note the act: intercourse occurred under a semblance of right (shubha).
The marriage is contracted by the usurper without the owner's permission.
2
State the ruling
The text explicitly states that the ʿuqr (bride-price) belongs to the owner, not the usurper. This is the core ruling to memorize.
The ʿuqr is wajib upon the husband to the owner.
3
Locate the legal cause (ʿilla)
The text gives the reason: 'because the bride-price is obligatory by the enjoyment of the vulva's usufruct when there is a shubha, not by contract.' The ʿilla is the actual enjoyment of the usufruct (istīfāʾ manfaʿat al-baḍʿ) under shubha.
Shubha here means the semblance of a valid marriage, but the contract is defective due to lack of ownership/authority.
4
Explain why the usurper does not receive it
The usurper never owned the usufruct; it belongs to the owner. The ʿuqr is tied to the benefit of the vulva, which is a property right of the owner. Therefore, the compensation goes to the party whose property was enjoyed.
This distinguishes ʿuqr from mahr (dower), which is due by contract regardless of consummation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غصب جارية وغصبها ووطئها الزوج فالعقر للمالك دون الغاصب لأن العقر يجب باستيفاء منفعة البضع عند قيام الشبهة لا بالعقد.
If he usurped a slave-girl and [the usurper] married her and the husband had intercourse [with her], then the bride-price (ʿuqr) belongs to the owner, not the usurper, because the bride-price is obligatory by the enjoyment of the vulva's usufruct when there is a semblance (shubha), not by contract.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn. It clarifies that when a usurped slave-girl is given in marriage by the usurper and the husband has intercourse, the bride-price (ʿuqr) – the compensation for the usufruct of the vulva – is the right of the owner of the slave-girl, not the usurper. The reason is that ʿuqr is due for the actual enjoyment of the usufruct under a semblance of right (shubha), such as when a marriage is contracted by one who has no authority. It is not due simply by the marriage contract. Therefore, the owner, whose property was wrongfully used, is entitled to this compensation.

Key Terms
عقر (ʿuqr): Compensation for the usufruct of the vulva (manfa'at al-bad') when intercourse occurs under a semblance of right (shubha).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a usurped slave-girl married by the usurper, who receives the bride-price (ʿuqr) if the husband has intercourse?
A) The usurper
B) The owner of the slave-girl
C) The husband
D) The state treasury

Key Takeaway
The bride-price (ʿuqr) for a usurped slave-girl belongs to the owner because it is compensation for the enjoyment of the usufruct, not for the marriage contract.

Rulings on Usufruct and Decrease in Value of Usurped Property
Intra-Hanafi dispute on usufruct charity and rules for decrease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the usufruct of usurped property, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the rules for liability for decrease.

Core Idea
The usurper must give the usufruct in charity according to the two; Abu Yusuf disagrees; decrease must be compensated if in substance.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the rulings of usurped property (ghasb). It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf regarding the usufruct. It also explains when the usurper is liable for a decrease in value and how the usufruct may be used to discharge the guarantee. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the law of ghasb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the usurper must give the usufruct in charity. Abu Yusuf holds he does not.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
Note the preferred view
The text indicates that the view of the two is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The text says 'المذكور هنا قولهما'.
3
Understand liability for decrease
The usurper is liable for decrease only if it is in the substance (ayn), not for market fluctuations.
Decrease due to loss of parts or change in substance must be compensated.
4
Apply the special rule for ribawi goods
For ribawi items, if the decrease is in quality, the owner cannot claim compensation; if in quantity (parts), he can.
Quality has no value in ribawi items according to Hanafis.
5
Recognize the use of usufruct to pay guarantee
If the property perishes after use, the usurper may use the usufruct to pay the owner's guarantee. But if he sold it, he cannot use it to repay the buyer.
The taint (khubth) is only removed when the owner receives it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول وهو الاستعمال فالمذكور هنا قولهما وهو التصدق، وعند أبي يوسف لا يتصدق به وقد ذكرنا الوجه في الجانبين في المسألة التي قبلها . وكان ينبغي أن يتصدق بما زاد على ما ضمن عندهما لا بالغلة كلها كما في المسألة الأولى، ثم إنما يضمن الغاصب النقصان إذا كان النقصان في العين وكان غير زيوف لأنه دخل جميع أجزائه في ضمانه فيجب عليه قيمة ما تعذر رده ما أجزائه كلاً أو بعضاً بخلاف المبيع حيث النقصان الحادث فيه قبل القبض إلا بالخيار ولا . ܥܐ يوجب شيء من الثمن لأن الأوصاف لا تضمن بالعقد وتضمن بالفعل، وإن كان لتراجع السعر لا يضمن بعد أن في مكان الغصب لأن ذلك لقلة الرغبات فيه لا لنقصان في العين بفوات جزء. وإن كان ربوياً لا يمكنه أن يضمنه النقصان استرداد العين لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا إذا لجودة لا قيمة لها في الأموال الربوية ولكنه يخير بين أن يأخذه ولا شيء له وبين أن يتركه على الغاصب من الربويات أو قيمته. ولك أن تقول : عدم إمكان ذلك مسلم فيما إذا كان نقصان الربويات في الأوصاف كما إذا غصب حنطة فعفنت في يده لأنه لا اعتبار للوصف عندنا، وأما إذا كان نقصانها في الأجزاء كما إذا غصب كيلياً أو وزنياً فتلف بعض أجزائه فنقص فرده كيلاً أو وزناً فيكون لصاحب المال تضمين النقصان مع استراد الباقي ولا يؤدي إلى الربا كما لا يخفى. وفي العناية فسر الربويات بما إذا غصب حنطة فعفنت عنده أو إناء فضة فإنهشم في يده أقول : في كون إناء الفضة من الربويات عندنا فيه نظر ظاهر فإنهم صرحوا في شرح الهداية ومنهم صاحب العناية بأن الوزني الذي في تبعيضه ضرر كالمصوغ من القمقم والذث ليس هو بمثلي بل هو من ذوات القيم، ولا شك أن إناء الفضة منه فكيف مثل به؟ ولاستغلال العبد المستعار بالايجار كاستغلال المغصوب حتى يجب عليه ضمان النقصان ويتصدق بالغلة عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف والوجه قد بيناه. ولو هلك في يده بعدما استعمله فضمنه المالك كان له أن يستعين بالغلة في أداء الضمان لأن الخبث لأجل المالك، فإذا أخذه المالك لا يظهر الخبث في حقه ولهذا لو أسلم الغلة إليه مع العبد يباح له التناول فيزول الخبث بالتسليم وتبرأ ذمته عن القيمة بقدره بخلاف ما إذا باعه الغاصب بعدما استغله وهلك في يد المشتري وضمنه المالك قيمته ثم رجع المشتري على الغاصب بالثمن حيث لا يكون للغاصب أن يستعين بالغلة في أداء الثمن إلى المشتري لأن الخبث كان لحق المالك والمشتري ليس بمالك فلا يزول الخبث بالأداء إليه فلا يؤديه إليه إلا إذا كان لا يجد غيره فيرجح هو على غيره من الفقراء باعتبار أنه ملكه وهو محتاج إليه كما أن للملتقط أن يصرف الغلة على نفسه إذا كان محتاجاً، ثم إذا أصاب مالاً يتصدق بمثله إن كان غنياً وتعذر الاستغلال، وإن كان فقيراً فلا شيء عليه لما ذكرنا من ترجيحه على غيره من الفقهاء.
As for the first [matter], which is use [of the usurped property], what is mentioned here is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): that he gives [the usufruct] in charity. According to Abu Yusuf, he does not give it in charity. We have already mentioned the reasoning for both sides in the preceding issue. It would have been appropriate that he give in charity only the amount exceeding what he guaranteed, not the entire usufruct, as in the first issue. Then the usurper is only liable for the decrease if the decrease is in the substance (ʿayn) and the coin is not counterfeit, because all its parts entered his guarantee, so he must pay the value of what became impossible to return, whether all parts or some, unlike a sale where a decrease occurring before possession [does not obligate anything] except under option. [...] And if it is a ribawi item, he cannot claim the decrease and recover the substance because that leads to riba, since quality has no value in ribawi properties. But he is given the choice between taking it with nothing [for the decrease] or leaving it with the usurper for a ribawi equivalent or its value. You might say: The impossibility is conceded when the decrease in ribawi items is in qualities, as when he usurped wheat and it rotted in his hand, because attributes are not considered in our school. But if the decrease is in parts, as when he usurped a measured or weighed item and some of its parts perished, then it is reduced and returned by measure or weight, so the owner can claim the decrease along with taking back the remainder, and this does not lead to riba, as is clear. In al-ʿInayah, ribawi items are explained as when he usurped wheat and it rotted in his hand, or a silver vessel and it broke in his hand. I say: Considering a silver vessel as ribawi in our school is clearly questionable, for they state in Sharh al-Hidayah, including the author of al-ʿInayah, that a weighed item whose division causes harm, like manufactured vessels and [unclear], is not a mithli but rather a qimi, and undoubtedly a silver vessel is among them, so how can it be used as an example? And the exploitation of a borrowed slave by hiring is like the exploitation of a usurped one, so that he is liable for the decrease and gives the usufruct in charity according to the two, contrary to Abu Yusuf, and we have explained the reasoning. And if it perished in his hand after he used it and the owner claimed the guarantee, he may use the usufruct to pay the guarantee because the unlawfulness (khubth) is in right of the owner. When the owner takes it, the unlawfulness does not appear against him; hence if he gives the usufruct to him along with the slave, it becomes permissible for him to take it, the unlawfulness is removed by delivery, and his liability for the value is discharged to the extent of it. This is contrary to the case where the usurper sold it after exploiting it and it perished in the buyer's hand, and the owner claimed its value from the buyer, and then the buyer sought recourse from the usurper for the price; in that case, the usurper cannot use the usufruct to pay the price to the buyer, because the unlawfulness was in right of the owner, and the buyer is not the owner, so the unlawfulness is not removed by paying him. He should not pay him unless he finds nothing else; then he gives preference to himself over other poor persons, considering that it is his property and he is in need, just as a finder may spend the lost property's usufruct on himself if needy. Then if he later acquires wealth, he gives the equivalent in charity if he becomes rich and it is not possible to make up for the usufruct; but if he remains poor, nothing is due from him, due to the preference we mentioned over other jurists.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage from the sharh expands on rulings regarding the usufruct (ghalla) of usurped property and the liability for decrease (nuqsan). The first point is that the two imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the usurper must give the usufruct in charity, while Abu Yusuf holds he does not. The preferred view is that he gives the excess over the guaranteed amount in charity. Next, the usurper is liable for any decrease in the substance of the property, but not for market fluctuations. For ribawi goods, if the decrease is in quality, he cannot claim it due to riba, but if in quantity, he can. The passage also discusses the borrowed slave, equating it with usurped property regarding the obligation to give the usufruct in charity and liability for decrease. Finally, if the property perishes after use, the usurper may use the usufruct to pay the owner's claim, but if he sold it and it perished with the buyer, he cannot use it to repay the buyer, because the unlawfulness is only removed when the owner receives it. The passage also notes that a poor usurper may keep the usufruct for himself if in need, and later give charity if he becomes rich.

Key Terms
غلة (ghalla): The usufruct or income generated by the usurped property, such as rent from a slave or crop from land.
نقصان (nuqsan): Decrease in the value of the property due to loss of a part or change in substance, as opposed to market fluctuation.
خبث (khubth): Unlawfulness or taint in property due to it being wrongfully acquired; it is removed by returning it to the owner or by giving it in charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon view regarding the usufruct of usurped property?
A) It must be returned to the owner.
B) It must be given in charity.
C) The usurper may keep it.
D) It must be destroyed.

Key Takeaway
The usufruct of usurped property must be given in charity according to the two imams, and the usurper is liable for decrease in substance but not market fluctuations.

Profit from Usurped Specified Property: The Basic Ruling
States the ruling for specified goods with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on profit from specified usurped property and explain the reasoning (khubth) that requires charity.

Core Idea
Profit from a usurped specified item is tainted and must be given in charity.

This lesson covers the foundational rule for usurped property that is specified by designation, such as a specific commodity. The text from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that any profit above the value is not lawful for the usurper and must be given in charity due to the taint (khubth) that arises from the contract attaching to the specific item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property type
Determine whether the usurped property is specified (muta'ayyin) like a commodity or unspecified like currency.
The ruling hinges on this distinction.
2
State the ruling for specified property
For specified property, profit above the value is not lawful (la yatibu) and must be given in charity (yatassadaq bihi).
The profit is not pure for the usurper.
3
Understand the reasoning
The contract attaches to the specific item (yata'allaq bi al-'ayn). If the contract is dissolved by destruction before receipt, the taint (khubth) becomes established in the profit.
This is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli).
4
Note the exception
Before guaranteeing the value (damān), the usurper cannot take anything; after guaranteeing, the principal value is lawful but the profit remains unlawful.
The charity is only for the excess over the value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغصب. الله تعالى : الثاني وهو ما إذا تصرف في المغصوب أو الوديعة وربح فهو على وجوه: إما أن يكون مما يتعين بالتعيين كالعرض أو لا يتعين كالنقدين، فإن كان مما يتعين لا يحل له التناول منه قبل ضمان القيمة وبعده يحل إلا فيما زاد على قدر القيمة وهو الربح المذكور هنا فإنه لا يطيب له ويتصدق به لأن العقد يتعلق فيما لا يتعين بالعتيين حتى تنفسخ بالهلاك قبل القبض فتمكن الخبث فيه. وإن كان مما لا يتعين فقد قال الكرخي : إنه على أربعة أوجه : إما إن أشار ونقد منه أو أشار إليه ونقد من غيره أو أشار إلى غيره ونقد منه أو أطلق إطلاقاً ونقد منه، وفي كل ذلك يطيب له إلا في الوجه الأول وهو ما إذا أشار إليه ونقد منه لأن الإشارة إليه لا تفيد التعيين فيستوي وجودها وعدمها إلا إذا تأكدت بالنقد منهما. وقال مشايخنا رحمهم أنه لا يطيب له بكل حال وهو المختار وإطلاق الجواب في الجامعين يدل على ذلك. ووجهه بالنقد منه استفاد سلامة المشتري وبالإشارة استفاد جواز العقد لتعلق العقد في حق الوصف والقدر فيثبت فيه شبهة لحرمة لمالكه بسبب خبيث واختار بعضهم الفتوى على قول الكرخي في زماننا لكثرة الحرام. وهذا كله على قولهما، وعند أبي يوسف لا يتصدق بشيء منه والوجه ما بينا. وهذا الاختلاف منهم فيما إذا صار بالتقلب من جنس ما ضمن بأن غصب مثلاً وصار في يده من ثمن المغصوب دراهم كان في يده من بدله بخلاف جنس ما دراهم ضمن بأن غصب دراهم وفي يده من بدله طعام أو عروض لا يجب عليه التصدق بالإجماع لأن الربح إنما يتعين عند اتحاد الجنس وما لم يصر بالتقلب من جنس ما ضمن لا يظهر الربح ولو اشترى بثمن المبيع بيعاً فاسداً شيئاً وأشار إليه ونقد منه يطيب الربح لأن الثمن صار ملكاً بالقبض بتراضيهما، ولأنه متى نقض البيع واسترد الثمن يرد مثل الثمن لا عينه ولكن هذا لا يوجب بينهم الخبث في التصرف للحال ولو اشترى بالدراهم المغصوبة طعاماً حل التناول، ولو اشترى بالدراهم المغصوبة دنانير لم يجز له أن يتصرف في الدنانير لأن الدراهم لو استحقت بعد ما افترقا انتقض البيع في الدنانير فوجب عليها ردها، فأما البيع في الطعام لا ينقض باستحقاق الدراهم لأنه يجب عليه رد مثلها لا عينها. ولو اشترى بالثوب المغصوب جارية يحرم عليه أن يطأها حتى يدفع قيمة الثوب إلى صاحبه لأن بالاستحقاق تبين أن البيع فاسد لأن البيع يتعلق بعين الثوب . ولو اشترى بالدراهم المغصوبة جارية حل له وطؤها لأن البيع لا يتعين بتلك الدراهم، ولو تزوج بالثوب المغصوب جارية امرأة حل له وطؤها لأن النكاح لا ينتقض باستحقاق المهر، ولو أخذ المالك القيمة بقول الغاصب في الجارية المغصوبة لم يحل له وطؤها واستخدامها ولا بيعها إلا إذا أعطاه قيمتها بتمامها لأنها من غير رضا المالك، ولهذا لا يملك الفسخ إن ظهر مستحقة. ولو اعتق الغاصب العبد بعد القضاء عليه بالقيمة الناقصة جاز عنده وعليه تمام القيمة ; كذا في المحيط مختصراً.
Book of Usurpation. Allah the Exalted: The second is when he transacts in the usurped or trust and makes a profit, then it is in several ways: Either it is something that becomes specified by designation like a commodity, or not specified like the two coin types (gold/silver). If it is that which becomes specified, it is not lawful for him to take from it before guaranteeing the value, and after that it is lawful, except what exceeds the amount of the value, which is the profit mentioned here; then it is not pure for him and he must give it in charity, because the contract, in what is not specified, attaches to the two things until it is dissolved by destruction before receipt, so corruption becomes established in it. And if it is that which is not specified, then Al-Karkhi said: it is in four ways: either he pointed to it and paid from it, or he pointed to it and paid from other than it, or he pointed to other than it and paid from it, or he made an absolute (pointing) and paid from it. In all of that it is pure for him except the first way, which is when he pointed to it and paid from it, because pointing to it does not effect specification, so its presence and absence are equal, unless it is confirmed by paying from both. And our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said: it is not pure for him in any case, and that is the chosen position, and the absolute answer in the two Jami's indicates that. And its reasoning: by paying from it, he obtained safety for the buyer, and by pointing, he obtained permissibility of the contract, because the contract attaches with respect to description and amount, so a doubt of prohibition for its owner due to evil is established. And some chose the fatwa according to Al-Karkhi's statement in our time due to the abundance of unlawful. And all this is according to their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) statement. According to Abu Yusuf, he does not give anything in charity from it, and the opinion is as we explained. And this difference among them is when the property has by transformation become of the same type as what he guaranteed, e.g., he usurped an item and in his hand from the price of the usurped item there are dirhams that are from its substitute, as opposed to if it became of a different type from the dirhams he guaranteed, e.g., he usurped dirhams and in his hand from their substitute there is food or commodities: he is not obligated to give in charity by consensus, because profit is only determined when the type is the same, and as long as it has not by transformation become of the same type as what he guaranteed, the profit does not appear. And if he buys something with the price of a void sale, pointing to it and paying from it, the profit is pure because the price became ownership by receipt with mutual consent, and because when he cancels the sale and reclaims the price, he returns the equivalent of the price, not its exact item, but this does not necessitate evil in the transaction for the present. And if he buys food with usurped dirhams, it is lawful to consume. And if he buys dinars with usurped dirhams, it is not permissible for him to transact in the dinars because if the dirhams are claimed as rightfully owned after they separated, the sale of the dinars is rescinded, so he must return them. As for the sale of food, it is not rescinded by the claim of the dirhams because he must return their equivalent, not their exact items. And if he buys a slave-girl with the usurped garment, it is forbidden for him to have intercourse with her until he pays the value of the garment to its owner, because by the claim it becomes clear that the sale is void, since the sale attaches to the exact garment. And if he buys a slave-girl with usurped dirhams, it is lawful for him to have intercourse with her because the sale is not specified by those dirhams. And if he marries a woman with the usurped garment as dower, it is lawful for him to have intercourse with her because marriage is not rescinded by the claim of the dower. And if the owner takes the value based on the statement of the usurper regarding the usurped slave-girl, it is not lawful for him to have intercourse with her, use her for service, or sell her, unless he gives her full value, because it is without the owner's consent; for this reason, he does not have the option of rescission if it appears that she was rightfully owned by another. And if the usurper emancipates the slave after being ordered to pay the deficient value, it is valid according to him (Abu Hanifa), and he owes the full value; thus in Al-Muhit, abridged.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Usurpation (Kitab al-Ghasb) in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the ruling on profit (ribh) when a usurper or trustee transacts with usurped or trust property and makes a gain. The passage is an expansion probe, building on the base text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author first distinguishes between property that is 'specified by designation' (muta'ayyin) like a commodity, and property that is not specified like currency (gold and silver coins). For specified property, any profit above the value is not lawful for the usurper and must be given in charity, because the contract attaches to the specific item, and after destruction before receipt, the contract dissolves, leaving a taint (khubth). For unspecified property, the author presents the view of Al-Karkhi, who divides the transaction into four scenarios based on whether the usurper pointed to the property and paid from it or not; in all but one scenario the profit is lawful. However, the majority of our scholars (mashayikh) hold that it is never lawful and must be given in charity; this is the chosen (mukhtar) view. The reason is that the usurper's action combines paying (which secures the buyer's safety) and pointing (which makes the contract permissible but creates a doubt due to the unlawful origin). Some latter scholars preferred Al-Karkhi's fatwa in their time due to widespread unlawful transactions. The passage then notes that this ruling applies according to the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf holds that no charity is required at all. The difference is confined to cases where the usurped property has been transformed into the same type as the guarantee (e.g., the usurper obtains dirhams from selling the usurped item). If the type differs (e.g., usurped dirhams turned into food), there is consensus that no charity is required because profit only appears when types are the same. The passage then gives multiple illustrative cases: buying food with usurped dirhams is lawful; buying dinars is not lawful because the sale can be rescinded; buying a slave-girl with a usurped garment forbids intercourse until the value is paid, while with dirhams it is lawful; marriage with usurped dower does not invalidate the marriage; if the owner takes the value based on the usurper's statement, he cannot use the slave-girl until he receives full value; and emancipation by the usurper after partial payment is valid but he owes the full value.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun): Specification by designation; whether the property is identified as a specific item (like a commodity) or not (like currency).
خبث (khubth): Moral or legal taint/impurity in the transaction due to the unlawful origin of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, what must a usurper do with profit from selling a specific usurped car?
A) Keep it as his own
B) Return it to the owner
C) Give it in charity
D) Destroy it

Key Takeaway
Profit from a usurped specified item is tainted and must be given in charity; the principal becomes lawful after guaranteeing the value.
