The Owner's Options When Property is Damaged by a Usurper: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on takhyir when property is damaged

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether an owner may claim depreciation (nuqsan) when taking damaged property, and apply the criteria for fahish vs. yasir tearing.

Core Idea
Slaughtering is an increase, so taking meat forfeits depreciation; cutting a limb of a non-edible animal destroys all value.

When a usurper damages your property, you have choices—but not always the same choices. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether you can both keep the damaged item and claim compensation for its loss in value. Understanding the distinction between fahish (excessive) and yasir (minor) damage, and the special case of usurping edible animals versus non-edible ones, is essential for applying the correct ruling in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling category
The act is damaging another's property by tearing, slaughtering, or cutting limbs. Determine whether the damage is fahish (excessive) or yasir (minor) based on loss of substance and benefit.
The criterion: fahish = loss of part of the substance and part of the benefit; yasir = only loss of quality.
2
State the general rule for fahish damage
For fahish tearing or slaughtering an edible animal, the owner has a choice (takhyir): either take the full value and leave the damaged item, or keep the item and claim depreciation (nuqsan).
This is the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) as presented by the author.
3
Note Abu Hanifa's objection for slaughtered animals
Abu Hanifa holds that if the owner takes the meat of a slaughtered sheep, he cannot claim depreciation because slaughtering and skinning are considered an increase (ziyada)—the risk of natural death is removed and meat becomes usable.
Thus Abu Hanifa says: take full value and leave meat, or take meat with no further compensation.
4
Apply the rule to non-edible animals and limb cutting
For non-edible animals like donkeys, cutting a limb renders the animal completely useless (no carrying, no riding), so the owner must take full value and cannot keep the carcass with depreciation. For edible animals, cutting a limb is different: the animal can still be used for other purposes (e.g., slaughtered for meat), so the choice applies.
Cutting a limb of an edible animal does not destroy all value; the animal still has meat value.
5
Identify the exception for ribawi goods
For ribawi goods (e.g., gold, silver, food staples), any defect—whether fahish or yasir—gives the owner only two options: keep the item and claim nothing, or surrender it and claim full value (or like). Depreciation compensation is impossible because it leads to riba.
This applies only when no new workmanship has been added by the usurper.

Source Text

كتاب الغصب ثوباً فاحشاً ضمن القيمة وسلم المغصوب أو ضمن النقصان وفي الخرق اليسير ضمن وعليه قيمته والباب له، وكذا لو نقش إناء فضة بالنقر، وذكر الكرخي أنه موضوع مسألة الساجة إذا بنى الغاصب حول الساجة. أما لو بنى على نفس الساجة لا يبطل ملك المالك بل ينقض وهو اختيار الفقيه أبي جعفر الهندواني لأنه إذا بنى حولها لم يكن متعدياً، وإذا بنى عليها كان متعدياً، والصحيح أن الجواب في الموضعين على حد واحد؛ كذا في البدائع . قال رحمه الله : (ولو ذبح شاة أو خرق ثوباً فاحشاً ضمن القيمة وسلم المغصوب أو ضمن النقصان وكذا لو ذبح وقطع اليد أو الرجل لأن هذه الأشياء إتلاف من وجه باعتبار فوات بعض الأغراض من الحمل والدار والنسل وفوات بعض المنفعة في الثوب فيخير بين تضمين جميع قيمته وتركه له وبين تضمين نقصانه وأخذه . وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه ليس له أن يضمنه النقصان إذا أخذ اللحم لأن الذبح والسلخ زيادة فيها لانقطاع احتمال الموت حتف أنفها وأمكن الانتفع بلحمها يتعين والأول هو الظاهر لأنه نقصان باعتبار فوات بعض الأغراض على ما بينا ولو كانت الدابة غير مأكولة اللحم يضمن قاطع الطرف جميع قيمتها لأنه استهلاك من كل وجه بخلاف قطع الطرف لأنه بعد القطع صالح لجميع ما كان صالحاً قبله من الانتفاع ولا كذلك الدابة فإنها لا تصلح للحمل ولا للركوب بعد القطع. قيد التخيير بذبح الشاة وما يوكل لحمه احترازاً عما لا يؤكل لحمه قال في الخانية : ولو ذبح حمار غيره فليس له أن يضمن النقصان في قول الإمام ولكن يضمنه جميع القيمة، وعلى قول محمد له أن يمسك الحمار ويضمنه النقصان وإن شاء ضمنه كل القيمة ولا يمسك الحمار، وإن قتله قتلاً فهو على الخلاف السابق والاعتماد على قول الإمام ولو قطع يد حمار أو بغل أو قطع رجله أو فقأ عينه قال الإمام: إن شاء سلم الجسد وضمنه جميع القيمة وليس له أن يمسك الجسد ويضمنه النقصان وفي المنتقى هشام عن محمد: رجل قطع يد حماراً وبغل أو رجله وكان لما بقي منه قيمة فله أن يمسكه ويأخذ النقصان وفي النوازل : إذا قطع أذن الدابة أو بعضه يضمن النقصان ولو قطع أذنيها يضمن النقصان. وعن شيخه رضي الله عنه : إذا قطع ذنب حمار القاضي يضمن جميع قيمته وإن كان لغيره يضمن النقصان اهـ. أقول : ويلحق بحمار القاضي حمار المفتي والعالم والأمير. وفي التجريد: والصحيح في الحد الفاصل بين الخرق الفاحش واليسير أن الخرق الفاحش ما يفوت به بعض العين وبعض المنفعة واليسير مما لا يفوت به شيء من المنفعة وإنما تفوت الجودة. قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني : القطع أنواع ثلاثة : فاحش غير مستأصل وهو ما بينا، وقطع يسير وهو أن يقطع طرفاً من أطراف الثوب ولا يثبت فيه الخيار للمالك ولكن يضمنه النقصان وقطع فاحش مستأصل للثوب وهو أن يقطع الثوب قطعاً لا يصلح لما يراد منه ولا يرغب في شرائه؛ فعن الإمام المالك بالخيار إن شاء ترك المقطوع وضمنه القيمة وإن شاء أخذ المقطوع ولا شيء له. المنفعة وعندهما له أن يأخذ القيمة ويضمنه النقصان .اهـ. فظهر أن ما أطلقه المؤلف في الخرق الفاحش إنما يتأتى على قولهما لا على قول الإمام وفي المنتقى بشر عن أبي يوسف: غصب شاة فحلبها ضمن قيمة اللبن .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (وفي الخرق اليسير ضمن نقصانه) يعني مع أخذ عينه وليس له غير ذلك لأن العين قائمة من كل وجه وإنما دخله عيب فنقص بذلك وكان له أن يضمنه النقصان وقد بينا الفرق بين الفاحش واليسير وقال الشارح: واختلفوا في الخرق اليسير والفاحش، قيل ما يوجب نقصان ربع القيمة فاحش وما دونه يسير، وقيل ما ينقص به نصف القيمة والصحيح أن الفاحش ما يفوت به بعض العين وجنس المنفعة ويبقى بعض العين وبعض المنفعة، واليسير ما لا يفوت به شيء من المنفعة وإنما يدخل فيه نقصان في المنفعة لأن الاستهلاك المطلق من كل وجه عبارة عن إتلاف جميع والاستهلاك من وجه عبارة عن تفويت بعض المنفعة والنقصان عبارة عن تفويت المنافع مع بقائها وهو تفويت الجودة لا غير، ولا عبرة بقيام أكثر المنافع لأن الرجحان إنما يطلب إذا تعذر العمل بأحدهما ومتى أمكن العمل بهما لا يضر الترجيح ولا يشتغل به. قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : الحكم الذي ذكرناه في الخرق في الثوب إذا كان فاحشاً هو الحكم في كل الأعيان إلا في الأموال الربوية فإن التعيب فيها سواء كان فاحشاً أو يسيراً فالمالك فيهما يخير بين أن يمسك العين ولا يرجع على الغاصب بشيء وبين أن يسلم العين ويضمنه مثله أو قيمته لأن تضمين النقصان متعذر لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا هذا إذا قطع الثوب ولم يجدد فيه صنعة، وأما إذا جدد فيه صنعة فيأتي في المتن . وفي الأصل: غصب ثوباً فعفن عنده أو أصفر أخذه المالك وما نقص منه إذا كان النقصان يسيراً ولو فاحشاً خير بين الأخذ والترك اهـ .
The Book of Usurpation: For a garment [torn] excessively, he guarantees the value and delivers the usurped [item] or guarantees the depreciation. And for a minor tear, he guarantees and pays its value, and the door is for him. Likewise, if he engraved a silver vessel. And al-Karkhi mentioned that it is the subject of the case of the post if the usurper built around the post. As for if he built on the post itself, the ownership of the owner is not nullified, but it is demolished, which is the choice of al-Faqih Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani because if he built around it, he was not transgressing, and if he built on it, he was transgressing. The correct is that the answer in both places is the same; thus in al-Bada'i'. He (the author) said: 'And if he slaughtered a sheep or tore a garment excessively, he guarantees the value and delivers the usurped object or guarantees the depreciation. Likewise, if he slaughtered and cut off the hand or foot because these things are destruction from one aspect, considering the loss of some purposes like carrying, housing, and offspring, and the loss of some benefit from the garment, so he has a choice between compensating the entire value and leaving it to him, or compensating the depreciation and taking it. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that he does not have the option to guarantee the depreciation if he takes the meat because slaughtering and skinning is an increase due to the elimination of the possibility of natural death, and benefit can be obtained from its meat, so it is determined. The first is the apparent position because it is a decrease in consideration of the loss of some purposes as we have explained. And if the animal is not edible meat, the cutter of the limb guarantees its entire value because it is consumed from every aspect, unlike cutting a limb because after cutting, it is suitable for all that it was suitable for before in terms of benefit. But not so for the animal, for it is not suitable for carrying or riding after cutting. The restriction of the choice to slaughtering a sheep and what is edible meat is a precaution to exclude what is not edible meat. In al-Khaniya: If he slaughtered another's donkey, he does not have the option to guarantee the depreciation according to Imam, but he guarantees the entire value. According to Muhammad's saying, he may keep the donkey and guarantee the depreciation, and if he wishes, he guarantees the entire value and does not keep the donkey. And if he killed it, it follows the previous difference, and reliance is on Imam's saying. And if he cut the hand of a donkey or mule, or cut its leg, or gouged out its eye, Imam said: If he wishes, he delivers the body and guarantees the entire value, and he does not have the option to keep the body and guarantee the depreciation. In al-Muntaqa, Hisham from Muhammad: A man cut the hand of a donkey or mule or its leg, and what remained had value, so he may keep it and take the depreciation. In al-Nawazil: If he cut the ear of a beast or part of it, he guarantees the depreciation; if he cut both ears, he guarantees the depreciation. From his teacher: If he cut the tail of the judge's donkey, he guarantees the entire value; if it belonged to someone else, he guarantees the depreciation. He said: I say: The donkey of the judge is joined by the donkey of the mufti, the scholar, and the amir. In al-Tajrid: The correct in the decisive criterion between excessive and minor tearing is that excessive tearing is what causes loss of part of the substance and part of the benefit, and minor is what does not cause loss of any benefit, but only loss of quality. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: Cutting is of three types: excessive non-destructive, which is what we have mentioned; minor cutting, which is cutting an edge of the garment edges, and the owner does not have the option, but he guarantees the depreciation; and excessive destructive cutting of the garment, which is to cut the garment in a way that it is not suitable for its intended purpose and no one desires to buy it. In such a case, the owner has the option: if he wishes, he leaves the cut piece and guarantees the value, and if he wishes, he takes the cut piece and has nothing. The benefit: According to them (Sahibayn), he has the option to take the value and guarantee the depreciation. So it appears that what the author stated regarding excessive tearing applies to their saying, not to Imam's saying. In al-Muntaqa, Bishr from Abu Yusuf: He usurped a sheep and milked it; he guarantees the value of the milk. He (the author) said: 'And for minor tearing, he guarantees its depreciation' meaning along with taking the item itself, and he has nothing else because the substance is intact from every aspect, and it only incurred a defect, so it depreciated thereby, and he has the right to guarantee the depreciation. And we have already explained the difference between excessive and minor. The commentator said: They differed regarding minor and excessive tearing. It is said: What causes a decrease of a quarter of the value is excessive, and what is less is minor. And it is said: What decreases it by half of the value. The correct is that excessive is what causes loss of part of the substance and the type of benefit, and part of the substance and part of the benefit remain. Minor is what does not cause loss of any benefit, but only a decrease in benefit enters because absolute consumption from every aspect is destruction of all, consumption from one aspect is loss of some benefit, and depreciation is loss of benefits while they remain, which is loss of quality only. No consideration is given to the existence of most benefits because preference is only sought when it is impossible to act on one of them; whenever it is possible to act on both, preference does not cause harm and it is not engaged in. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: The ruling we mentioned regarding tearing of a garment if it is excessive is the ruling for all goods except usurious wealth, because defect in them whether excessive or minor, the owner in both cases has a choice between keeping the item and not claiming anything from the usurper, or surrendering the item and claiming its like or value, because guaranteeing the depreciation is impossible as it leads to riba. This is if he cut the garment and did not add any workmanship. As for if he added workmanship, it will come in the text. In al-Asl: He usurped a garment and it rotted with him or turned yellow; the owner takes it and what is decreased from it if the depreciation is minor; if it is excessive, he has a choice between taking and leaving.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding usurpation (ghasb). The author explains the ruling when a usurper damages the property. The central issue is the owner's option (takhyir) when the damage is substantial (fahish) vs. minor (yasir). For a tear that is fahish (excessive), the owner may choose either to take the damaged item and claim full value from the usurper, or keep the item and claim only the depreciation. For a yasir (minor) tear, the owner must take the item and can only claim the depreciation. The same principle applies to slaughtering an animal or cutting limbs, with a distinction between edible and non-edible animals. An intra-Hanafi dispute arises: Abu Hanifa holds that if a slaughtered animal is taken, the owner cannot claim depreciation because the slaughter and skinning are considered an increase (the meat becomes available and eliminates the risk of natural death). Muhammad holds that the owner may keep the meat and claim the depreciation. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's. For non-edible animals like donkeys, cutting a limb renders them completely useless, so the owner gets full value. The passage also provides a precise definition of fahish vs. yasir: fahish causes loss of part of the substance and part of the benefit; yasir only affects quality, not benefit. The exception is for ribawi goods, where any defect triggers the option between keeping or claiming full value or like, because depreciation would involve riba.

Key Terms
الخرق الفاحش (al-kharq al-fāḥish): An excessive tear that causes loss of part of the substance and part of the benefit, making the item unsuitable for its main purpose and unattractive for purchase.
الخرق اليسير (al-kharq al-yasīr): A minor tear that only affects quality (جودة) without any loss of benefit.
تضمين النقصان (taḍmīn al-nuqṣān): Compensation for the decrease in value (depreciation) of the damaged item, as opposed to full value compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a usurper slaughters someone's sheep, what option does the owner have if he wants to take the meat?
A) He may take the meat and claim the full value of the sheep.
B) He may take the meat and claim the depreciation (nuqsan).
C) He must either take the full value and leave the meat, or take the meat and forgo compensation.
D) He may only claim the full value and cannot take the meat.

Key Takeaway
For damaged property, the owner's options depend on the extent of damage: fahish gives a choice; yasir forces depreciation only; slaughtering an animal is treated as an increase, blocking depreciation when taking the meat; and ribawi goods never allow depreciation.

Building or Planting on Another's Land: Removal and Compensation
Ruling with proof and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for building/planting on another's land, identify the prophetic proof, and apply the exception for high-value buildings.

Core Idea
A wrongful addition on another's land must be removed, but if the building exceeds the land's value, the usurper may compensate and keep it.

This lesson examines a classic case of usurpation (ghasb) in Hanafi law: building or planting on someone else's land without permission. The core principle is that the landowner's property rights are paramount, so the usurper must restore the land to its original state. However, a key exceptional scenario exists to prevent excessive loss. Let's trace the evidence and reasoning from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The act is planting or building on another's land without permission. The default ruling is that the usurper must demolish or uproot and return the land to its owner.
This is a wajib obligation upon the usurper.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith: 'There is no right for a wrongful root' (laysa li 'irqin zalimin haqq). Identify the phrase and understand its legal implication.
The 'wrongful root' refers to any structure or plantation placed wrongfully on another's property.
3
Understand the Hanafi legal reasoning
The land remains in the owner's ownership because it is not consumed nor actually usurped. The usurper merely placed something on it, which does not transfer ownership. Therefore, he must vacate it just as one must return someone else's container after filling it with food.
The land is not maghsubah haqiqatan – it is not actually seized; only the benefit is usurped.
4
Identify the exception based on value
If the building's value exceeds the value of the land (specifically the 'post' or the land itself), the usurper may pay the owner the value of the land and keep the building. This is to prevent greater loss (daf' al-darar).
This is an exception to the general rule of removal.
5
Note the analogical examples
The text provides examples: a chicken swallowing a pearl, a calf growing in a house, a cow's head stuck in a pot. In each case, the party with the more valuable item compensates the other.
These illustrate the same principle of avoiding excessive loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله تعالى: (ولو غرس أو بنى في أرض الغير قلعاً وردت) أي قلع البناء والغرس وردت الأرض إلى صاحبها لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «ليس لعرق ظالم حق» أي ليس الذي عرق ظالم وصف العرق بصفة صاحبه وهو الظلم وهو من المجاز كما يقال صائم نهاره وقائم ليله قال الله تعالى {فيها يفرق كل أمر حكيم} [الدخان: ٤] ولأن الأرض باقية على ملكه إذا لم تكن مستهلكة ولا مغصوبة حقيقة ولم يوجد فيها شيء يوجب الملك للغاصب فيؤمر بتفريغها وردها إلى مالكها كما إذا أشغل ظرف غيره بالطعام. هذا إذا كانت قيمة الساجة أكثر من قيمة البناء، وإن كانت قيمة البناء أكثر فللغاصب أن يضمن له قيمة الساجة ويأخذها ذكره في النهاية. وعلى هذا لو بلعت دجاجة لؤلؤة ينظر أيهما أكثر قيمة فلصاحبه أن يأخذ ويضمنه قيمة الأخرى. وعلى هذا التفصيل لو أدخل فصيل غيره في داره وكبر فيها ولم يمكن إخراجه إلا بهدم الحائط، وعلى هذا التفصيل لو أدخل البقر رأسه في قدر من النحاس فتعذر إخراجه وقد استوعبنا هذه المسألة بفروعها في مسألة نقصان الأرض فلا نعيده. وفي
He (the author), may Allah Almighty have mercy on him, said: 'And if he planted or built in another's land, he uproots and returns' meaning he demolishes the building and plantation and returns the land to its owner, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'There is no right for a wrongful root'; meaning it is not a root that is wrongfully planted; the description of the root with the quality of its owner, which is oppression, and it is figurative, as it is said 'a faster of his day, a prayer of his night'. Allah Almighty said: {In it is distinguished every wise matter} [al-Dukhan: 4]. And because the land remains in his ownership since it is not consumed nor actually usurped, and nothing exists in it that necessitates ownership for the usurper, so he is ordered to vacate it and return it to its owner, just as if he had occupied another's container with food. This is if the value of the post is greater than the value of the building; but if the value of the building is greater, then the usurper has the option to guarantee the value of the post and take it, as mentioned in al-Nihaya. Based on this, if a chicken swallowed a pearl, one looks to which is more valuable; its owner can take it and guarantee the value of the other. Based on this detail, if he inserted another's calf into his house and it grew and could not be removed except by demolishing the wall; and based on this detail, if a cow inserted its head into a copper pot and its removal became difficult. We have comprehensively covered this issue with its branches in the issue of decrease in land, so we will not repeat it.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the matn regarding building or planting on another's land without permission. The ruling is clear: the usurper (ghasib) must demolish the building or uproot the plantation and return the land to its owner. The proof is the prophetic hadith: 'There is no right for a wrongful root' (laysa li 'irqin zalimin haqq), meaning that any structure or planting that is wrongful (i.e., on another's property) gives no right to the builder. The hadith uses a figure of speech: the 'root' (trees/plants) is described as 'wrongful' because its planter is wrong. The Quranic verse {In it is distinguished every wise matter} is cited as a general proof for the wisdom of divine decrees. The legal reasoning: the land remains the owner's property because it is not consumed (istihlak) nor actually usurped (maghsubah haqiqatan) — the usurper only placed something on it, which does not transfer ownership. Therefore, the land must be vacated just as if someone occupied another's container with food (he must return the container). However, an exception arises when the value of the building exceeds the value of the land (specifically the 'post' or the land itself). In that case, the usurper may compensate the owner for the value of the land and keep the building. This is based on a principle of preventing loss: if demolishing the building would cause greater loss than the land's value, the usurper can pay for the land and retain the building. The text provides analogical examples: a chicken swallowing a pearl, a calf growing too large to remove without demolishing a wall, a cow's head stuck in a copper pot. In each case, the party with the more valuable item compensates the other. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle of avoiding excessive loss (daf' al-darar). The author notes he has covered this issue comprehensively elsewhere and will not repeat.

Key Terms
الغرس والبناء في أرض الغير (al-ghars wa al-binā' fī arḍ al-ghayr): Planting trees or constructing buildings on land owned by another person without permission (usurpation).
العرق الظالم (al-'irq al-ẓālim): A 'wrongful root' – any plantation or structure placed on another's property without right, so called because of the wrongdoing of the person who placed it.
النقصان بالقلع (al-nuqṣān bi-al-qal'): The decrease in land value caused by the removal (uprooting/demolition) of the wrongful addition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, under what condition may the usurper keep the building he built on another's land?
A) Never; he must always demolish and return the land.
B) Only if the building's value is greater than the land's value.
C) Only if the landowner gives permission after the fact.
D) Only if the building is made of stone rather than wood.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a wrongful addition on another's land must be removed, but if the addition's value exceeds the land's value, the usurper may compensate and keep it to avoid greater loss.

الخرق الفاحش واليسير: Options When a Garment is Torn
States ruling for slight tear and defines fahish vs. yasir with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on severe tear, define severe vs. slight tear, and apply the mufta bihi view in a given scenario.

Core Idea
A severe tear allows the owner to take full value only according to Sahibayn; slight tear only compensation for defect.

When a garment is torn by a usurper, the owner's remedies differ depending on the severity of the tear. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the dispute within the Hanafi school between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Understanding this distinction is essential for determining proper compensation in cases of property damage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the passage deals with a garment torn by a usurper (ghasib). The issue is whether the owner can demand the full value or only compensation for the loss in value.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam vs. Sahibayn.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam holds that the owner must keep the garment and can only claim compensation for the defect (nuqsan). He cannot surrender it for full value because the garment still exists as an 'ayn.
3
State Sahibayn's position
Sahibayn allow the owner to either keep the garment and claim the loss, or surrender it and claim its full value. They consider the severe tear as partial destruction (istihlak min wajh).
This is the mufta bihi view according to the author.
4
Define severe vs. slight tear
The correct definition is that a severe tear destroys some of the essence (ayn) and all utility (manfa'ah); a slight tear only reduces quality (jūdah) without destroying any utility.
Other definitions based on value (quarter or half) are considered less correct.
5
Note the ribawi exception
For ribawi goods (e.g., money, food staples), any defect—whether severe or slight—gives the owner the choice to keep the item with no claim or surrender it for its like or value, because compensating for the defect would lead to riba.
This exception overrides the general rule for non-ribawi items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندهما له أن يأخذ القيمة ويضمنه النقصان .اهـ. فظهر أن ما أطلقه المؤلف في الخرق الفاحش إنما يتأتى على قولهما لا على قول الإمام وفي المنتقى بشر عن أبي يوسف: غصب شاة فحلبها ضمن قيمة اللبن .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (وفي الخرق اليسير ضمن نقصانه) يعني مع أخذ عينه وليس له غير ذلك لأن العين قائمة من كل وجه وإنما دخله عيب فنقص بذلك وكان له أن يضمنه النقصان وقد بينا الفرق بين الفاحش واليسير وقال الشارح: واختلفوا في الخرق اليسير والفاحش، قيل ما يوجب نقصان ربع القيمة فاحش وما دونه يسير، وقيل ما ينقص به نصف القيمة والصحيح أن الفاحش ما يفوت به بعض العين وجنس المنفعة ويبقى بعض العين وبعض المنفعة، واليسير ما لا يفوت به شيء من المنفعة وإنما يدخل فيه نقصان في المنفعة لأن الاستهلاك المطلق من كل وجه عبارة عن إتلاف جميع والاستهلاك من وجه عبارة عن تفويت بعض المنفعة والنقصان عبارة عن تفويت المنافع مع بقائها وهو تفويت الجودة لا غير، ولا عبرة بقيام أكثر المنافع لأن الرجحان إنما يطلب إذا تعذر العمل بأحدهما ومتى أمكن العمل بهما لا يضر الترجيح ولا يشتغل به. قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : الحكم الذي ذكرناه في الخرق في الثوب إذا كان فاحشاً هو الحكم في كل الأعيان إلا في الأموال الربوية فإن التعيب فيها سواء كان فاحشاً أو يسيراً فالمالك فيهما يخير بين أن يمسك العين ولا يرجع على الغاصب بشيء وبين أن يسلم العين ويضمنه مثله أو قيمته لأن تضمين النقصان متعذر لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا هذا إذا قطع الثوب ولم يجدد فيه صنعة، وأما إذا جدد فيه صنعة فيأتي في المتن . وفي الأصل: غصب ثوباً فعفن عنده أو أصفر أخذه المالك وما نقص منه إذا كان النقصان يسيراً ولو فاحشاً خير بين الأخذ والترك اهـ.
And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he may take the value and guarantee the loss. So it appears that what the author [of Kanz] mentioned regarding the severe tear is only applicable according to their view, not according to the Imam [Abu Hanifa]. And in al-Muntaqa, it is related from Abu Yusuf: 'If someone usurps a goat and milks it, he guarantees the value of the milk.' He (the author) said: 'And in the slight tear, he guarantees its loss' meaning while taking the very same [garment]; he has no other option because the essence is fully intact, only a defect entered it, so it decreased thereby, and he may guarantee him the loss. And we have explained the difference between the severe and slight. The commentator said: 'They differed regarding the slight and severe tear. Some said: what necessitates a loss of a quarter of the value is severe, and what is less is slight. Some said: what decreases by half the value [is severe]. The correct [view] is that severe is that by which some of the essence and the entire utility are lost, while some essence and some utility remain; slight is that by which no utility is lost, only a decrease in quality enters. For absolute consumption from all aspects is destruction of all, consumption from one aspect is loss of some utility, and loss (nuqsan) is loss of utilities while they remain, which is only loss of quality. No consideration is given to the majority of utilities remaining, because preference is only sought when it is impossible to act by both; when it is possible to act by both, preference does not harm and one does not engage in it. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: The ruling we mentioned regarding the tear in the garment if it is severe is the ruling for all objects except for ribawi goods, for in them the defect, whether severe or slight, the owner has the choice between keeping the object and not claiming anything from the usurper, and between surrendering the object and claiming its like or value, because guaranteeing the loss is impossible as it leads to riba. This is if he cut the garment and did not add a new [manufacturing] process. As for if he added a new process, it will come in the text. And in al-Asl: 'If someone usurps a garment and it rots or yellows, the owner takes it and what is lost from it if the loss is slight; if it is severe, he has the choice between taking and leaving.'
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and definition ruling. It clarifies the options of the owner when a garment is torn by the usurper. The author (in Kanz) said: 'For a slight tear, he guarantees the loss' meaning the owner keeps the garment and claims compensation for the defect. The commentator explains that for a severe tear, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the owner can only claim the defect, but according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the owner may surrender the garment and claim its full value, and also guarantee the loss (which is effectively the same). The author then discusses the definition of severe vs. slight tear. Several definitions are given: some measure by quarter of value, some by half. The correct definition according to the commentator is that severe tear destroys some of the essence and all utility, while slight tear only reduces quality. Then the passage quotes al-Sarakhsi: this distinction applies to all goods except ribawi items (e.g., money, food staples). For ribawi goods, any defect (slight or severe) gives the owner the option to either keep the item with no claim or surrender it and claim its like or value. This is because compensating for the defect would involve riba. The passage also mentions the case where the garment rots or yellows, where the rule is similar: if the loss is slight, the owner takes the garment; if severe, he has the option.

Key Terms
الخرق الفاحش (al-kharq al-fāḥish): A tear that destroys some of the essence (ayn) and all utility (manfa'ah) of the garment.
الخرق اليسير (al-kharq al-yasīr): A tear that reduces quality (jūdah) but does not destroy any utility.
الاستهلاك من وجه (al-istihlāk min wajh): Partial consumption or destruction; the item is not completely destroyed, but part of its benefit is lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the passage, if a usurper makes a severe tear in a non-ribawi garment, what options does the owner have?
A) The owner must keep the garment and can only claim compensation for the defect.
B) The owner may either keep the garment and claim the defect, or surrender it and claim its full value.
C) The owner must surrender the garment and claim its like or value.
D) The owner may only claim the value of the garment without returning it.

Key Takeaway
The owner's remedy for a torn garment depends on whether the tear is severe (fahish) or slight (yasir), and on the nature of the goods (ribawi vs. non-ribawi).

Ruling on Planting or Building on Usurped Land
Ruling with proof from hadith and analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on removal of structures from usurped land, explain the proof from hadith, and apply the valuation method when land is damaged.

Core Idea
The usurper must remove his structure and return the land; compensation for land damage is valued as removed materials.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a person who plants trees or builds a structure on land belonging to someone else without permission. The key question: what must the usurper do? The answer is not simply pay compensation—the default is physical removal, rooted in the hadith 'There is no right for a wrongful root.' We will explore the proof, the rule, and how valuation works when removal harms the land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is planting or building on another's land. The ruling is wajib: the usurper must remove the structure and return the land to its owner.
The ruling applies whether the structure is a building or trees.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith 'La haqqa li-'irqin zalim' (there is no right for a wrongful root). This hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in Hanafi usul.
The phrase 'irq zalim' metaphorically describes the wrongfully placed plant or structure.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The negation 'la haqqa' explicitly denies any legal right for the wrongful root. This implies the usurper cannot keep the structure; it must be removed to restore the land to its owner.
The Hanafi school interprets this as a general command to remove all wrongful structures, not just trees.
4
Understand the valuation method when land is damaged
If removal damages the land (e.g., causes a hole), the owner can guarantee the value of the structure as removed (i.e., as materials like firewood or rubble), not as standing. The value is calculated by comparing land value with and without the structure.
The exception: if the building is more valuable than the land, the usurper may pay the land value and keep the building (per Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله تعالى: (ولو غرس أو بنى في أرض الغير قلعاً وردت) أي قلع البناء والغرس وردت الأرض إلى صاحبها لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام ليس لعرق ظالم حق» أي ليس الذي عرق ظالم وصف العرق بصفة صاحبه وهو الظلم وهو من المجاز كما يقال صائم نهاره وقائم ليله قال الله تعالى فيها يفرق كل أمر حكيم [الدخان : ٤] ولأن الأرض باقية على ملكه إذا لم تكن مستهلكة ولا مغصوبة حقيقة ولم يوجد فيها شيء يوجب الملك للغاصب فيؤمر بتفريغها وردها إلى مالكها كما إذا أشغل ظرف غيره بالطعام. هذا إذا كانت قيمة الساجة أكثر من قيمة البناء، وإن كانت قيمة البناء أكثر فللغاصب أن يضمن له قيمة الساجة ويأخذها ذكره في النهاية. وعلى هذا لو بلعت دجاجة لؤلؤة ينظر أيهما أكثر قيمة فلصاحبه أن يأخذ ويضمنه قيمة الأخرى. وعلى هذا التفصيل لو أدخل فصيل غيره في داره وكبر فيها ولم يمكن إخراجه إلا بهدم الحائط، وعلى هذا التفصيل لو أدخل البقر رأسه في قدر من النحاس فتعذر إخراجه وقد استوعبنا هذه المسألة بفروعها في مسألة نقصان الأرض فلا نعيده. وفي التتارخانية : لو غصب حنطة فزرعها تصدق بالفضل .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (فإن نقصت الأرض بالقلع ضمن له البناء والغرس مقلوعاً ويكونان له أي إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع كان لصاحب الأرض أن يضمن للغاصب قيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعاً ويكونان له لأن فيه دفع الضرر عنهما فتعين فيه النظر لهما، وإنما يضمن قيمتها مقلوعاً لأنه مستحق للقلع وليس له يستديم فيها فتعتبر قيمته في ذلك الوقت مقلوعاً وكيفية معرفتها أنه يقوم الأرض وبها بناء أو شجر ويستحق قلعه أي أمر بقلعه وتقوم وحدها ليس فيها بناء ولا غرس فيضمن فضل ما بينهما، كذا قالوا وهذا ليس بضمان لقيمته مقلوعاً بل هو ضمان لقيمته قائماً مستحق القلع، وإنما يكون ضماناً لقيمته مقلوعاً أن لو قدر البناء أو الغرس مقلوعاً موضوعاً في الأرض بأن يقدر الغرس حطباً والبناء أجراً أو البناء حجارة مكومة على الأرض فيقوم وحده من غير أن يضم إلى الأرض فيضمن له قيمة الحطب والحجارة المكومة دون المبنية.
He (the author) said: (If he plants or builds in another's land, he must remove [it] and return [the land]) i.e., remove the building and planting and return the land to its owner because of his saying (PBUH): 'There is no right for a wrongful root' i.e., the root is wrongful, describing the root with the attribute of its owner, which is injustice, and it is metaphorical, as it is said 'fasting by day, standing by night'. Allah said: 'Therein, every wise matter is distinguished' (Quran 44:4). And because the land remains on his ownership, as it is not consumed nor truly usurped, and there is nothing that causes ownership for the usurper, so he is ordered to vacate it and return it to its owner, just as if he occupies another's container with food. This is if the value of the timber is more than the value of the building; if the value of the building is more, the usurper may guarantee him the value of the timber and take it, mentioned in al-Nihayah. And likewise, if a chicken swallows a pearl, one looks at which is more valuable; its owner may take it and guarantee the value of the other. And likewise, if someone brings another's calf into his house and it grows large and cannot be removed except by demolishing the wall. And likewise, if an ox inserts its head into a copper pot and cannot be removed. We have comprehensively covered this issue with its branches in the issue of loss of land, so we will not repeat it. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: 'If someone usurps wheat and plants it, he gives the excess in charity.' He (the author) said: (If the land decreases [in value] by removal, he guarantees to him the building and planting as removed, and they become his) meaning, if the land decreases due to removal, the owner of the land may guarantee to the usurper the value of the building and planting as removed, and they become his because this prevents harm to both, so consideration for both is determined. He only guarantees its value as removed because it is subject to removal and he cannot keep it, so its value at that time is considered as removed. The method of knowing it: The land is valued with the building or trees on it and subject to removal (i.e., ordered to be removed), and it is valued alone without the building or planting; then he guarantees the difference between the two. Thus they said. And this is not a guarantee of its value as removed, but rather a guarantee of its value as standing but subject to removal. It would be a guarantee of its value as removed if the building or planting is estimated as removed and placed on the ground, e.g., the planting estimated as firewood and the building as rubble or the building as stones piled on the ground; then it is valued alone without being attached to the land, so he guarantees the value of the firewood and piled stones, not the built ones.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from hadith and Quranic analogy regarding a usurper who plants or builds on another's land. The basic ruling: the usurper must remove his structures and return the land to its owner. The proof is the hadith 'There is no right for a wrongful root', meaning the plant that is wrongfully placed has no right to remain. The Quranic verse is used metaphorically: just as Allah distinguishes every matter, so must we distinguish between the land owner's right and the usurper's act. Since the land remains owned by its original owner, the usurper is ordered to vacate. The text also covers the case where the value of the building exceeds the value of the land: the usurper may pay the land value and take the building. Analogous cases are given, such as a chicken swallowing a pearl, or a calf growing inside a house, or an ox's head stuck in a pot. The method of valuation is explained: if removal damages the land, the land owner can guarantee the value of the building/planting as removed (i.e., as materials) and become the owner of those materials. The valuation is done by comparing the land value with and without the structure, and the difference is guaranteed. It is clarified that this is not exactly the value as removed, but the value as standing but subject to removal.

Key Terms
قلع (qal'): Uprooting or demolishing the structure (building or planting) and removing it from the land.
عرق ظالم ('irq ẓālim): Literally 'oppressive root'; a plant or structure placed wrongfully on another's property.
ضمان (daman): Financial guarantee or compensation for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurper builds a house on someone else's land, and the land is damaged when the building is removed, what is the ruling?
A) The land owner must accept the building as compensation.
B) The usurper must compensate for the damage to the land and the building remains.
C) The usurper removes the building and compensates for any decrease in land value, and the land owner may guarantee the value of the removed materials.
D) The land owner must pay the usurper for the building and keep it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires physical removal of structures placed on usurped land, rooted in the hadith of the wrongful root.

Owner's Options When Usurper Dyes or Mixes (Hanafi vs. Shafi'i)
Ruling on owner's options with Shafi'i comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the owner's choice when a usurper dyes a garment or mixes sawiq with oil, and compare it with the Shafi'i position, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The owner of the original gets priority: he may claim the value of his original or take the combined item and pay the increase.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common problem in property law: what happens when a usurper adds value to a wrongfully taken item? The Hanafi school gives the original owner a powerful choice, while the Shafi'i school insists on physical separation if possible. Understanding the basis for these positions sharpens your appreciation of how Hanafi usul (qiyas and istihsan) operates in real cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the two options the owner has for both garment dyeing and sawiq mixing.
The owner can either take the value of a white garment (or like of sawiq) separately, or take the dyed/mixed item and pay the increase.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
State al-Shafi'i's view: the usurper must remove the dye by washing and compensate any loss; for oil (mixing) removal is impossible, so the item must be sold and the price divided.
Note the distinction Shafi'i makes between dye (removable) and oil (inseparable).
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand why the Hanafi school rejects removal: dye dissolves in washing and cannot be returned intact, so the owner of the original gets choice. Compare with the building case where demolition is possible.
The key difference: dye is an attribute that disappears upon removal; a building remains after demolition.
4
Examine the valuation differences
Recognize that sawiq and oil are fungibles (mithliyyat) – the guarantee is by like (mithl). Dye and garment are qimiyyat – guarantee is by value (qima).
Also note the intra-Hanafi difference on black dye: according to Imam it is a loss, according to the Sahibayn it is an increase.
5
Appreciate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, the owner has the choice whether to take the combined item or not; in the Shafi'i school, the owner must take the item after the usurper's attempt to remove the dye, and the risk of loss falls on the usurper.
This difference affects the allocation of risk and the burden of compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن صبغ أو لت السويق بسمن ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض ومثل السويق أو أخذهما وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن) يعني إذا غصب ثوباً وصبغه أو سويقاً فلته فالمالك بالخيار بسمن إن شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض ومثل السويق، وإن شاء أخذ المصبوغ والملتوت وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن. وقال الإمام الشافعي يؤمر الغاصب بقلع الصبغ بالغسل بقدر الإمكان ويسلمه لصاحبه وإن انتقص قيمة الثوب بذلك فعليه لضمان النقصان بخلاف السمن لتعذر التمييز . ولنا أن الصبغ مال متقوم كالثوب وبجنايته لا يسقط تقوم ماله فيجب ضمانه حقهما ما أمكن فكان صاحب الثوب أولى بالتخيير لأنه صاحب الأصل والآخر صاحب وصف وهو قائم بالأصل، وكذا السويق أصل والسمن تبع بخلاف البناء لأن التمييز ممكن بالنقص وله وجود بعد النقص فأمكن إيصال حق كل واحد منهما إليه والصبغ يتلاشى بالغسل فلا يمكن إيصاله إلى صاحبه، وبخلاف ما إذا انصبغ من غير فعل أحد كإلقاء الريح حيث لا يثبت فيه لرب الثوب الخيار بل يؤمر صاحب الثوب بتملك الصبغ بقيمته وظاهر العبارة انحصار الحكم فيما ذكر. وقال أبو عصمة في مسألة الغصب : إن شاء رب الثوب باعه فيضرب كل واحد منهما بقيمة ماله وهذا وجه حسن في وصول حق كل واحد منهما إلى صاحبه وتتأتى بغرامة يضمن فيها مثل هذا فيما إذا كان انصبغ بنفسه أيضاً. والجواب في اللت كالجواب في البصغ أنه يضمن مثل السويق وفي الصبغ قيمته لأن السويق والسمن من ذوات الأمثال بخلاف الصبغ والثوب وقال في الكافي قال في المبسوط : يضمن قيمة سويقه لأنه يتفاوت القلي فلم يكن مثلياً كالخبز، وما روي عن الإمام أنه إذا صبغ الثوب اسود فهو نقصان، وعندهما زيادة كالخمرة والصفرة راجع إلى اختلاف عصر وزمان فإن بني أمية في زمانه كانوا
He (the author) said: (And if he dyes or mixes sawiq with oil, he guarantees the value of a white garment and the like of sawiq, or he takes both and pays the increase of the dye and oil) meaning if he usurps a garment and dyes it, or sawiq and mixes it with oil, the owner has the choice: if he wishes, he guarantees the value of a white garment and the like of sawiq; if he wishes, he takes the dyed garment and the mixed sawiq and pays the increase of the dye and oil. Imam al-Shafi'i said: 'The usurper is ordered to remove the dye by washing as much as possible and return it to its owner; if the value of the garment decreases thereby, he is liable for the loss, unlike the oil because distinguishing is impossible.' Our proof: The dye is a valuable property like the garment; by his offense, the value of his property does not fall, so he must guarantee his right as much as possible. Thus the garment owner is more entitled to the choice because he is the owner of the original, and the other is the owner of an attribute that is attached to the original. Likewise, sawiq is the original and oil is subordinate, unlike the building because distinguishing is possible by demolition and it has existence after demolition, so it is possible to deliver each owner's right. Dye dissolves with washing, so it cannot be delivered to its owner. And unlike the case where it becomes dyed without anyone's action, like wind blowing, where the garment owner does not have a choice; rather, the garment owner is ordered to take ownership of the dye at its value. The apparent expression confines the ruling to what was mentioned. Abu 'Isma said in the issue of usurpation: 'If the garment owner wishes, he may sell it, and each takes the value of his property.' This is a good way to deliver each owner's right, and it can be achieved by a fine in which the like is guaranteed in the case where it becomes dyed by itself. The answer for mixing is the same as for dyeing: he guarantees the like of sawiq, and for dyeing its value, because sawiq and oil are fungibles (mithliyyat), unlike dye and garment. Al-Kafi said, quoting al-Mabsut: 'He guarantees the value of his sawiq because it varies when roasted, so it is not fungible like bread.' And what is related from the Imam that if the garment is dyed black, it is a loss; according to the two companions, it is an increase like redness and yellowness, referring to difference of era and time, for the Umayyads in his time were...
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding a usurper who dyes a garment or mixes sawiq (a type of food) with oil. The Hanafi ruling: the owner has two options: (1) claim the value of an undyed white garment plus one mithl (like quantity) of sawiq, or (2) take the dyed garment and mixed sawiq and pay the increase in value due to the dye/oil. The Shafi'i position is that the usurper must remove the dye by washing if possible, and if the value decreases, he compensates the loss; for oil, removal is impossible, so the owner must sell and divide proceeds. The Hanafi response argues that dye is a separate valuable property, and since it is inseparable from the garment, the owner of the original (garment) gets the choice. The analogy with building is distinguished because a building can be separated by demolition. The passage also discusses the valuation: sawiq and oil are fungibles (mithliyyat) so like is guaranteed; dye and garment are not, so value is guaranteed. A minority view (Abu 'Isma) allows selling the combined item and dividing proceeds. There is also an internal Hanafi difference: according to Imam, black dye is a loss; according to Sahibayn, it is an increase (like red or yellow). The final sentence is cut off, indicating the difference arises from customs of the time.

Key Terms
الصبغ (al-ṣabgh): Dye; a valuable substance that becomes an attribute of the garment after application.
اللت (al-latt): Mixing a dry substance (like sawiq) with a liquid (like oil) to form a paste or mixture.
ذوات الأمثال (dhawāt al-amthāl): Fungible goods; items that can be replaced by similar items of the same type and quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a usurper dyes a white garment black, what options does the owner have?
A) The owner must take the black garment and pay for the dye.
B) The owner may either claim the value of a white garment plus the value of the dye, or take the black garment and pay the increase in value due to the dye.
C) The owner must force the usurper to remove the dye by washing.
D) The owner has no claim because the garment is still his.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a usurper adds a valuable attribute to a usurped item, the owner of the original may choose either to claim the value of his original separately or to take the combined item and pay the increase.

Ruling on Building/Planting on Usurped Land: Compensation When Removal Damages Land
Explains the ruling when land is damaged by removing usurper's building/planting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the landowner's option when the usurper's building or planting damages the land upon removal, and explain the method of valuation.

Core Idea
The landowner may take the usurper's building/planting and pay its value as removed, calculated by land value difference.

When a usurper builds or plants on someone else's land, the landowner has the right to reclaim the land. But what happens if removing the structure damages the land? The Hanafi school provides a balanced solution: the landowner may take the removed structure and compensate the usurper for its value at the time of removal. This prevents both parties from suffering harm. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the precise valuation method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the problem
Recognize the case: a usurper (ghasib) builds or plants on another's land. The landowner wants the land back, but removal causes the land's value to diminish.
The core issue is how to compensate the usurper for the building/planting without unjustly enriching the landowner.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to the text, the landowner has the option to 'guarantee' the building/planting uprooted (i.e., take ownership) and pay the usurper its value as uprooted.
This option exists only if the land actually diminishes in value by removal.
3
Understand the valuation method
The value is not determined by separately appraising the materials as firewood or rubble. Instead, it is the difference between the land's value with the structure standing (subject to removal) and the land's value empty.
The commentator emphasizes that this is a guarantee of the standing structure's value, not the literal value of uprooted materials.
4
Distinguish from alternative valuation
The text contrasts this with a true 'value uprooted' calculation (e.g., estimating firewood or piled stones). The Hanafi method is a practical calculation to avoid injustice.
This distinction prevents confusion and ensures the compensation is fair to both parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (فإن نقصت الأرض بالقلع ضمن له البناء والغرس مقلوعاً ويكونان (له أي إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع كان لصاحب الأرض أن يضمن للغاصب قيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعاً ويكونان له لأن فيه دفع الضرر عنهما فتعين فيه النظر لهما، وإنما يضمن قيمتها مقلوعاً لأنه مستحق للقلع وليس له يستديم فيها فتعتبر قيمته في ذلك الوقت مقلوعاً وكيفية معرفتها أنه يقوم الأرض وبها بناء أو شجر ويستحق قلعه أي أمر بقلعه وتقوم وحدها ليس فيها بناء ولا غرس فيضمن فضل ما بينهما، كذا قالوا وهذا ليس بضمان لقيمته مقلوعاً بل هو ضمان لقيمته قائماً مستحق القلع، وإنما يكون ضماناً لقيمته مقلوعاً أن لو قدر البناء أو الغرس مقلوعاً موضوعاً في الأرض بأن يقدر الغرس حطباً والبناء أجراً أو البناء حجارة مكومة على الأرض فيقوم وحده من غير أن يضم إلى الأرض فيضمن له قيمة الحطب والحجارة المكومة دون المبنية.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If the land diminishes by uprooting, he guarantees the building and planting uprooted, and they become his) meaning if the land diminishes by uprooting, the landowner may guarantee to the usurper the value of the building and planting uprooted, and they become his, because therein is repelling harm from them both, so consideration for them both is determined. He only guarantees their value uprooted because he is entitled to uprooting and may not keep them in the land, so their value is considered at that time uprooted. The method of knowing it is that the land is valued with building or trees, and he is entitled to its uprooting, i.e., ordered to uproot it, and the land alone is valued without building or planting, and he guarantees the difference between them. This is what they said, and this is not a guarantee of its value uprooted, but rather a guarantee of its value standing while entitled to uprooting. It would be a guarantee of its value uprooted only if the building or planting were estimated as uprooted and placed on the ground – such as estimating the planting as firewood and the building as rent, or the building as stones piled on the ground – then it is valued alone without being added to the land, and he guarantees the value of the firewood and piled stones, not the built stones.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation on the compensation for building and planting on usurped land. The author clarifies the ruling from the matn: if the land is damaged by removal, the landowner has the option to take the building/planting (uprooted) and compensate the usurper for their value as removed items. This prevents harm to both parties. The valuation method is to compare the land's value with and without the structures, and the difference is the compensation. However, this is not a true 'value uprooted' in the sense of separately appraising firewood or stones; rather, it is the value of the standing structure that must be removed. The commentator distinguishes between the two valuation methods to avoid confusion.

Key Terms
مقلوعاً (maqlu'an): Uprooted or removed; referring to the building/planting after being taken out of the ground.
ضمن (damina): To guarantee or be liable for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurper builds a house on usurped land, and removing the house damages the land, what option does the landowner have?
A) The landowner must let the building remain and claim rent.
B) The landowner may take the building after it is removed and pay the usurper its value as removed.
C) The landowner may force the usurper to destroy the building at his own cost.
D) The landowner has no recourse and must accept the damaged land.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view balances harm by allowing the landowner to take the structure while paying its calculated removal value based on land value difference.

Usurper's Alteration: Owner's Choice vs. Restoration
Hanafi ruling on usurper altering property: owner's option vs Shafi'i requirement of restoration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a usurper who alters property and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument concerning the remedy.

Core Idea
Owner chooses: claim original value or accept altered item and pay for improvement.

When a usurper alters property—such as dyeing a garment or mixing sawiq with ghee—a dispute arises over how to restore the owner's rights. The Hanafi school gives the owner a choice, while the Shafi'i school insists on reversing the alteration if possible. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate the owner's two options: claim the value of the unaltered original, or take the altered item and pay the increase in value due to the alteration.
The text uses 'أو أخذهما وغرم ما زاد'—the owner may take both and pay the increase.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the usurper must remove the alteration (e.g., wash out dye) as much as possible and compensate any loss in value.
For mixing ghee, removal is impossible, so a different rule applies in Shafi'i view.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument is based on the principle that the original property should be restored as closely as possible; the usurper bears the cost of restoration.
Distinguish between removable alterations (building on land) and non-removable ones (dye).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply argues that dye is valuable property that disintegrates upon washing, making return impossible. Giving the owner the choice balances the rights of both owner (original) and usurper (improvement).
Contrast with natural dyeing (wind) where no choice exists—owner must pay for dye.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the owner may end up paying for the dye; under Shafi'i, the usurper bears the cost of removal and any deficiency.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; both schools have sound dalil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن صبغ أو لت السويق بسمن ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض ومثل السويق أو أخذهما وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن) يعني إذا غصب ثوباً وصبغه أو سويقاً فلته فالمالك بالخيار بسمن إن شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض ومثل السويق، وإن شاء أخذ المصبوغ والملتوت وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن. وقال الإمام الشافعي يؤمر الغاصب بقلع الصبغ بالغسل بقدر الإمكان ويسلمه لصاحبه وإن انتقص قيمة الثوب بذلك فعليه لضمان النقصان بخلاف السمن لتعذر التمييز . ولنا أن الصبغ مال متقوم كالثوب وبجنايته لا يسقط تقوم ماله فيجب ضمانه حقهما ما أمكن فكان صاحب الثوب أولى بالتخيير لأنه صاحب الأصل والآخر صاحب وصف وهو قائم بالأصل، وكذا السويق أصل والسمن تبع بخلاف البناء لأن التمييز ممكن بالنقص وله وجود بعد النقص فأمكن إيصال حق كل واحد منهما إليه والصبغ يتلاشى بالغسل فلا يمكن إيصاله إلى صاحبه، وبخلاف ما إذا انصبغ من غير فعل أحد كإلقاء الريح حيث لا يثبت فيه لرب الثوب الخيار بل يؤمر صاحب الثوب بتملك الصبغ بقيمته وظاهر العبارة انحصار الحكم فيما ذكر.
And if he dyes or mixes sawiq with ghee, he guarantees the value of a white garment and the like of sawiq, or he takes them both and pays the increase of the dye and ghee. Meaning: if he usurps a garment and dyes it, or sawiq and mixes it with ghee, the owner has the choice: if he wishes, he may claim the value of a white garment and the like of sawiq; if he wishes, he may take the dyed [garment] and the mixed [sawiq] and pay the increase of the dye and ghee. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The usurper is ordered to remove the dye by washing as much as possible and hand it over to its owner, and if the value of the garment decreases thereby, he must guarantee the deficiency, contrary to the ghee because distinguishing is impossible. Our evidence: dye is valuable property like the garment, and by his wrongdoing his property value does not fall, so guaranteeing the rights of both is required as much as possible; thus the owner of the garment is more entitled to the choice because he is the owner of the original, and the other is the owner of an attribute that subsists in the original. Similarly, sawiq is the original and ghee is subsidiary, unlike building because distinguishing is possible through damage and it has existence after damage, so it is possible to deliver the right of each to him. Dye disintegrates with washing so it cannot be delivered to its owner. And unlike the case where it becomes dyed without anyone's action, like wind throwing [it], where the garment owner has no choice; rather he is ordered to own the dye at its value. The apparent wording restricts the ruling to what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding a usurper who alters the property. The Hanafi position gives the owner a choice: either claim compensation for the original value (like an unblemished garment and unmixed sawiq) or take the altered item and pay the usurper for the increase in value due to the alteration. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the usurper must try to undo the alteration (e.g., wash out the dye) and compensate for any loss. The Hanafi response is that dye is valuable and cannot be returned intact after washing, so the owner should have the choice to either claim compensation or take the altered item and pay for the improvement. The commentator distinguishes this from building on land, where removal is possible, and from cases of natural dyeing where no one acted, where the owner must pay for the dye. The ruling is specific to intentional usurpation.

Key Terms
السويق (al-sawiq): A type of food made from roasted barley or wheat, often ground. Here it is an item that can be mixed with ghee.
اللت (al-latt): Mixing (especially a dry substance with a liquid). Here, mixing sawiq with ghee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a usurper dyes a white garment black, what options does the owner have?
A) The owner must accept the dyed garment and pay for the dye.
B) The owner can demand the garment be washed to remove the dye.
C) The owner can choose to claim the value of a white garment or take the dyed garment and pay the increase in value due to dye.
D) The owner can demand compensation for the full value of the dyed garment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a usurper who alters property does not automatically own the improvement; the owner retains the right to choose compensation or acceptance with payment.

Valuation of Dye and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Black Dye
Refines the valuation rule for dye when it increases or decreases value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on compensating for dye when it decreases a garment's value, and evaluate the apparent dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding black dye.

Core Idea
Actual market effect, not color, determines dye compensation; the Lawgiver balances rights to prevent unjust enrichment.

This passage refines the valuation rule for dye when it increases or decreases the value of a garment. We encounter an apparent intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa considered black dye a decrease, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) considered it an increase. The commentator resolves this by noting the historical context (Umayyad vs. Abbasid fashion). Then, a detailed compensatory mechanism from Imam Muhammad is presented for the case where the dye actually reduces the garment's value, leading to a set-off between the owner's claim for deficiency and the usurper's claim for the dye's potential value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa said black dye is a decrease; the Sahibayn said it is an increase. The commentator resolves that this difference is due to different eras (Umayyad forbade black, Abbasid wore black), so there is no real substantive dispute. The true criterion is the actual increase or decrease in market value.
This shows how social context can affect rulings without changing the underlying principle.
2
Understand Muhammad's case when dye decreases value
Garment originally worth 30 dirhams becomes worth 20 after dye (decrease of 10). The dye would increase a suitable garment by 5 dirhams. The owner takes his garment and receives 5 dirhams from the usurper (net after offsetting the 5 increase against the 10 deficiency), and the usurper owes the remaining 5 dirhams.
The calculation: owner's claim (10) minus dye's value (5) = net 5 owed to owner.
3
Analyze the commentator's objection and resolution
Ibn Nujaym objects: how can the usurper claim anything when he caused the loss? And how can destruction waive liability? He resolves by stating that the Lawgiver balances rights to prevent the usurper's dye from being wasted for free; the dye has intrinsic value that must be considered.
The objection is not a fatwa but a methodological reflection; the rule stands.
4
Apply the principle to other cases
The key principle is that compensation for dye is based on actual market effect, not fixed color. If the dye increases value, the increase is credited to the usurper; if it decreases, the deficiency is deducted from the dye's value, and any remaining value is paid to the usurper by the owner.
This prevents unjust enrichment on either side.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الكافي قال في المبسوط : يضمن قيمة سويقه لأنه يتفاوت القلي فلم يكن مثلياً كالخبز، وما روي عن الإمام أنه إذا صبغ الثوب اسود فهو نقصان، وعندهما زيادة كالخمرة والصفرة راجع إلى اختلاف عصر وزمان فإن بني أمية في زمانه كانوا يمنعون عن لبس السواد وفي زمانهما بنو العباس كانوا يلبسون السواد ولا خلاف في الحقيقة ولهذا لم يتعرض في المختصر للون الصبغ لأن من الثياب ما يزاد بالسواد ومنها ما ينقص والمعتبر هو الزيادة والنقصان حقيقة، فلو صبغه فنقصه الصبغ بأن كانت قيمته ثلاثين درهماً فرجعت بالصبغ إلى عشرين، فعند محمد ينظر إلى ثوب يزيد فيه ذلك الصبغ، فإن كانت الزيادة خمسة يأخذ رب الثوب ثوبه وخمسة لأن صاحب الثوب وجب له على الغاصب ضمان نقصان قيمة ثوبه عشرة ووجب عليه للغاصب قيمة صبغة لا خمسة فالخمسة بالخمسة النقصان قصاصاً ويرجع عليه بما بقي وهو خمسة؛ رواه هشام عن محمد : قال الشارح : وهو مشكل من حيث إن المغصوب منه لم يصل إليه المغصوب كله وإنما وصل إليه بعضه وكان من حقه أن يطالب إلى تمام حقه فكيف يتوجه عليه الطلب وهو لم ينتفع بالصبغ شيئاً ولم يحصل له به إلا تلف ماله؟ وكيف يسقط عن الغاصب بعض قيمة المغصوب بالإتلاف والإتلاف مقرر لوجوب جميع القيمة فكيف صار مسقطاً له هنا؟ ولك أن تقول : لا إشكال لأن الشارع ناظر إلى حق كل منهما فلو ألزمناه أن يدفع الشعرة ضاع مال الغاصب وهو الصبغ مجاناً وذلك ظلم والظالم لا يظلم فأوجبناها على رب الثوب فوصل إلى المغصوب منه كما ذكر كل حقه ما عليه وما بقي له وكون الإتلاف مقرراً لا ينافي كونه مسقطاً لأن الإتلاف بالنظر إلى النقصان والإسقاط بالنظر إلى عين الصبغ فتأمل .
And said in al-Kafi, said in al-Mabsut: He guarantees the value of his sawiq because it varies in roasting, so it is not fungible like bread. And what is narrated from Imam [Abu Hanifa] that if he dyes the garment black, it is a decrease, while according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] it is an increase, like red and yellow, refers to difference of era and time, because the Umayyads in his time used to forbid wearing black, while in their time the Abbasids wore black, and there is no real dispute. For this reason, al-Mukhtasar did not mention the color of the dye, because some garments increase with black and some decrease, and what matters is the actual increase or decrease. So if he dyes it and the dye reduces it, e.g., its value was thirty dirhams and it became twenty with the dye, then according to Muhammad, one looks at a garment in which that dye would increase; if the increase is five, the garment owner takes his garment and five, because the garment owner has a claim against the usurper for the decrease of ten, and he owes the usurper the value of his dye, which is not five but actually the increase is five, so the five offsets the deficiency, and the usurper is owed the remaining five. Hisham narrated from Muhammad. The commentator said: This is problematic because the victim of usurpation did not receive all his property but only part, and he should claim his full right; how can a claim be made against him when he did not benefit from the dye at all and only suffered loss? And how can some of the value of the usurped property be waived due to destruction, when destruction establishes full liability? How does it become a waiver here? But you may say: There is no problem, because the Lawgiver considers the right of both; if we obliged him to pay the hair, the usurper's property (the dye) would be wasted for free, which is injustice, and the unjust are not wronged. So we impose it on the garment owner, and thus each receives his right as mentioned, and the destruction being established does not prevent it being a waiver, because destruction is considered in terms of decrease, and waiver is considered in terms of the dye itself. So reflect.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage refines the valuation of dye by addressing intra-Hanafi differences. Imam Abu Hanifa considered black dye a decrease, while the Sahibayn considered it an increase due to changing fashion (Umayyad vs. Abbasid). The real criterion is actual market effect. Then a complex case from Muhammad: if the dye reduces the garment's value, the owner takes the garment plus the amount the dye would have increased a suitable garment (using the dye's potential), leading to a set-off. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) flags a problem: how can the usurper claim anything when his act caused loss? He resolves it by noting that the dye is valuable in itself; the Lawgiver balances rights to prevent unjust enrichment. This is a critical refinement of the application.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Set-off or offsetting mutual claims; here the five dirhams offset the deficiency.
الشعرة (al-sha'rah): Literally 'hair'; in context, a metaphor for a small amount or the dye itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a usurper dyes a garment with a dye that in fact reduces its value, how is compensation calculated under Muhammad's view?
A) The owner pays nothing and takes the garment.
B) The owner takes the garment and pays the usurper the value of the dye without offset.
C) The owner takes the garment and pays the amount the dye would have increased a suitable garment, offset against the deficiency.
D) The usurper must pay the owner the full original value and keep the garment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school evaluates dye compensation based on actual market effect, not fixed color, and the Lawgiver balances rights to prevent unjust enrichment.

Mixing Usurped Dirhams: Abu Yusuf vs. Sahibayn
Ruling on mixing usurped coins with the usurper's own coins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on mixing usurped dirhams and apply the ruling of Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn to a given scenario.

Core Idea
When usurped coins are mixed, the victim's choice depends on the ratio — Abu Yusuf vs. Sahibayn differ.

This passage from al-Muntaqa presents a question to Imam Muhammad about mixing usurped dirhams with the usurper's own. The answer reveals an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on quantity, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) give the victim unconditional choice. Understanding this ikhtilaf helps you grasp how Hanafi jurists determine when mixing constitutes consumption (istihlak) and what remedies the victim has.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the question
Hisham asks Muhammad about a man who usurped 1000 dirhams and mixed them with his own dirhams. Identify the key variables: the usurper's coins vs. the victim's coins.
The ratio matters in Abu Yusuf's view.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf distinguishes two sub-cases: (1) if the usurper's added coins are more, the mixture is consumed (istihlak) and the usurper must guarantee the victim's dirhams; (2) if the usurper's coins are fewer, the victim has a choice to claim his dirhams or become a partner.
When equal, Abu Yusuf's position is unknown.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
According to both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the victim always has the choice, regardless of the proportion of mixed coins.
This is reported as 'qawluhuma' (their view).
4
Identify the point of difference
The contested issue is the definition of istihlak: does mixing with a larger amount render the victim's property consumed, removing the option of partnership?
Abu Yusuf's view ties istihlak to quantity; the Sahibayn do not.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, if the usurper's coins are more, the victim cannot claim a share of the mixture; under the Sahibayn, the victim can always claim a share.
This affects the remedy in cases where the usurper has added more coins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى قال هشام: سألت محمداً عن رجل غصب من رجل ألف درهم وخلط بها دراهم من ماله قال : مذهب أبي يوسف في هذا الباب أن دراهم المخالط إذا كانت أكثر فهو مستهلك وضمن الدراهم المغصوب. وإن كانت دراهم الخالط أقل فالمغصوب منه بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه دراهمه، وإن شاء شاركه بالمخلوط بقدر دراهمه. قلت: فإن كانا سواء فما مذهب أبي يوسف؟ قال: لا أدري، وأما في قولهما فالمغصوب منه بالخيار على كل حال إن شاء ضمن الغاصب دراهمه، وإن شاء كان شريكاً فيهما.
And in al-Muntaqa, Hisham said: I asked Muhammad about a man who usurped from another a thousand dirhams and mixed with them dirhams from his own property. He said: The madhhab of Abu Yusuf in this chapter is that if the mixed dirhams are more, then he is a consumer (mustahlik) and guarantees the usurped dirhams. If the mixer's dirhams are less, then the victim has the choice: if he wishes, he may claim his dirhams, and if he wishes, he may become a partner in the mixture according to his dirhams. I said: If they are equal, what is the madhhab of Abu Yusuf? He said: I do not know. As for the view of them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the victim has the choice in any case: if he wishes, he may claim his dirhams from the usurper, and if he wishes, he may become a partner in both.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage addresses intra-Hanafi differences on mixing usurped dirhams with the usurper's own. The questioner Hisham asks Muhammad al-Shaybani about the ruling. Abu Yusuf's view distinguishes based on quantity: if the usurper's added coins are more, the mixture is considered consumed (istihlak), and the usurper must pay back the exact usurped coins. If the usurper's coins are fewer, the victim may either claim his coins or become a partner in the mixture proportional to his share. When equal, Abu Yusuf's position is unknown. According to both Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the victim always has the choice, regardless of proportion. This shows a difference within the Hanafi school in determining when mixing amounts to consumption (istihlak).

Key Terms
مستهلك (mustahlik): One who consumes the property, making it impossible to return the same items; here, when the usurper's coins outnumber the victim's, the mixture is considered consumed.
شراكة (shirakah): Partnership; here, the victim becomes co-owner of the mixture proportional to his contribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Abu Yusuf's position when the mixed dirhams are equal in quantity?
A) The victim has a choice.
B) The victim must accept partnership.
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani says 'I do not know'.
D) The usurper is considered a consumer.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, mixing usurped property with one's own can lead to either forced partnership or compensation, depending on the quantity and the jurist's view of istihlak.
