Liability When Your Act Causes Accidental Mixing
Rulings on fault-based accidental mixing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the liable party and state the compensation rule (like-for-like or options) when a person's accidental act causes his property to mix with another's.

Core Idea
If your act causes your property to mix with another's, you are liable for his loss—even accidentally.

This lesson examines cases where a person's act, like spilling his saweeq or pouring inferior goods, causes mixing. The Hanafi school holds the actor liable, with specific rules depending on whether the substance is mithli or qimi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the substances
In each case, note who performed the action (e.g., owner of saweeq spilled) and what mixed (e.g., saweeq and ghee).
Act must be the direct cause of mixing.
2
Determine liability based on causation
The one whose act caused the consumption (istihlak) of the other's property is liable.
3
Apply compensation rule per substance type
For mithli (commutable) goods like ghee, pay like (mithl). For qimi goods like lime, the victim has options: take deficient saweeq and demand like lime, or make the other liable for like of his saweeq.
Options only for qimi goods.
4
Handle variant cases
Pouring inferior onto good: actor guarantees like the good and becomes a partner. Pouring food onto food: if more, liable; if less, not liable (consumer).
Principle of proportionality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ولم غيب المغصوب وضمن قيمته ملكه والقول في القيمة للغاصب مع يمينه والبينة . الحكم كذلك، ولهذا روى هشام عن محمد فقال : إذا كان مع رجل سويق ومع رجل آخر أو زيت فاصطدما فانصب زيت هذا أو سمنه في سويق هذا، فإن صب السويق يضمن سمن لصاحب السمن أو الزيت مثل سمنه أو زيته لأن صاحب السويق استهلك سمن يستهلك صاحب السمن سويق هذا أو سمنه في سويق هذا فإن صاحب السويق يضمن لصاحب السمن ولأن هذا زيادة في السويق. وإن كان أحدهما سويق ومع الآخر نورة مع أخذ فاصطدما فانصب سويق هذا في نورة هذا فإن شاء صاحب السويق ناقصاً سويقه وأعطى الآخر مثل النورة، وإن شاء ضمن صاحب النورة مثل كيل سويقه وسلم سويقه إليه أو ضمن صاحب السويق لصاحب النور مثل كيل نورته وفي الذخيرة: وإذا فعل ذلك غيرهما وذهب فليس لصاحب النورة على صاحب السويق شيء والسويق لصاحب السويق . وفي الخانية : ولو اختلط نورة رجل بدقيق آخر بغير صنع أحد يباع المختلط ويضرب لكل واحد منهما بقيمة نصيبه مختلطاً لأن هذا نقصان حصل لا بفعل أحد فليس أحدهما بإيجاب النقصان عليه بأولى من الآخر. وفي جامع الجوامع : صب رديئاً على جيد ضمن مثل الجيد وإن كان قليلاً وكان شريكاً بقدر ما صب من الجنس فيه. وفي التجريد عن أبي يوسف فيمن طعاماً على طعام إن كان طعامه أكثر كان ضامناً، وإن كان طعامه أقل لم يكن ضامناً يصر مستهلكاً. وفي الخانية : رجل جاء إلى خلانسان وصب فيها خمراً وهما نصفان قال : لصاحب الخمر أن يأخذ نصف الخل وعن أبي القاسم رحمه الله تعالى : رجل غصب خمراً وجعلها في حبه وصب فيها خلاً . من عنده فصار الخمر خلاً قال : يكون الخمر للغاصب قياساً. وقال الفقيه أبو الليث رحمه الله تعالى : قيل الخل يكون بينهما على قدر حقهما لأنه صار كأنهما خلطا خلهما قال وبه نأخذ ؛ كذا في الأصل. وفي المنتقى عند محمد: رجل دراهم ينظر إليها فوقع بعضها في دراهم رجل فاختلط كان ضامناً لها والله تعالى أعلم.
This and not... The usurped object was hidden and he guaranteed its value, he owns it. The say regarding the value is for the usurper with his oath and the evidence. The ruling is likewise. For this reason Hisham narrated from Muhammad: If a man has saweeq and another man has oil or ghee, and they collide and the oil or ghee of this one spills into the saweeq of that one, then the one who spilled the saweeq guarantees ghee for the owner of the ghee or oil like his ghee or oil, because the owner of the saweeq consumed the ghee, and the owner of the ghee consumes the saweeq of this one. So the owner of the saweeq guarantees the owner of the ghee, and because this is an increase in the saweeq. And if one has saweeq and the other has lime with alkali, and they collide and the saweeq of this one spills into the lime of that one, then if the owner of the saweeq wishes, he takes his saweeq deficient and gives the other the like of the lime; and if he wishes, he makes the owner of the lime liable for the like of the measure of his saweeq and hands over his saweeq to him; or the owner of the saweeq is liable to the owner of the lime for the like of the measure of his lime. In al-Dhakhira: If someone else does that and leaves, then the owner of the lime has no claim against the owner of the saweeq, and the saweeq belongs to its owner. In al-Khaniyya: If the lime of a man mixes with the flour of another without any act, the mixture is sold and each is given a share according to the value of his portion mixed, because this is a loss occurring without anyone's act, so one is not more entitled to impose the loss on the other. In Jami' al-Jawami': If one pours inferior (stuff) onto good, he guarantees the like of the good even if it was little, and he becomes a partner to the extent of what he poured of the same type. In al-Tajrid from Abu Yusuf: Regarding a man (pouring) food onto food: if his food is more, he is liable; if his food is less, he is not liable, because he becomes a consumer. In al-Khaniyya: A man came to vinegar and poured wine into it, and they were half and half. He said: The owner of the wine may take half the vinegar. From Abu al-Qasim: A man usurped wine and put it in his container and poured vinegar from his own into it, and the wine turned into vinegar. He said: The wine belongs to the usurper by analogy. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: It was said the vinegar would be between them according to their shares because it became as if they mixed their vinegar. He said: And we take this. Thus in al-Asl. In al-Muntaqa from Muhammad: A man had dirhams looking at them, and some of them fell into the dirhams of another man and mixed, he is liable for them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings from Hanafi sources regarding accidental mixing of substances where the owner is not clearly at fault. The key principle is that liability follows causation: if one party's act causes the loss of another's property, he is liable to compensate. For example, if saweeq (a type of food) spills into ghee, the owner of the saweeq must compensate the owner of the ghee because his saweeq consumed the ghee (mixing made it inseparable). Conversely, if lime mixes with saweeq, the victim has options: either take his deficient saweeq and demand lime from the other, or make the other liable for the value of his saweeq. If mixing occurs without anyone's act (e.g., wind), the mixture is sold and proceeds divided by value. The passage also covers cases of pouring inferior onto good, and vinegar-wine mixtures. In the later case, Imam Muhammad rules the vinegar is shared proportionally, which is the relied-upon position. The overall principle is that accidental loss without a liable party is borne proportionally, while deliberate or negligent acts trigger full liability.

Key Terms
سويق (sawīq): A type of roasted barley or wheat flour, often mixed with oil or dates.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage to another's property.
نورة (nūrah): A depilatory lime paste, not a standard market commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's narration, if a man's saweeq spills into another's ghee, what must the saweeq owner do?
A) Pay the value of the ghee.
B) Give an equal amount of ghee.
C) Share the mixture equally.
D) Pay nothing.

Key Takeaway
Liability for accidental mixing follows causation: the actor compensates in kind for mithli goods or gives options for qimi goods.

No Liability When Mixing Occurs Without Human Act
Rulings on no-fault mixing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that accidental mixing without a liable party results in no liability, and the mixture is sold with proceeds divided by value.

Core Idea
If no one caused the mixing, the loss is shared proportionally by value.

When substances mix without any human act—e.g., wind or an unknown third party—liability cannot be assigned. The Hanafi school rules that the mixture is sold and each party receives a share according to the value of his portion. This prevents unfair imposition of loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify absence of human act
Check if the mixing occurred without any person's direct action (e.g., wind, unknown third party after leaving).
If a third party acted and fled, no liability on the owner.
2
Apply the rule of no liability
Since no one is at fault, neither party can claim compensation from the other.
The loss is treated as a joint loss.
3
Sell the mixture and divide proceeds
The mixture is sold as is, and each person receives money equal to the value of his original property in the mixture.
Division is by value, not volume.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ولم غيب المغصوب وضمن قيمته ملكه والقول في القيمة للغاصب مع يمينه والبينة . الحكم كذلك، ولهذا روى هشام عن محمد فقال : إذا كان مع رجل سويق ومع رجل آخر أو زيت فاصطدما فانصب زيت هذا أو سمنه في سويق هذا، فإن صب السويق يضمن سمن لصاحب السمن أو الزيت مثل سمنه أو زيته لأن صاحب السويق استهلك سمن يستهلك صاحب السمن سويق هذا أو سمنه في سويق هذا فإن صاحب السويق يضمن لصاحب السمن ولأن هذا زيادة في السويق. وإن كان أحدهما سويق ومع الآخر نورة مع أخذ فاصطدما فانصب سويق هذا في نورة هذا فإن شاء صاحب السويق ناقصاً سويقه وأعطى الآخر مثل النورة، وإن شاء ضمن صاحب النورة مثل كيل سويقه وسلم سويقه إليه أو ضمن صاحب السويق لصاحب النور مثل كيل نورته وفي الذخيرة: وإذا فعل ذلك غيرهما وذهب فليس لصاحب النورة على صاحب السويق شيء والسويق لصاحب السويق . وفي الخانية : ولو اختلط نورة رجل بدقيق آخر بغير صنع أحد يباع المختلط ويضرب لكل واحد منهما بقيمة نصيبه مختلطاً لأن هذا نقصان حصل لا بفعل أحد فليس أحدهما بإيجاب النقصان عليه بأولى من الآخر. وفي جامع الجوامع : صب رديئاً على جيد ضمن مثل الجيد وإن كان قليلاً وكان شريكاً بقدر ما صب من الجنس فيه. وفي التجريد عن أبي يوسف فيمن طعاماً على طعام إن كان طعامه أكثر كان ضامناً، وإن كان طعامه أقل لم يكن ضامناً يصر مستهلكاً. وفي الخانية : رجل جاء إلى خلانسان وصب فيها خمراً وهما نصفان قال : لصاحب الخمر أن يأخذ نصف الخل وعن أبي القاسم رحمه الله تعالى : رجل غصب خمراً وجعلها في حبه وصب فيها خلاً . من عنده فصار الخمر خلاً قال : يكون الخمر للغاصب قياساً. وقال الفقيه أبو الليث رحمه الله تعالى : قيل الخل يكون بينهما على قدر حقهما لأنه صار كأنهما خلطا خلهما قال وبه نأخذ ؛ كذا في الأصل. وفي المنتقى عند محمد: رجل دراهم ينظر إليها فوقع بعضها في دراهم رجل فاختلط كان ضامناً لها والله تعالى أعلم.
This and not... The usurped object was hidden and he guaranteed its value, he owns it. The say regarding the value is for the usurper with his oath and the evidence. The ruling is likewise. For this reason Hisham narrated from Muhammad: If a man has saweeq and another man has oil or ghee, and they collide and the oil or ghee of this one spills into the saweeq of that one, then the one who spilled the saweeq guarantees ghee for the owner of the ghee or oil like his ghee or oil, because the owner of the saweeq consumed the ghee, and the owner of the ghee consumes the saweeq of this one. So the owner of the saweeq guarantees the owner of the ghee, and because this is an increase in the saweeq. And if one has saweeq and the other has lime with alkali, and they collide and the saweeq of this one spills into the lime of that one, then if the owner of the saweeq wishes, he takes his saweeq deficient and gives the other the like of the lime; and if he wishes, he makes the owner of the lime liable for the like of the measure of his saweeq and hands over his saweeq to him; or the owner of the saweeq is liable to the owner of the lime for the like of the measure of his lime. In al-Dhakhira: If someone else does that and leaves, then the owner of the lime has no claim against the owner of the saweeq, and the saweeq belongs to its owner. In al-Khaniyya: If the lime of a man mixes with the flour of another without any act, the mixture is sold and each is given a share according to the value of his portion mixed, because this is a loss occurring without anyone's act, so one is not more entitled to impose the loss on the other. In Jami' al-Jawami': If one pours inferior (stuff) onto good, he guarantees the like of the good even if it was little, and he becomes a partner to the extent of what he poured of the same type. In al-Tajrid from Abu Yusuf: Regarding a man (pouring) food onto food: if his food is more, he is liable; if his food is less, he is not liable, because he becomes a consumer. In al-Khaniyya: A man came to vinegar and poured wine into it, and they were half and half. He said: The owner of the wine may take half the vinegar. From Abu al-Qasim: A man usurped wine and put it in his container and poured vinegar from his own into it, and the wine turned into vinegar. He said: The wine belongs to the usurper by analogy. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: It was said the vinegar would be between them according to their shares because it became as if they mixed their vinegar. He said: And we take this. Thus in al-Asl. In al-Muntaqa from Muhammad: A man had dirhams looking at them, and some of them fell into the dirhams of another man and mixed, he is liable for them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings from Hanafi sources regarding accidental mixing of substances where the owner is not clearly at fault. The key principle is that liability follows causation: if one party's act causes the loss of another's property, he is liable to compensate. For example, if saweeq (a type of food) spills into ghee, the owner of the saweeq must compensate the owner of the ghee because his saweeq consumed the ghee (mixing made it inseparable). Conversely, if lime mixes with saweeq, the victim has options: either take his deficient saweeq and demand lime from the other, or make the other liable for the value of his saweeq. If mixing occurs without anyone's act (e.g., wind), the mixture is sold and proceeds divided by value. The passage also covers cases of pouring inferior onto good, and vinegar-wine mixtures. In the later case, Imam Muhammad rules the vinegar is shared proportionally, which is the relied-upon position. The overall principle is that accidental loss without a liable party is borne proportionally, while deliberate or negligent acts trigger full liability.

Key Terms
سويق (sawīq): A type of roasted barley or wheat flour, often mixed with oil or dates.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage to another's property.
نورة (nūrah): A depilatory lime paste, not a standard market commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Khaniyya, if lime and flour mix without any human act, what is the ruling?
A) The owner of the lime must compensate the flour owner.
B) The mixture is sold and proceeds divided by value.
C) Each party takes his share by weight.
D) The flour owner gets the mixture because his flour was more valuable.

Key Takeaway
When no human act causes mixing, no liability arises; the mixture is sold and proceeds divided by value.

4 min
Rulings on Pouring Inferior onto Good and Food onto Food
Specific rulings for pouring inferior onto good and food onto food

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the specific rulings for pouring inferior substances onto good ones and for pouring food onto food, distinguishing between liability and partnership.

Core Idea
Pouring inferior onto good makes you liable for like the good and a partner; pouring food onto food makes you liable only if your amount is more.

The Hanafi school provides nuanced rules when one person pours his inferior goods into another's superior goods, or pours food onto food. These cases involve both liability and potential co-ownership, depending on quantity and quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action and substances
In the first case, a person pours inferior stuff onto good. In the second, a person pours food onto food.
Both are deliberate acts, not accidental spills.
2
Apply the rule for inferior onto good
The actor guarantees the good like for like (mithl) even if his quantity was small, and he becomes a partner in the mixture to the extent of what he poured.
Partnership is proportional to quantity.
3
Apply the rule for food onto food
If the actor's food is more than the other's, he is liable. If his food is less, he is not liable because he became a consumer (mustahlik).
Liability depends on quantity.

Source Text

According to Jami' al-Jawami', what happens when a man pours inferior stuff onto good stuff of the same type?
A) He only pays the difference in value.
B) He guarantees like the good and becomes a partner proportionally.
C) He is not liable because his amount was little.
D) He must take back his inferior stuff.

Key Takeaway
Pouring inferior goods onto good of the same type imposes liability for like good plus partnership; food onto food liability depends on quantity.

Wine-Vinegar Mixture and Dirhams Cases
Rulings on wine-vinegar mixture and dirhams mixing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling for accidental wine-vinegar mixtures and the ruling for dirhams mixing, and explain the basis for each.

Core Idea
When wine mixes with vinegar, the resulting vinegar is shared proportionally; accidentally mixing dirhams makes the owner of the fallen dirhams liable.

This lesson covers two special cases: the mixture of wine and vinegar (where the property changes chemically) and the accidental mixing of dirhams (fungible currency). The Hanafi school resolves these using analogy and the principle of consumption.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the wine-vinegar case
A man pours wine into another's vinegar. After mixing, the wine transforms into vinegar. The key question is ownership of the resulting vinegar.
Multiple opinions exist; the relied-upon view is sharing.
2
Examine the opinions
Imam Muhammad (via al-Khaniyya) gives the wine owner the option to take half the vinegar. Abu al-Qasim says the wine (now vinegar) belongs entirely to the usurper (by analogy). Abu al-Layth says it is shared proportionally, and this is the preferred (mufta bihi) view.
3
Understand the dirhams case
A man drops some of his dirhams into another's pile of dirhams. He is liable because his coins were consumed in the mixture.
Dirhams are mithli, so liability attaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what happens when wine and vinegar mix in equal amounts?
A) The wine owner takes all, because his wine transformed into vinegar.
B) The vinegar owner takes all, because vinegar is the base.
C) The mixture is shared between them.
D) The mixture is discarded.

Key Takeaway
Accidental mixing of wine and vinegar results in shared ownership of the resulting vinegar; accidental mixing of dirhams imposes liability for like dirhams.

intermediate
8 min
Ownership Transfer upon Paying Value of Lost Usurped Property
Hanafi ruling with proof and Shafi'i opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership transfer when a usurper pays the value of a lost object, explain the rational proof (istihsan), and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
When the usurper pays the value of the lost object, ownership transfers to him to prevent double recovery and achieve justice.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author presents a key Hanafi rule: if a usurped object is lost and the usurper pays its value, he becomes the owner. This ruling is based on a rational principle (istihsan) that avoids combining the original object and its substitute in the same ownership. The Shafi'i school objects, arguing that usurpation cannot be a cause for ownership. We will examine both proofs and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
State exactly what the Hanafi school rules: when the usurped object is lost and the usurper pays its value, he owns it.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and their proof
State the Shafi'i opposition: ownership does not transfer because usurpation is prohibited. Note their proof from Quran 4:29 and analogy to mudabbar.
3
Follow the Hanafi rational proof (istihsan)
Explain the Hanafi proof: the owner now owns the substitute (value), so to avoid double recovery, his ownership of the original must cease. The substitute is for the lost possession, not the object.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to objections
Show how the Hanafi rebuts the Shafi'i proofs: the verse is about the act of usurpation, not the consequence; mudabbar is not analogous because it is non-transferable by nature.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Explain the real-world consequence: under Hanafi, after payment the usurper is owner; under Shafi'i, the original owner retains ownership and can reclaim the object later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما فرغ من بيان كيفية ما يوجب الملك للغاصب بالضمان شرع في ذكر مسائل تتصل بمسائل الغصب. قال رحمه الله : (غيب المغصوب وضمن قيمته ملكه) وقال الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله : لا يملكه لأن الغصب محظور فلا يكون سبباً للملك كما في المدبر، وهذا لأن الملك مرغوب فيه فلا ينال بالمحرم لأنه منهي عنه لقوله تعالى ولا تأكلوا أموالكم بينكم بالباطل إلا أن تكون تجارة عن تراض منكم ﴾ [النساء : ۲۹] والغصب ليس فيه تراض. ولنا أن المالك ملك بدل المغصوب رقبة ويداً فوجب أن يزول ملكه عن المبدل إن كان يقبله دفعاً للضرر عن الغاصب وتحقيقاً للعدل حتى لا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك رجل واحد، ولأن الفائت بفعل الغاصب هو اليد دون الملك عما فات من اليد مع بقاء العين في ملكه لكان إجحافاً بالغاصب بإزالة ملكه وإثبات الملك فيه للمغصوب منه بمقابلة عين في ملكه مع إمكان تحقيق العدل بينهما وهذا خلف لأن هذا ضرورة القضاء بقيمة العين زوال ملكه عنها والجواب عن الآية أن الرضا قد وجد منه لما طلب القيمة، ولا يقال لو غصب مدبراً وغيبه لا يملكه لأنا نقول : المدبر لا يقبل النقل من ملك إلى آخر. ولم يتعرض المؤلف لما إذا غاب المغصوب بغير صنع من الغاصب بأن كان عبداً فأبق عنده فإنه إذا ضمن قيمته ملكه كما ذكر ، فلو قال «غاب» مكان «غيب» لكان أولى لأنه إذا ملكه فيما إذا غاب بغير صنعه علم الحكم فيما إذا كان بصنعه بطريق الأولى. ولم يتعرض لما إذا غاب المغصوب منه وترك العين روى ابن سماعة عن محمد : للقاضي أن يأخذ المال من الغاصب والسارق إذا كان المالك غائباً ويحفظ عليه فإن ضاع ثم خاصم صاحب المال فله أن يضمن الغاصب ولا يبرئ بأخذ القاضي اهـ. وفي الخانية : غاب المغصوب منه فطلب الغاصب من القاضي أن يأذن له بالإنفاق ليرجع بذلك على المالك لا يجيبه القاضي إلى ذلك والنفقة تكون على الغاصب، ولو قضى بالإنفاق على المغصوب منه لا يجب عليه منه شيء، وإن رأى القاضي أن يبيع العبد أو الدابة ويمسك الثمن فلو فعل ذلك صح ا هـ . جارية قيمتها ألف فغصبها منه آخر فأبقت الجارية يضمن الغاصب الثاني للغاصب الأول لأن للأول أخذها لو كانت قائمة ليتمكن من ردها إلى المالك فيبرأ عن الضمان، فإن أخذ القيمة فلا سبيل للمالك على الغاصب الثاني لأنه خرج عن عهدة الضمان برد القيمة لأن رد القيمة حال عجزه عن رد العين كرد ،العين فإن كانت القيمة قائمة عنده فللمالك أخذها لأنها نزلت منزلة العين، فإن كانت هالكة يلزمه الضمان لولي الجارية لأنه بمنزلة ما لو استرد الجارية وهلكت عنده لأنه لا يخرج عن عهدة الضمان ما لم يردها إلى المالك، وإن كانت قيمتها ألفاً عند الأول فقبضها الثاني وقيمتها ألفان فأبقت من يد الثاني وأخذ الأول من الثاني الفي درهم وهلكت من يد الأول لم يكن للمالك أن يضمن الأول ألفي درهم وإنما يضمنه قيمتها يوم الغصب ألف درهم لأن الألف الثانية أمانة في يده لأنها حدثت بعد الغصب الأول والزيادة الحادثة في يد الغاصب أمانة كالزيادة في عين المغصوب فإن ظهرت الجارية والقيمة في الأول فالمولى بالخيار إن شاء أخذ الجارية وإن شاء أخذ القيمة وإن شاء ضمن الأول قيمتها يوم غصبها منه . أراد بالتضمين أن يأخذ القيمة من الأول برضاه فيكون بمنزلة المبيع الجارية لما عادت من الإباق فقد قدر الأول على رد المغصوب والغاصب ما دام قادراً على رد
When he finished explaining the manner of what necessitates ownership for the usurper by guarantee, he began mentioning issues connected to the issues of usurpation. He (the author) said: (The usurped object was lost and he guaranteed its value, he owns it). Imam al-Shafi'i said: He does not own it because usurpation is prohibited so it cannot be a cause for ownership, just like in the case of a mudabbar. This is because ownership is desirable so it cannot be attained through the prohibited, because it is forbidden by His statement: 'Do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly unless it be trade by mutual consent' (4:29), and usurpation has no mutual consent. Our proof is that the owner owns the substitute of the usurped object both in essence (raqaba) and possession (yad), so it is necessary that his ownership of the object being substituted ceases if he accepts it, to remove harm from the usurper and to achieve justice so that substitute and the object are not combined in the ownership of one person. Also, what is lost by the act of the usurper is possession (yad), not ownership, since his ownership remains in the object, so the substitute is not for the object. Therefore we said: If one broke another's heart (a type of vessel?) and the judge ruled against him to pay its value and he took the heart, then they separated before receipt, the ruling is not invalidated. If it were a substitute for the object, it would be invalid as currency exchange (sarf). And do not say: If it were a substitute for what is lost of possession while the object remains in his ownership, it would be unjust to the usurper by removing his ownership and establishing ownership for the usurped-from in exchange for an object in his ownership while it is possible to achieve justice between them. This is a false deduction because this is a necessity of the ruling for the value of the object, that his ownership of it ceases. The answer to the verse is that consent has been found from him when he demanded the value. And do not say: If he usurped a mudabbar and lost it, he does not own it, for we say: The mudabbar does not accept transfer from one ownership to another. The author did not address the case when the usurped object is lost without the act of the usurper, like a slave who runs away from him, for then if he guarantees its value, he owns it as mentioned. So if he had said 'it was lost' instead of 'he lost it', it would have been better because if he owns it when it is lost without his act, the ruling when it is by his act is known by primary reasoning. And he did not address the case when the usurped-from is absent and leaves the object. Ibn Sama'a narrated from Muhammad: The judge may take the property from the usurper and the thief if the owner is absent and preserve it for him; if it is lost then the owner may hold the usurper liable and he is not absolved by the judge's taking. In al-Khaniyya: The usurped-from is absent, so the usurper asks the judge to permit him to spend (on the property) to reclaim it from the owner; the judge does not grant this, and the expense is on the usurper. And if the judge rules that the expense is on the usurped-from, nothing is due from him. And if the judge sees to sell the slave or the animal and keep the price, if he does so it is valid. (Then:) A slave girl worth one thousand is usurped by another person, then the slave girl runs away; the second usurper guarantees the first usurper because the first could have taken her if she were present to return her to the owner and be discharged from liability. If he takes the value, then the owner has no recourse against the second usurper because he has exited the liability by returning the value because returning the value when unable to return the object is like returning the object. If the value is still in hand, the owner may take it because it stands in place of the object. If it is destroyed, he owes liability to the guardian of the slave girl (i.e., the owner) because it is like if he retrieved the slave girl and she perished in his hand; he does not exit from liability until he returns her to the owner. And if its value was one thousand to the first, and the second took it and its value is two thousand, and she runs away from the second, and the first took two thousand dirhams from the second, and then the two thousand perished in the first's hand, then the owner may not hold the first liable for two thousand dirhams; he only holds him liable for its value on the day of usurpation, one thousand dirhams, because the second thousand is a trust (amana) in his hand because it arose after the first usurpation, and the increase occurring in the hand of the usurper is a trust like the increase in the usurped object. If the slave girl appears and the value is with the first, the master has the choice: if he wishes, he takes the slave girl; if he wishes, he takes the value; if he wishes, he holds the first liable for its value on the day he usurped it from him. By 'holding liable' he means he takes the value from the first with his consent, which becomes like the sale of the slave girl because she has returned from running away; so the first is able to return the usurped object, and the usurper, as long as he is able to return...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn 'ghayyaba al-maghsub wa damana qimatahu malakahu' (If the usurped object is lost and the usurper pays its value, he owns it). The author first states the Hanafi ruling, then presents the Shafi'i objection: usurpation is forbidden, so it cannot be a valid cause for ownership, analogous to a mudabbar (adult slave whose emancipation is tied to master's death) which cannot be transferred. The Hanafi proof is rational (istihsan): the original owner now owns the substitute (value) in both ownership and possession, so to avoid double recovery, his ownership of the original object must cease. The substitute is not for the object itself but for the lost right of possession (yad), since ownership of the object remains with the usurper. This also avoids injustice to the usurper who paid value but does not own the object. The author then addresses objections: if the object is a mudabbar, it cannot be owned because it is not freely transferable. He also notes that the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) used 'ghayyaba' (he lost it), but the ruling applies even if the object is lost without the usurper's act (e.g., the slave runs away). Additional masail are provided: if the usurped-from is absent, the judge may take the property; expenses are on the usurper. The passage also discusses a scenario of multiple usurpers and the liability chain. The overall principle is that payment of value transfers ownership to prevent unjust enrichment and multiplicity of claims.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghaṣb): Usurpation: taking another's property without right, with force or dominance.
القيمة (al-qīmah): The monetary value of the usurped object at the time of loss.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is tied to the master's death, not freely transferable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Hanafi proof for ownership transfer upon payment of value of a lost usurped object?
A) A Quranic verse about mutual consent
B) Rational necessity to avoid combining substitute and object in one ownership
C) Analogy to mudabbar
D) Consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, paying the value of lost usurped property transfers ownership to the usurper as a remedial measure to achieve justice and prevent double recovery.

Owner's Option Upon Reappearance of Usurped Property After Compensation
Ruling on owner's option after compensation and reappearance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the owner's two options when usurped property reappears after compensation has been paid, and explain why the owner cannot demand a higher value without the usurper's consent.

Core Idea
When usurped property reappears after compensation, the owner has a choice: take back the property or keep the value, but cannot demand a higher value without consent.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from al-Muhit regarding a scenario where a usurped slave-girl reappears after the second usurper has already compensated the owner. The property's value may have increased, but the owner does not automatically receive the higher amount. Instead, an option (khiyar) is granted, balancing the rights of the owner, the first usurper, and the second usurper. Understanding this choice is essential for correctly applying the law of ghasb and daman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The passage involves two usurpers: the first takes from the owner, the second from the first. The owner claims from the second, who pays compensation based on the owner's statement, evidence, or refusal to take an oath. Later, the original property reappears with increased value.
2
Understand the Owner's Options
The owner may either take back the property and refund the compensation paid, or affirm the sale (i.e., keep the compensation). The sale is suspended pending the owner's approval.
3
Note the Limitation on Demanding Higher Value
The owner cannot demand the higher value of the reappeared property unless the first usurper consents. The compensation already paid is treated as a sale price, and the owner's acceptance of that payment transfers ownership to the second usurper.
4
Trace the Legal Consequence for the Second Usurper
If the owner takes back the property, the second usurper has recourse against the first for the value paid, since that value was a substitute that did not remain with the second.
This follows the principle that the second usurper legally owned the property by paying the value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمالك وإن ظهر وقيمته أكثر وقد ضمنه بقول المالك أو ببينة أو بنكول الغاصب فهو المغصوب ليس للمالك أن يضمنه قيمته إلا برضاه والغاصب الأول لما ضمن الثاني القيمة فقد ملك الجارية منه حكماً فصار كما لو غصب الجارية من الثاني بغير أمر المولى فيتوقف البيع على إجازته إن شاء رده وأخذ الجارية، وإن شاء أجازه وأخذ بدلها، فإذا أخذ المولى الجارية رجع الثاني على الأول بالقيمة لأنه بدل لم يسلم له؛ كذا في المحيط .
For the owner, if the property appears and its value is more, and he had guaranteed it by the statement of the owner or by evidence or by the usurper's refusal to take an oath, then the usurped property is not for the owner to guarantee its value except with his consent. The first usurper, when he guaranteed the value to the second, he legally owned the slave-girl from him, so it becomes as if he had usurped the slave-girl from the second without the permission of the master, so the sale is suspended pending his permission: if he wishes, he returns it and takes the slave-girl; if he wishes, he permits it and takes its substitute. When the master takes the slave-girl, the second has recourse against the first for the value because it is a substitute that did not remain in his possession; thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit, cited by the author, presents a ruling on the owner's option when the usurped property (here a slave-girl) reappears after the usurper has already compensated the owner with its value. The scenario involves two usurpers: the first usurps from the owner, then the second usurps from the first. When the owner claims from the second, the second pays the value based on the owner's statement, evidence, or the second's refusal to take an oath. Then the original property appears with increased value. The ruling states that the owner cannot demand the higher value unless the first usurper agrees. Instead, the owner has an option: either annul the transaction, take back his slave-girl, and refund the value paid, or affirm the sale and keep the value. If the owner chooses to take back the slave-girl, the second usurper can recover the value he paid from the first usurper. This follows the principle that when the second usurper pays the value, he legally owns the slave-girl from the first, so the first's subsequent sale to the second is suspended pending the owner's approval.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Unlawful taking of another's property without permission, rendering the taker liable for compensation.
الضمان (al-daman): Guarantee or compensation; the financial liability to pay the value of the usurped property.
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option or choice given to the owner to either take back the property and refund the compensation, or affirm the sale and keep the compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a usurped slave-girl reappears with increased value after the second usurper has already paid compensation to the owner based on the owner's statement, what options does the owner have?
A) He can only take the slave-girl without refunding the compensation.
B) He can either take back the slave-girl and refund the value, or affirm the sale and keep the value.
C) He can demand the higher value from the usurper.
D) He must accept the value already paid and cannot take the slave-girl.

Key Takeaway
When usurped property reappears after compensation, the owner may either take the property back (refunding the value) or keep the compensation, but cannot demand a higher value without the usurper's consent.

Owner's Options When Property Appears After Guarantee by Oath or Statement
Explains owner's option when property appears after guarantee by usurper's oath, and related rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two scenarios of guarantee (by owner's admission vs. usurper's oath) and apply the preferred Hanafi ruling on the owner's option to reclaim the property.

Core Idea
When compensation is paid under oath without full consent, the owner retains the right to reclaim the property.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the owner's options when a usurper has already paid compensation for the usurped property. The ruling hinges on how the compensation was determined—whether by the owner's own admission or by the usurper's oath. Understanding this nuance is key to correctly applying Hanafi principles of consent and obligation in cases of guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Compensation Scenarios
First, the usurper guarantees based on the owner's statement, evidence, or the owner's refusal to take an oath (nukul). Second, the usurper guarantees based on his own oath.
The first scenario implies the owner's satisfaction; the second involves procedural compulsion.
2
Determine the Matn's Ruling for Each
In the first scenario, the matn states the usurper becomes owner of the property and the owner has no option. In the second scenario, the matn gives the owner an option to either keep the guarantee or take back the property after refunding the value.
The phrase 'wa qimatuhu akthar' (its value is more) is a condition in the first scenario, not the second.
3
Analyze the Reasoning
In the first scenario, the owner's consent is implied by his own statement or failure to prove. In the second, the owner accepts less value only due to lack of proof, not full satisfaction.
The author distinguishes between genuine satisfaction and procedural necessity.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Zahir al-Riwayah holds no option in the oath scenario, but the preferred view (al-ashah) establishes an option because the owner's true consent was absent.
The author sides with the option, noting the owner may not have his property except by a price he willingly chooses.
5
Understand Practical Consequences
If the owner chooses to reclaim the property, the usurper can detain it until the refund is paid. This is because the value was given in exchange for the property.
This differs from the case of a mudabbar (promised freedom), where the value is not directly exchangeable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن ظهر وقيمته أكثر وقد ضمنه بقول المالك أو ببينة أو بنكول الغاصب فهو للغاصب ولا ملکه خيار للمالك) لأنه رضي به وتم برضاه حيث سلم له ما ادعاه ولم يتعرض كثير لقدر الزيادة. وفي المجتبى : لو ظهر وقد زادت قيمته دانقاً فللمالك ما ذكر من الأحكام. وقوله وقيمته أكثر قيد في هذه المسألة لا في التي بعدها كما سيأتي . قال رحمه الله : (وإن ضمنه بيمين الغاصب) فالمالك يمضي الضمان أو يأخذ المغصوب ويرد العوض لعدم تمام رضاه بهذا الضمان. وإنما أخذ دون القيمة لعدم الحجة لا للرضا به ولو ظهر المغصوب وقيمته من قال مثل ما ضمنه به أو أقل من هذه الصورة وهي ما إذا ضمنه بقول الغاصب مع يمينه . رحمه الله : لا خيار له لأنه توفر عليه مالية ملكه بكماله وفي ظاهر الرواية يثبت له الخيار وهو الأصح لأن ثبوت الخيار لفوات الرضا وقد فات هنا حيث لم يحصل له ما يدعيه ، وله أن لا ماله إلا بثمن يختاره ويرضى به وكان له الخيار ثم إذا اختار المالك أخذ العين فللغاصب أن يحبس العين حتى يأخذ القيمة التي دفعها إليه لأنها مقابلة بالعين بخلاف المدبر لأنه غير مقابل به بل بما فات من البدل على ما بينا
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'If it appears and its value is more, and he had guaranteed it by the statement of the owner, or by evidence, or by the usurper's refusal to take an oath, then it belongs to the usurper and there is no option for the owner.' Because he was satisfied with it and it was completed by his satisfaction, as he conceded what he claimed, and he did not concern himself much with the amount of the increase. In al-Mujtaba: If it appears and its value has increased by a daniq, then the owner has the rulings mentioned. His statement 'and its value is more' is a condition in this issue, not in the one after it, as will come. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'If he guaranteed it by the oath of the usurper,' then the owner executes the guarantee or takes the usurped property and returns the compensation due to the lack of complete satisfaction with this guarantee. He takes less than the value not due to satisfaction but due to lack of proof. If the usurped property appears and its value is, he said, equal to what he guaranteed with or less, this is the case when he guaranteed by the statement of the usurper with his oath. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'There is no option for him because the full financial value of his property has come to him.' In the apparent transmission, the option is established for him, and it is the most correct because the establishment of the option is due to the loss of satisfaction, and it has been lost here since he did not obtain what he claimed. He may not have his property except by a price he chooses and is satisfied with, so he has the option. Then if the owner chooses to take the thing, the usurper may detain the thing until he takes the value he paid because it is in exchange for the thing, unlike the mudabbar because it is not exchanged for it but rather for what was lost of the substitute, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage discusses two related scenarios from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. First: if the usurped property appears with increased value and the usurper had already guaranteed (paid compensation) based on the owner's statement, evidence, or his own refusal to take an oath (nukul), then the usurper becomes owner and the owner has no option to contest. The reason is that the owner consented by his statement or by failing to prove his claim. However, the author notes from al-Mujtaba that even a minimal increase (a daniq) triggers the same rulings. The second scenario: if the guarantee was based on the usurper's oath (i.e., the owner could not prove his claim and the usurper swore to a lower value), then the owner still has an option: either accept the guarantee (i.e., keep the value paid) or take back the property and return the compensation. The commentary clarifies that the owner does not truly consent; he accepts the lower value only for lack of proof. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: in Zahir al-Riwayah, the owner has no option because the full value has been paid, but the preferred view ('the most correct') is that the owner has an option because his true consent was absent—he didn't get what he claimed (the higher value). If the owner chooses the property, the usurper can detain it until the owner refunds the value he paid, because the value is in exchange for the property. This is distinguished from the case of a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon master's death), where the value is not directly exchangeable.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): Refusal of the defendant to take an oath, which results in the plaintiff's claim being accepted.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The transmitted rulings in the six major works of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered the primary authority.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (al-ashah), what option does the owner have when the usurper guarantees by his own oath and the property later appears?
A) The owner must accept the guarantee and cannot reclaim the property.
B) The owner may either accept the guarantee or reclaim the property after refunding the value.
C) The owner can demand an additional payment from the usurper.
D) The owner must reclaim the property and cannot keep the payment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a usurper pays compensation based on his own oath, the owner retains the right to choose between keeping the payment or reclaiming the property after refund, because true consent was absent.

intermediate
12 min
Increments of Usurped Property: Trust vs. Guarantee
Ruling with proof and expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on increments of usurped property and explain when they become guaranteed, using the distinction between amanah and daman.

Core Idea
Increments of usurped property are a trust (amanah), not automatically guaranteed—only upon refusal to return (ta'addi).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands the matn ruling that increments of usurped property (e.g., offspring, fruit) are held as a trust by the usurper. The key legal question: do they follow the principal in liability? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the concept of 'hand' (yad) and transgression (ta'addi). We will unpack the proof, the Shafi'i objection, and several branch rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that increments are amanah (trust), not automatically guaranteed. They become guaranteed only upon ta'addi (transgression), such as refusal to return after the owner's demand.
The matn says: 'And increments of the usurped property are a trust, so they are guaranteed by transgression.'
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Understand the logic: Usurpation is removal of the owner's hand and establishment of the usurper's hand. The increment was never in the owner's hand, so the usurper's hand is not established on it from the start. Hence, it is not guaranteed until the usurper commits ta'addi.
This is a dalil (proof) based on the definition of usurpation.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that increments are always guaranteed because they follow the principal in liability. The Hanafi response: the analogy fails because the increment was never in the owner's possession.
This is a clear inter-madhhab ikhtilaf.
4
Examine the special gazelle case
The offspring of a gazelle taken from the Haram is guaranteed even without refusal, but the reasoning is not standard usurpation. It is a penalty for violating the sanctity of the Haram, as explained by most mashayikh.
This exception supports the rule: the liability here is not based on yad but on removal from safety.
5
Apply to furu' branching rulings
Work through the additional cases from later texts: sale of increments by the usurper, surety for the usurper, and an absconding slave. Each illustrates the amanah/daman distinction.
These furu' test your ability to apply the principle consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وزوائد المغصوب أمانة فتضمن بالتعدي) أي بالمنع بعد طلب المالك. وقال الشافعي: هي مضمونة على الغاصب، ولا فرق بين أن تكون الزيادة متصلة أو منفصلة أو كانت بالعسر ولنا أن الغصب إزالة يد المالك عنه وإثبات يد الغاصب ولا يتحقق ذلك في الزيادة لأنها لم تكن في يد المالك فلا تضمن إلا بالتعدي أو بالمنع عند طلبه لأنه يصير متعدياً به. وإنما ضمن ولد الظبية التي أخرجها من الحرم فولدت لوجود المنع من الرد لأن الرد واجب عليه إلى الحرم لحق الشرع حتى لو ردها وهلكت قبل من الرد لا يضمن لعدم المنع ؛ على هذا أكثر مشايخنا ولو قلنا بوجوب الضمان مطلقاً تمكن من الرد أو لم يتمكن فهو ضمان إتلاف لأن الصيد كان في الحرم أميناً ببعده عن أيدي الناس وقد فوت الأمن بإثبات اليد عليه فتحققت الجناية عليه لذلك، ولهذا لو أخرج جماعة محرمون صيداً واحداً من الحرم يجب على كل واحد منهم جزاء كامل ولو كان ضمان الغصب لوجب عليهم قيمة واحدة وفي العناية واعترض على الدليل بأنه يقتضي أن يضمن الولد إذا غصب الجارية كاملاً لأن اليد كانت ثابتة عليه وليس كذلك فإنه لا فرق بينه وبين ما إذا غصبها غير حامل فحبلت في يده فولدت والرواية في الأسرار. وأجيب بأن الحبل قبل الانفصال ليس بمال بل يعد عيباً في الأمة فلم يصدق عليه إثبات اليد على مال الغير سلمنا ذلك لكن لإزالة ظاهراً. وفي الكافي : ولو باع الغاصب الأصل والزوائد وسلم والزيادة متصلة، فإن كان قائماً أخذه صاحبه وإن كان هالكاً فهو بالخيار إن شاء ضمن الغاصب قيمته الغصب، وإن شاء ضمن المشتري قيمته يوم القبض وليس له أن يضمن البائع. وفي يوم العناية : لو كفل إنسان عن الغاصب بغير أمره وأدى الضمان فالعبد له. وفي الينابيع : ولو أبق العبد من الغاصب فالجعل على المولى عند أبي يوسف ولا يرجع به الغاصب. وقال محمد : يرجع على الغاصب ا هـ .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And increments of the usurped property are a trust, so they are guaranteed by transgression), i.e., by refusal after the owner's demand. Al-Shafi'i said: They are guaranteed on the usurper, and there is no difference between whether the increment is connected or disconnected or was by force. Our proof: usurpation is the removal of the owner's hand and establishment of the usurper's hand, and this does not occur with respect to the increment because it was not in the owner's hand, so it is not guaranteed except by transgression or refusal upon demand, because he becomes a transgressor thereby. The offspring of the gazelle that he took out of the sanctuary and then it gave birth is only guaranteed because of the prohibition of return, because return to the sanctuary is obligatory upon him as a right of the Law. Even if he returned it and it perished before return, he would not be liable due to the absence of prohibition. This is the view of most of our scholars. If we say that liability is absolute whether he was able to return or not, it is a liability of destruction, because the game in the sanctuary was safe due to its distance from people, and he removed that safety by taking control, so the aggression is realized for that reason. For this reason, if a group of people in ihram remove one game from the sanctuary, each of them must pay a full penalty, whereas if it were a liability of usurpation, they would owe a single value. In al-‘Inayah, an objection is made to the proof: it would imply that the child should be guaranteed fully if the female slave is usurped, because the hand was established on it, but that is not the case, for there is no difference between that and if he usurped her while not pregnant and then she conceived in his hand and gave birth. The narration is in al-Asrar. The answer is that the pregnancy before separation is not property but is considered a defect in the slave-girl, so establishment of hand on another's property is not applicable. We accept that, but it is [only] apparent removal. In al-Kafi: If the usurper sold the original and the increments, and delivered, and the increment is connected, then if it remains, the owner takes it; if it is perished, he has the option to either hold the usurper liable for its value as usurpation, or hold the buyer liable for its value on the day of receipt, and he cannot hold the seller liable. In ‘Umdat al-‘Inayah: If a person stands surety for the usurper without his permission and pays the guarantee, the slave belongs to him. In al-Yanabi‘: If the slave runs away from the usurper, the reward is upon the master according to Abu Yusuf, and the usurper cannot recover it. Muhammad said: He recovers it from the usurper.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn ruling that the increments (zawa'id) of usurped property are considered an amanah (trust) in the hands of the usurper, not guaranteed by the mere fact of usurpation. They only become guaranteed if the usurper commits ta'addi (transgression), such as refusing to return them after the owner's demand. The commentator contrasts the Shafi'i view that all increments are guaranteed. The Hanafi proof is that the increment was never in the owner's hand, so the usurper's hand is not established on it from the start. The special case of the gazelle taken from the Haram is explained: the liability there is not standard usurpation but a penalty for violating the sanctity of the Haram. The passage also includes several furu' (branch rulings) from later texts: sale of increments, surety for the usurper, and absconding slaves.

Key Terms
أمانة (amanah): A trust; property held without liability for loss unless the holder is negligent or transgresses.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression; here, refusing to return the increment after the owner's demand renders the usurper liable.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; wrongful taking of another's property without consent.
مضمون (makhmun / madmun): Guaranteed; property for which the holder is liable for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, under what condition does the usurper become liable for the increments of the usurped property?
A) Immediately upon usurpation, like the principal.
B) Only if he uses the increment.
C) Only if he refuses to return it after the owner's demand (ta'addi).
D) Only if the increment is separated from the principal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, increments of usurped property are a trust until the usurper commits transgression by refusing to return them.
